Wall Street Deploys Troops to Battle Y2K---Nervous InvestorsHoard Cash, GoldAs Chaos Hedges
Your Money Matters
Wall Street Deploys Troops to Battle Y2K --- Nervous Investors Hoard Cash, Gold As Chaos Hedges
By Ruth Simon
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,478 words
22 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Many people are preparing for the year 2000 by stocking up on champagne and caviar. Joseph S.
Dombrowski has loaded up on gold coins.
A year ago, Mr. Dombrowski was fully invested in stock mutual funds. But during a two-month period,
beginning in December 1998, the Air Force officer moved 90% of his assets into American Eagles, Canadian
Maple Leafs and Vienna Philharmonics.
"If there are significant disruptions . . . I want a little safe harbor," he explains. If Y2K proves to be a
"nonevent," Mr. Dombrowski says, he plans to shift 99% of his assets back into funds early next year.
In case you've been paying less attention than Mr. Dombrowski, the Y2K problem is that older computers
programmed using two digits to represent the years could malfunction or even shut down entirely when Jan. 1
rolls around.
Banks, brokerage firms and other financial institutions are well-prepared, government officials and other
experts insist. Although some glitches are likely, they say Y2K is likely to cause far fewer problems than
initially feared.
"All the data confirm our belief that major U.S. companies will be ready before the turn of the century," says
Lorraine Wang, associate U.S. investment strategist for Morgan Stanley Dean Witter & Co.
Some people, though, are preparing for the worst. Konstantin Kupfer, a jeweler in Burlingame, Calif., who
specializes in fine jewelry and high-grade watches, says he has sold "more than a couple hundred thousand
dollars" of gold coins to people with Y2K fears. "In the last eight or nine months, people have just come in
and asked for coins," he says. "Everyone said the same thing: When the crash happens, they will have to use
coins for survival."
Terence Hanlon, president of the metals division of Dillon Gage Inc. of Dallas, a full-service brokerage firm,
says that sales of gold coins to dealers are running 60% to 70% above normal holiday levels. "There are
[coin] dealers we supply who really created businesses geared strictly to Y2K," he says.
Stories of people who have stocked up on cash are also plentiful, although these people don't want to be
identified -- for obvious reasons. Terry Murray, chairman and chief executive of Boston-based FleetBoston
Financial Corp., recounts sitting next to a woman at a dinner in Newport, R.I., who said she was going to take
out $11,000 in cash and put it in her home in Vermont.
One well-to-do New Orleans businessman took out a few thousand dollars every time he deposited a
paycheck during the past year. His stash of cash, now well into the five figures, sits in a safe in his home.
Even fellow family members think he is overreacting, and he is vague as to what catastrophe he expects to
befall him.
To allay fears, the Federal Reserve is keeping more than $200 billion in cash on hand-something like an extra
$2,000 for every household in the United States. That doesn't include an additional $50 billion sitting in
commercial bank vaults, so there should be plenty to go around. Some banks plan to open Jan. 1 to ease
Y2K angst.
The Fed says currency in circulation was up 14% in November, about double the increase the Fed would
normally expect. "It's hard to say it's all Y2K," says Federal Reserve Board spokeswoman Rose Pinalto. "We
keep getting indications from retailers that there is a lot of spending."

--- Page 32 ---
Page 32 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ms. Pinalto notes that most Americans don't seem particularly worried. Only 5% of those polled by the Gallup
Organization say they are "very concerned" about Y2K. That is down from 11% in March, according to the
Gallup survey, which was released in November by the Federal Reserve Board and the Federal Deposit
Insurance Corp. Sixty-four percent say they are "not too concerned" or "not at all concerned," the poll found.
But Mr. Dombrowski with his gold coins isn't most investors. "It's kind of an unpopular position to take," he
says, but "I'm glad I'm here."
And he certainly isn't alone in acting on his concerns. Over the past two months, Peter Hitch has sold most of
the stocks held by four family trusts he manages. "The gain we could make over a 30-day period, from Dec.
15 to Jan. 15, is minimal compared to the risk of leaving it there," he says.
Mr. Hitch, who handles acquisitions for pickle manufacturer M.A. Gedney Co., Chaska, Minn., is reducing the
trusts' stock-market exposure to 10% of total assets. To minimize the tax hit, Mr. Hitch is holding on to stocks
that have a low cost basis. "The sale of that stock would be a huge tax implication for the trust," he explains.
Or consider Larry Kunkel, a manufacturer's representative in the Midwest who sells printing and
packaging-related items. At the beginning of this year, he told his five brokers that at some point in 1999 he
would issue an order to shift entirely to cash or U.S. Treasurys.
He isn't as worried anymore, in part because he's been listening to Michael Chasnoff, a Cincinnati financial
adviser who works with him. "This is a short-term phenomenon," says Mr. Chasnoff. "We prefer to ride it out."
Still, Mr. Kunkel is hedging his bets. He began selling the stocks in tax-deferred pension, profit-sharing and
annuity accounts last month. "By the end of the year, I expect to be about 80% in cash" in these accounts,
Mr. Kunkel says.
"I'm giving up 60 days of potential gain," he adds. "I may lose out on that end, but if we do have a major
correction, I have cash available for a buying opportunity."
Lawrence Astrove, a semi-retired former golf-course owner, started a lot earlier. In October of last year, Mr.
Astrove, who lives near Portland, Ore., told Marilyn Bergen, the financial adviser who manages his
mutual-fund holdings, to shift his weighting in stock funds to 20% from 80% of total assets. "We've lost a
couple thousand points," says Mr. Astrove, who continued to invest in individual stocks on his own. "In
retrospect, we shouldn't have done a thing with Marilyn's portion."
Ms. Wang of Morgan Stanley Dean Witter says that her firm doesn't "see Y2K causing a bear market. If there
are isolated disruptions, it could have some impact on the stock market for a couple of days or so," she adds.
"But because this is a temporary phenomenon, investors will look beyond that."
Edward Yardeni, chief economist for Deutsche Banc Alex. Brown, a unit of Deutsche Bank AG, says that "for
most investors, the choice is to sell stocks now and share half the gains with the federal government or look
through the Y2K problem."
Mr. Yardeni, a noted Y2K bear, says he is no longer a Y2K "alarmist," though he still expects "a fairly intense,
but short recession." He says, "If investors don't think they can comfortably ride out the storm, the most
reasonable thing to do . . . is to talk to your broker and consider defensive strategies including buying puts to
protect your gains." A put is a contract that gives the holder the right, for a small fee, to sell shares at a preset
price up to a predetermined date.
But the best move people can make in anticipation of Y2K is to put their finances in order. From now through
the first two months of next year, they should save all brokerage-firm, bank and mutual-fund records and
check them against transaction records. They should also review mortgage and loan statements and
creditcard, utility and property tax bills.
If you do decide to load up on cash, be careful. Gale Hayman, the founder of Georgio Beverly Hills and Gale
Hayman Beverly Hills Cosmetics Inc., learned the risks of hoarding cash the hard way. In November, Ms.
Hayman told police that $15,000 that she had stashed away in preparation for Y2K had been stolen from an
unlocked safe in her apartment at New York's Pierre Hotel. Ms. Hayman, who had tucked about $5,000 in
each of three envelopes, discovered the theft when she went to put a fourth envelope in the safe.
"I wanted to prepare just in case there were any delays . . . for the same reason I didn't want to leave the
country and fly," says Ms. Hayman, who declined to discuss the details of the robbery. "Being of conservative
nature, I thought it was a good idea."

The Fed Should Tighten -- but for the Right Reasons
The Fed Should Tighten -- but for the Right Reasons
By Wayne Angell
1,272 words
21 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A22
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The Federal Open Market Committee has been doing the right thing for the wrong reasons. I have no
sympathy for the growth-limiting objectives inherent in Fed explanations of its actions over the past seven
months. Yet the FOMC has been correct in increasing the federal-funds rate to 5.5% from 4.75%. When it
meets today, the FOMC should announce a tightening bias consistent with a move to 5.75% during the first
quarter of 2000.
The FOMC has it all wrong in basing its actions on the notion that rapid economic growth along with low
unemployment causes inflation. A faster growth of output in a monetary environment determined by a
reluctant FOMC means more goods are being chased by the same amount of money. That is, money is made
scarce by an increase in output of goods and services, which is consistent with a rise in the demand for
money. Hence, the inflation rate falls.
Likewise, a drop in unemployment is apt to accelerate the growth rate of worker pay but not the rate of
inflation. If the FOMC does its job properly, then executives are acutely aware of their lack of pricing power.
If their primary focus is to maximize shareholder value, then the growth rate of labor compensation is limited
to the growth rate of labor productivity.
The problem with the FOMC's demand-management thinking is that monetary restraint to limit the growth of
demand will also limit the growth of the supply of goods and services. The Fed cannot manage to increase
the unemployment rate for workers without also increasing the "unemployment rate" for capital.
The U.S. advantage over most of the rest of the world is that as our unemployment rate falls, the social and
political cost of more blatant substitution of capital for labor nearly disappears. Rising wages become the
friend of lower inflation as the stock of capital goods rises faster than the growth rate of labor. Nonresidential
capital goods production, buildings and equipment, has risen to 14% of GDP from 9% in 1990.
As the ratio of capital inputs to labor inputs rises, labor productivity increases. Technological innovation spurs
more capital-goods production, which spurs further technological innovation. Thus it's foolish for the FOMC to
attempt to limit the growth rate of a productivity-led surge in output. An increase in labor productivity is sure
both to increase the real wage rate and to augment the wealth of the American people.
The FOMC's worries about low unemployment rates and rapid economic growth leads it to an even riskier
minefield -- worrying about the level of stock prices. The FOMC cannot succeed in halting the growth of stock
prices, just as it could not in the 1920s. The FOMC will never take the only action that would lower stock
prices: announce that it has gone soft on inflation. Instead, as the Fed applies its wrongheaded thinking on
raising rates to slow economic growth, market perception is of a vigilant Fed. And the market likes a vigilant
Fed. That's why announcing a tightening bias tomorrow isn't likely to dampen stock-market exuberance.
Trial-and-error attempts to slow economic growth and to dampen market enthusiasm will surely lower the rate
of inflation in 2000 and 2001. As long as gradual disinflation is evident, some good things happen: the
effective tax rate on capital gains falls; the cost-cutting zeal of business leaders is reinforced; the economic
expansion goes on and on. Stock prices respond by continuing to rise.
In the U.S. in 1929 and Japan in 1989, monetary conditions had gone quite beyond disinflation to deflation.
As the ad hoc Reserve Bank open-market committee raised rates to tame stock price appreciation, it
generated a 5% to 6% annual rate of decline in wholesale prices. Deflation can be powerful enough to ruin an
economy's productive potential. A stock-market crash while the economy is being ruined is another matter
altogether. A devastated economy with a crash in earnings and stock prices will always be dubbed a "bubble"
market after the fact.
Why, then, do I favor an increase in the federal-funds rate to 5.75% during the first quarter of next year? First,
the current inflation rate of 1.5% to 2% is not zero. Just as the FOMC enhanced growth by cutting inflation

--- Page 34 ---
Page 34 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
from its 3% to 4% range of 1993, the economy would derive further benefits by gradually cutting the inflation
rate in half again. I believe that FOMC actions count even when the FOMC fails to announce its intention. An
increase in the funds rate to 5.75% would likely lower inflation expectations, and the price of gold would
likely respond by moving back to a $250 to $280 range.
Second, Alan Greenspan is correct in suggesting that if the Fed is to err, it is preferable to err on the side of
restraint. The easiest task for a central bank is to create enough money to make money less valuable. The
FOMC was very effective -- maybe too effective -- in eliminating the commodity deflation that spilled over into
a real-estate deflation in 1985-86.
Third, the U.S. economy eventually may be plagued by the persistent undersaving rate. Capital investment
spending continues to grow more rapidly than national saving. As a result, real interest rates have been on a
persistent rise since February 1994. Objective measurement of real interest rate increases since the Treasury
started issuing inflation-indexed 10-year notes in January 1997 shows a real rate rise to 4.3% from 3.36%.
Given the current misguided income-tax system, real rates are likely to need to rise further, perhaps as high
as 5%.
If the FOMC can engineer a rise in real interest rates that is driven primarily by a decline in the inflation
rate, then all is well. On the other hand, if the energy-driven inflation rate hiccup in 1999 were not reversed,
then real interest rate increases would stem primarily from increases in nominal interest rates. That seems
to me a bad solution. Thereby, I favor a disinflationary step to 5.75% on the funds rate.
Fourth, I would prefer that this economic expansion continue. Surely we know that economic expansions end
when the FOMC makes an inflationary mistake, such as it did from October 1987 to March 1988. Pre-emptory
action by the FOMC is the best way to increase the length of the expansion. If the committee can get
inflation expectations to match the disinflationary track of the core consumer price index at 2%, rather than
permit slippage of expectations to 3%, the likely 12-month total increase to be shown by the total CPI at year
end, that would be a giant step.
Monetary policy has one priority that always produces good results: to target the price level properly. The
most practical forward indicators of price-level changes can still be found in commodity prices, including the
price of gold. While current commodity price indicators suggest the Fed is on a proper course of monetary
restraint consistent with a stable rate of inflation, I would prefer that the FOMC effectively reinforce the
gradual disinflationary track shown by the core CPI. My advice is to announce a tightening bias today to be
followed by a probable rate increase to 5.75% on Feb. 2, provided that commodity indicators need one more
nudge to ensure a disinflationary course.
---
Mr. Angell, a former governor of the Federal Reserve, is chief economist at Bear Stearns.

Page 35 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Nasdaq's Gains Mask a House Divided;Stocks Show Equal Split of Rich and Poor
Abreast of the Market
Nasdaq's Gains Mask a House Divided; Stocks Show Equal Split of Rich and Poor
By E.S. Browning
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,288 words
20 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
This is a tale of two stock markets -- two very different markets that both have the same name.
One, the happy one, is loaded with soaring technology stocks, some of them up 1,000% or more in 1999. The
other market, full of sad drug companies, manufacturers and financial firms, doesn't have a single gainer this
year. Its best stocks have gone nowhere; its worst are down 90%, having lost most of their worth.
The two markets, each containing almost the same number of stocks, are the two halves of the Nasdaq Stock
Market.
Taken as a whole, the Nasdaq Composite Index is up an astounding 71% since the year began, by far the
biggest gain in Nasdaq history, or, for that matter, in the history of just about any of the nation's stock
indexes.
But Nasdaq divides almost evenly into two distinct places, according to data provided by Birinyi Associates,
Westport, Conn. Half the market is up. In a year when many analysts bemoan the "narrowness" of the
market, a much wider group of stocks has soared through the roof this year than many people realize. A total
of 997 stocks, more than one Nasdaq stock in five, have at least doubled in price this year. Such huge, broad
gains are unheard of. Of the stocks that are up on Nasdaq, the average stock has more than doubled, gaining
151%.
And yet, despite such heady gains on one side of the market, nearly half of all Nasdaq's stocks have actually
fallen in price this year. Of the losers, the average decline is almost 32%.
"It really has been a case of the haves and the have-nots," says Sally Anderson, portfolio manager at Kopp
Investment Advisors, Edina, Minn.
Investors are selectively buying the stocks they think will hold up in the face of rising interest rates and
stiffening business competition. In many cases, that means tech stocks. Some of the winners are thought to
be so competitive they can post big-time earnings, or at least strong revenue growth, no matter what. But
many of the winners -- around 47 of the 100 biggest gainers on Nasdaq, in fact -- aren't just tech companies,
they are closely linked to the Internet. Many of them aren't expected to show any profits at all for the
foreseeable future. For that select bunch, investors don't mind. They believe, rightly or wrongly, these stocks
will be part of the growing revolution in information technology and communications, and they want to own a
piece of the revolution.
The stocks that aren't part of the revolution, that have failed to deliver on promises or that are having trouble
boosting prices and profits in the face of cutthroat competition? No one wants them, not even if they are
cheap. And they are getting hammered. Without the tech winners, the Nasdaq market shows little if any gain.
"There is a whole group of stocks that are just lying there and not doing anything," Ms. Anderson says. "And it
is my impression that the common denominator is earnings growth. Most of those that are down have shown
disappointing fundamentals. If they have any growth it is only modest growth."
Skeptics shake their heads with worry that the gains represent an enormous bubble, which will leave
devastation behind when it bursts. And investors are ignoring the skeptics.
The astounding gains continued on Friday. With a surge of 38 points, or 1.02%, the Nasdaq composite hit
3753.06, its 25th record close in the past 35 trading days. The Dow Jones Industrial Average, whose 23%
annual gain would look huge in any other year, flirted with a record but fell back. It rose 12.54 points, or
0.11%, to 11257.43, leaving it just 68.61 points short of a record. But that represented a gain of just 32.73
points, or 0.29%, for the week. The Nasdaq composite, by contrast, was up close to 4% last week.

--- Page 36 ---
Page 36 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But seldom has a strong market had so many losers. The biggest single characteristic separating the winners
from the losers: the technology label. Of the 2,440 Nasdaq gainers, 46%, almost half, are in the tech
business. Of the 2,254 Nasdaq losers, less than 16% are tech. Of course, some troubled, small software and
computer companies do show up among the big losers. But none is a well-known name. The 10 biggest
losers don't include a single tech company; they all are in areas such as health care, retailing and
manufacturing.
Among the 200 worst-performing stocks, 29, or 15%, are tech companies -- little surprise given the huge
presence of tech names in that stock market. But the bottom 200 performers contain more drug and
health-care companies (36) and also more manufacturing stocks (31), a clear reflection of where investors
have preferred to have their money.
The winners have done so well that some well-known stocks, whose big gains have made them media
heroes, look almost like also-rans. Network-equipment company Cisco Systems, which to many people
symbolizes the market's strength, has been outperformed by more than 900 Nasdaq stocks this year; it has
doubled in value, and yet is only 906 on the list of Nasdaq gainers. Microsoft, up more than 66% this year,
has been on fire, and yet it actually trails the 71% overall advance of the Nasdaq index. It trails the median
change for all tech stocks on Nasdaq, which is a gain of 74%. Microsoft is only No. 1,250 on the list of
gainers.
"What people have been saying to me is that it is really just seven stocks that are driving the market, and that
if you don't own Cisco and Oracle and Dell Computer, you are nowhere. But that clearly isn't the case," says
Laszlo Birinyi, of Birinyi Associates.
Who does lead the list? A billon-dollar company called Puma Technology, which makes
wireless-communications software, and which is up 2,350%. The best-known names at the top: Red Hat, the
hot Linux software maker, up 1,700%, and Qualcomm, the cell-phone technology company, up 1,650%. Only
one of the top 10, Modern Medical Modalities, in the magnetic-resonance-imaging business, still is tiny. All 10
are tech or tech-related.
Some analysts believe the Nasdaq rocket ship could peak out early in the year. With the winners up so much,
many investors hesitate to sell them and take profits in the current tax year, preferring to postpone
capital-gains taxes. But in January, those holding the stocks for tax reasons will be able to sell.
But many investors insist they won't be selling their big tech holdings. Donald Berdine, chief investment
officer of PNC Advisors in Pittsburgh, part of PNC Bank, says as much as 45% of his group's small-stock
portfolio is in tech stocks, and a large hunk of his big-cap portfolio is there too. The technology revolution, he
says, "will undoubtedly be bigger than we think."
Wells Fargo economist Sung Won Sohn points out, over the past five years, Commerce Department figures
show tech companies accounted for 6% of the nation's economic production capacity, but 35% of economic
growth. Little wonder they have been showing the profits, the revenue growth and the stock-market gains.
Can some of the losers finally rebound in the new year?
"A lot will depend on the overall growth we see in the economy," Ms. Anderson says. "Because if we don't
see growth coming back to some of these areas that haven't been doing well, I don't know what gets them off
their bottoms."
---
Friday Market Activity
Blue-chip stocks gave back most of their big intraday gains but managed to finish modestly higher.
New York Stock Exchange volume hit a record 1.35 billion shares on Friday, an unprecedented fourth
consecutive day with more than one billion shares trading. The activity was partly the product of "triple
witching" Friday, marking the quarterly expiration of stock options, index options and futures.
General Electric jumped 4 1/4 to 151 3/4, hitting a 52-week high of 154 3/4 intraday. The Fairfield, Conn.,
company said it is splitting its stock 3-for-1, raising its dividend 17%, and increasing its share repurchase
program to $22 billion from $17 billion.
J.P. Morgan fell 13/16 to 128 1/16. Concern earlier in the session was that the company wouldn't meet
fourth-quarter earnings expectations, but analysts said those worries were probably unfounded. American
Express gained 2 1/2 to 155 1/8, Citigroup tacked on 5/8 to 54 1/2, Morgan Stanley Dean Witter improved 3
to 128 and PaineWebber gained 1 to 37 3/16.

--- Page 37 ---
Page 37 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Tandy fell 13 5/8, or 21%, to 52 3/8. Radio Shack, a unit of the Fort Worth, Texas, consumer-electronics
company, said its same-store sales growth in the first half of December was slightly below the monthly goal of
an 8% to 10% increase. But Radio Shack also said 55% to 60% of December sales have historically occurred
in the second half of the month, and said the week after Christmas is important.
Adobe (Nasdaq) improved 3 3/4, or 5.9%, to 67 1/2. Late Thursday, the company posted fourth-quarter
operating results of 46 cents a share, topping the consensus view of 42 cents. Adobe is a San Jose, Calif.,
maker of Web publishing and document-viewing software.
Nike jumped 8 3/4, or 20%, to 52 7/16. The Beaverton, Ore., shoe and apparel company earned 38 cents a
share in its fiscal second quarter, compared with operating net of 28 cents a year ago and 4 cents ahead of
expectations.
Alza fell 4, or 10.6%, to 32 7/16. After the close on Thursday, Abbott Laboratories said it won't be able to
complete its planned $7.3 billion merger with Alza, as the pharmaceutical companies weren't able to come to
terms with the Federal Trade Commission to satisfy concerns relating to the merger. In primary trading
Friday, Abbott tacked on 1 1/8 to 37 7/16. In after-hours composite trading on Thursday, Alza fell 11 7/8 to 27
3/4 and Abbott improved 4 5/16 to 38 1/8.
Anglogold improved 1 3/16 to 25 5/16. The gold-mining company, based in South Africa, said it has gained
acceptance amounting to more than 58% of the share capital of Acacia, up from less than 25% about three
weeks ago. Anglogold is offering 3.5 of its shares for every 100 shares of Acacia, Australia's fifth-largest gold
miner.
Monsanto gained 1 3/8, or 3.4%, to 41 3/4. The Wall Street Journal reported Pharmacia & Upjohn is in
merger talks with the St. Louis company. Shares of Pharmacia & Upjohn fell 2 5/8, or 5%, to 50 3/8.
Circuit City fell 15/16, or 2.3%, to 41 5/8. The company posted third-quarter income from continuing
operations of 27 cents a share, compared with the First Call estimate of 26 cents. Circuit City is a
consumer-electronics retailer based in Richmond, Va.
With stocks in the midst of what is typically the strongest part of the year, analysts said equities should
continue to perform well.
"It's very hard for the market to go down in November, December and January," said Brian Belski, chief
investment strategist at George K. Baum. "The opportunity cost of turning bearish at this time is too high."
Surely, the market is going to run into some speed bumps along the way, especially with some of the
valuations being placed on stocks in certain segments of the technology sector. But it is that sector in which
investors should see the best returns over the long haul.
"Major technological revolutions are always bigger than anyone ever thinks," said Ed Kerschner, chief
strategist at PaineWebber Inc., in a research note. "The ongoing U.S. [gross domestic product] shift will see
the virtual disappearance of the agriculture, mining and construction sectors, the continued shrinkage of the
manufacturing sector and further growth in the service sector."
While investors might be right in worrying short term about the level of major indexes, Mr. Kerschner said the
sizable household money flows into stocks should continue for at least another 10 to 15 years, helping prop
up the market over that time.
Meanwhile, the Commerce Department said new home sales fell 2.3% in November, an unexpected
slowdown. The data could be evidence the three interest-rate increases this year are starting to impact the
U.S. economy. Whether it is enough to stop the Federal Reserve from raising rates in early February is
another matter.
-- Thomas Granahan

A Special Summary and ForecastOf Federal and State TaxDevelopments
Tax Report
A Special Summary and Forecast Of Federal and State Tax Developments
By Tom Herman
875 words
15 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
CONGRESS EXTENDS a law helping workers pay for education expenses.
Part of a bill that President Clinton is expected to sign this week will benefit more than 800,000 workers
whose employers help them pay for their education costs. The legislation will extend a provision through Dec.
31, 2001, allowing a worker to receive as much as $5,250 each year in tax-free tuition assistance. This
provision was set to expire for courses beginning after May 31, 2000.
But House and Senate conferees decided to limit the exclusion once again to undergraduate courses,
rejecting proposals to expand it to graduate-level studies. The exclusion expired for graduate courses in
mid-1996. House Ways and Means Committee Chairman Bill Archer strongly opposes including graduate
courses. "Chairman Archer feels that future doctors, lawyers and corporate executives don't need tax breaks
for education," says Trent Duffy, an aide to the Texas Republican.
Congress's Joint Committee on Taxation estimates this extension will cost the Treasury about $584 million.
LAWMAKERS TARGET a tax dodge used mainly by high-income investors.
Another part of the bill awaiting Clinton's signature is designed to prevent investors from cutting taxes on
income from hedge funds, which are loosely regulated investment partnerships, and similar types of
investments. In a typical transaction, instead of investing directly in a fund, an investor buys a derivative
whose return is tied to the fund's performance. This "constructive ownership transaction" effectively converts
ordinary income or short-term capital gains into long-term capital gains taxed at much lower rates.
The measure will raise an estimated $517 million over the decade, says the Joint Committee on Taxation.
The measure applies to transactions entered into after July 11, 1999. The bill "is important as a fairness
issue, but even more so as a first step to reduce tax avoidance through the use of derivatives," says Rep.
Richard Neal, a Massachusetts Democrat.
MANY PEOPLE pass up a simple tax-saving technique, a report says. Surprisingly few people are taking
advantage of a tax-law provision that allows them to give away as much as $10,000 a year, tax-free, to a
child or anyone else. This conclusion emerges from a study by James M. Poterba of MIT and the National
Bureau of Economic Research. He says nearly two-thirds of elderly households for whom the estate tax may
loom as a potential burden aren't making these gifts.
Mr. Poterba offers several possible explanations. The most obvious is that many older people fear they will
need all their money. People also may worry their children won't make good use of the gifts. Another theory,
he says, is that many wealthy people think the tax laws may be overhauled in coming years.
The $10,000-a-year threshold is indexed for inflation but will remain unchanged next year.
NEW TROIKA: Carol Gold heads the employee-plans unit of the IRS's new tax-exempt and
government-entities division. Steve Miller runs the exempt-organizations unit, and Ed Weiler is director of the
new government-entities area.
THE SUPREME COURT declined to hear a man's appeal that he doesn't owe taxes on "religious, moral and
conscientious grounds." The man also had insisted he shouldn't have to pay taxes because the government
is corrupt and unjust. A lower court labeled these arguments "frivolous" and ordered him to pay $2,000 in
costs and attorney's fees.
SALES AND USE TAXES generate about 25% of all state and local tax revenues, says Harley Duncan,
executive director of the Federation of Tax Administrators in Washington. That is up from about 20% two
decades earlier.

--- Page 40 ---
Page 40 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
STATES ENJOY another year of bulging tax coffers. State tax revenues grew 5.7% in fiscal 1999 over 1998,
says the Center for the Study of the States at the Nelson A. Rockefeller Institute of Government in Albany,
N.Y. That was slightly slower than in the two prior years. But it was "still quite strong," especially since many
states cut taxes, says Elizabeth I. Davis, senior policy analyst.
California posted the largest tax cut in total dollars, Ms. Davis says. But many states had bigger cuts in
percentage terms, such as Connecticut, Delaware, Iowa, Kansas, Maine, Maryland, Massachusetts,
Minnesota, Missouri, Nebraska, Ohio and Wisconsin. Personal-income tax revenues jumped 8.1%, sales-tax
revenues increased 6.2% and corporate-income tax revenues edged up 0.7%.
"Barring any major economic shocks, the next legislative session will produce another crop of tax cuts," Ms.
Davis says.
BRIEFS: The top 25% of taxpayers, ranked by income, made 65% of total 1997 adjusted gross income and
paid about 82% of total federal individual income taxes, says a Tax Foundation report based on IRS data. . . .
Rep. Pete Stark, who voted against the tax bill awaiting Clinton's signature, calls the "Ticket to Work and
Work Incentives Improvement Act of 1999" a gross misnomer: "This title would never pass the `truth in
labeling' test if it were on a box of food, but you can still get away with such falsehoods here in Congress --
especially in the waning hours of the session."

Goodbye, Golden Decade. Now What Will the '00s Bring?
Heard on the Street
Goodbye, Golden Decade. Now What Will the '00s Bring?
By E.S. Browning
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,668 words
13 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Anyone bold enough to try predicting what will happen to stocks in the next decade ought first to look back at
what smart people in 1989 were expecting for the '90s.
Japan's market still was seen as a miracle -- although it was a bubble about to burst. The U.S. was standing
on the doorstep of its greatest bull market of the century, but the operative word among U.S. investors was
angst.
"In 1989, many people were extremely nervous -- nervous about the economy and nervous about the
markets," recalls stockmarket bull Abby Joseph Cohen, now of Goldman Sachs, then of Drexel Burnham
Lambert.
Deutsche Bank Securities investment strategist Ed Yardeni was predicting Dow 5000 within the next few
years, and was being laughed at. With the Dow Jones Industrial Average at 2753.20 as 1989 ended,
investors, still scarred by the 1987 crash, were debating whether it would get to 3000.
The transformation of the U.S. stock market since then has been profound. The Dow Jones Industrial
Average has more than quadrupled, the first time it has gained that much in any decade. The hot Nasdaq
Composite Index has multiplied in value almost eight times, rising to 3620.24 from 454.82 at the end of 1979.
A new lineup of dazzling stocks has created a breathless stock-market psychology that hasn't been seen
since the '60s, or perhaps the '20s. Many stars of the '80s -- Hasbro, Dillards, Liz Claiborne, Limited -- have
fizzled. This decade's stars cater to technologies, most obviously the Internet, that barely existed when the
decade began.
One obvious conclusion: In the new millennium's first decade, the big story could well be one that almost no
one has noticed yet.
As the old millennium ends with remarkably strong stock-index performance, bears warn that there isn't any
guarantee that the bubble won't burst in the new decade.
Of the 10 biggest stocks in the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, according to Ned Davis Research, the
biggest gainer during the '80s was a company that has since fallen under a cloud -- cigarette-maker Philip
Morris, whose share price multiplied nine-fold in 10 years.
Today, that advance looks puny. A share of America Online, the top performer among the big 10 stocks of the
'90s, has multiplied in value almost 800 times during the decade (on a split-adjusted basis), for a gain of
almost 80,000%. Microsoft has seen its price multiply 70 times over, a gain of more than 7,000%. Philip
Morris's stock, meanwhile, rose just 85% in the decade, far less than many Internet initial stock offerings rise
on their first trading day.
Tech stocks dominate the lists of the decade's strongest stocks. The top-performing financial stock of the
'90s? Charles Schwab, the bellwether of online trading. The biggest gainer of all major stocks? America
Online, followed by Dell Computer.
As the decade draws to a close, technology-oriented individual investors, many of them trading online and
cruising the Internet for ideas, have begun to play a role in the market that rivals that of the pros. The
hot-trading individuals send Internet-related initial public stock-offerings soaring. Although some pros worry
that Internet-related stocks are forming a giant bubble, and while most ordinary, non-tech stocks have been
declining in value, the major indexes are continuing to rise, due in large part to a few hot tech stocks.

--- Page 42 ---
Page 42 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Of 12 stocks recommended at the end of 1989 by four experts quoted in the Wall Street Journal, three were
gold companies -- a defensive investment if there ever was one. Most of the 12 have trailed the market
during the decade; the gold stocks actually have lost value. Among today's most actively traded stocks, a
surprising number weren't even publicly listed in 1989: Cisco Systems, Yahoo!, America Online, Qualcomm,
MCI WorldCom.
Four stocks gained at least 1,000% in both decades: Home Depot, up 5,000% in the '80s and 3,000% in the
'90s; Wal-Mart Stores, up 4,000% in the '80s and 1,000% in the '90s; Gap, up 3,700% in the '80s and 2,300%
in the '90s; and Computer Associates International, up 1,300% in the '80s and 1,600% in the '90s.
The shift in the basic ways that investors view the market may have been as important as the magnitude of
the gains. Fundamental research is out of fashion; simply chasing whatever is hot, which is delicately referred
to as "momentum investing" and was considered a goofy idea a decade ago, is in.
"Investment models have simply changed enormously in the past 10 years," says Henry Herrmann, chief
investment officer at mutual-fund group Waddell & Reed in Overland Park, Kan.
At a meeting with West Coast customers early this year, he scoffed at a question by one young man who
wondered if one of the group's hot funds, for small technology stocks, could match its 60% gain in 1998. So
far, he notes, the fund is doing even better this year than last.
Expectations are through the roof. "People are borrowing money on their houses to invest in stocks, and in
1989 you didn't see any of that," Mr. Herrmann says. "People trying to adopt a rational view these days are
viewed as Cassandras."
Almost no analysts in the '80s foresaw the stock bonanza of the '90s, Mr. Herrmann notes, but a few did
accurately identify the changes that were under way: productivity gains, new technologies, rising profits,
falling inflation and lower interest rates. Among the most accurate, he says, were Mr. Yardeni of Deutsche
Bank and PaineWebber investment strategist Edward Kerschner.
Goldman Sachs's Ms. Cohen, who accurately predicted a 1990 bear market, soon began issuing similarly
bullish reports. "Our numbers, our models, were telling me in the early part of decade that the S&P 500 was
as much as 50% undervalued," she recalls.
Can the gains continue?
Ms. Cohen and Mr. Kerschner now consider that major stock indexes have little hope of continuing their
strings of 20% annual gains. They have achieved fair value. Future advances, these analysts say, will come
from rises in corporate earnings. That means index gains could revert to their past average of a bit more than
7% a year.
For Mr. Kerschner, that means the Dow industrials aren't likely to be much higher than 12500 at the end of
next year, up perhaps 12% from here. But, he adds, if you compound 7% gains over the next decade, you still
wind up at Dow 25000 in 2010.
Perhaps more important than that, he and others now believe that the stock market's basic nature may have
changed, making bear markets less frequent and less severe.
In this view, the business cycle itself has been modified, with more emphasis on information technology and
less on boombust swings. In the first 40 years after World War II, Mr. Kerschner notes, the U.S. suffered a
recession every 4.6 years, on average. "Now, we have had just one in 18 years. You just aren't going to get
the big bear markets that were typical of an industrial age, with big inventories and boom-bust cycles."
Ms. Cohen is starting to think that this could be a good time to put money in economies that are going to
benefit from the trends that helped the U.S. in the '90s.
"Europe looks like the U.S. at end of 1980s," she says. "There are wonderful opportunities," but because
more work still must be done "on profitability and on cost cutting, outsiders can't see it all" yet.
As for Mr. Yardeni, the former raging bull now considers the market wildly overvalued. He has become
well-known for his warning that a jolt such as the year-2000 computer glitch could throw us into a recession
and a bear market. And then? He expects another big bull market, with Dow 15000 in 2005.
Robert Harrington, co-head of listed block trading at PaineWebber, notes that investors have gone from
fearing in 1987 that the overall investment system couldn't continue to work, to thinking in 1999 that stocks
have nowhere to go but up.

--- Page 43 ---
Page 43 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
People "are almost ready to throw in the towel now and say they are going up forever," he says. "We thought
that Microsoft's 10-year move was dynamic, and some of these Internet stocks are doing it in 10 weeks. If you
can honestly say you predicted that, then I tip my hat to you."

Money: The Century's Agony
Money: The Century's Agony
By Robert L. Bartley
1,600 words
10 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
STOCKHOLM -- A Nobel Prize is scarcely an invitation to modesty, least of all with the turn of a millennium
only weeks away. So Robert Mundell, the 1999 laureate in economics, took as his memorial lecture subject
"A Reconsideration of the Twentieth Century."
The point is that the economic dislocations of the century, and the political dislocations they produced or
advanced, flowed from ill-understood perturbations in international economics. In particular, the 20th century
began with a highly efficient international monetary mechanism operating smoothly to facilitate trade and
capital movements, but the traditional gold standard broke down with World War I. After confusions such as
the Depression of the 1930s and inflation of the 1970s, the century is closing with efforts to re-establish
some of the principles once applied through the use of gold. It has evolved "three islands of monetary
stability," the dollar, the euro and the yen, but "exchange rates between them will be the source of greatest
disturbances to the international monetary system."
The Mundellian view is familiar enough to readers who follow this newspaper's commentary and editorial
opinions. In addition to his own articles, such as his series on the advent of the euro last year, he's been the
intellectual fountainhead of many of the economic ideas advanced in our editorials over the past two
decades. He has been a champion not only of exchange rate stability but of the supply-side policy mix that
allowed the Reagan administration to subdue the stagflation it inherited in 1981. The then-heterodox idea of
fighting inflation with tight monetary policy and fighting stagnation with incentive-directed tax cuts has its
roots in his seminal 1960s work on international economics cited by the Nobel committee.
"The main thing we miss today is a universal money," Mr. Mundell told his audience here, a "standard of
value, the link between the past and the future and the cement of civilization linking remote parts of the
human race to one another." From the time of Augustus until 1914, this role was filled by gold; "currencies
were just names for particular weights of gold." So "the absence of gold as an intrinsic part of our monetary
system today makes our century, the one that has just passed, unique in several thousand years."
The gold standard worked not by magic or divine ordinance, but because of intellectual principles that are
reasonably well understood, especially after the body of work celebrated with this week's Nobel award. Gold
was at least part of the base for national money, so that if a nation lost gold its money supply would contract,
automatically correcting depreciation of its currency. Thus the standard was a "self-regulating rudder that
guided policy makers toward equilibrium." Exchange rates were predictable, though the inflation rate shared
by all nations was sometimes influenced by accidents such as new gold discoveries, or nations leaving the
gold standard, usually because of war, or returning to it afterward.
These effects struck with vengeance in World War I. Wartime deficit financing forced the belligerents off gold.
Much of their gold was then exported to the U.S., swelling its money supply; the U.S. price level doubled
during the war, and while the Federal Reserve reversed some of the inflation, the price level remained 35%
above prewar levels through most of the 1920s.
Germany broke its hyperinflation by establishing the reichsmark at a level appropriate to the dollar but 40%
overvalued against prewar gold. Britain then returned to gold at the prewar parity. France and other
countries generally also returned to gold. This created a huge demand for gold, and thus a powerful
deflationary force around the world, leading to the Great Depression.
A few economists understood. French economist Charles Rist had enough influence that his nation did not go
back to the prewar gold parity and escaped the worst of the downturn. Ludwig von Mises warned that
restoration of gold would bring deflation. After gold was restored Gustav Cassel of Sweden wrote of the
need to restrict monetary demand for it "to prevent a permanent fall of the general price level and a prolonged
and world-wide depression which would inevitably be connected with such a fall in prices." After all, this is
how the gold standard always worked -- correcting a period of inflation with a period of deflation.

--- Page 45 ---
Page 45 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the interwar period, the economic, political and intellectual price of the deflation proved intolerable. The
deflationary pressure led to the 1929 stock market crash, "sparked off by news that the notorious
Smoot-Hawley Tariff, which raised tariffs to levels unprecedented in over a century, would probably pass
Congress." General deflation was compounded by the implosion of trade and credit and the collapse of
sterling in 1931. By 1934 "the world depression had deepened, economic nationalism was on the march, and
the disease of totalitarianism had spread over most of the European continent."
These events might have been averted by a devaluation of the dollar, and thus currencies generally, against
gold. Instead of forcing the world economy to adjust to the prewar price level, that is, the answer was to
accept the wartime inflation and then continue with the automatic international discipline of the gold
standard. But almost no one thought of this; though the Roosevelt administration did float against gold and
then come back at a higher price, it prohibited citizens from even holding gold. Intellectually, "economists
blamed the gold standard instead of their mishandling of it and turned away from international automaticity to
national macroeconomic management."
Though John Maynard Keynes had warned of depression as early as 1928, publication of his "General
Theory" in 1936 offered prescriptions for management of a closed economy, oblivious to the impact of
international monetary events. Meanwhile, the world evolved what is known as the Bretton Woods system,
under which other currencies were fixed to the dollar and the U.S. would redeem their dollars in gold at $35
an ounce. This implied use of U.S. domestic monetary policy to stabilize gold at that price, a discipline that
the U.S. never accepted and that its huge stores of gold allowed it to ignore while it pursued internal
macroeconomic policies. When the Federal Reserve lost gold, it "sterilized" the contraction of the money
supply by buying an equivalent amount of government bonds.
In its monetary impact, World War II replayed World War I. The price level doubled, and gold became scarce
at $35 an ounce. "The stability in the world economy required increases in international reserves more or less
pari passu with the growth of income and trade"; but there was not enough gold to go around. This time,
however, policy makers did not repeat the mistake of the 1920s; instead, they made the opposite one. On
Aug. 15, 1971, President Nixon took the world monetary system off gold, ushering in a system of generalized
floating exchange rates.
This proved to be an engine of world inflation, since domestic inflation in any country was not subject to
international discipline. Also, financial speculation could move exchange rates, "leading to wasteful
misallocation of resources as resources are shifted from the international to the domestic sector and back
again." For small countries, speculation could destabilize exchange markets entirely.
To reduce the "chaos of a completely decentralized system," the world has now gravitated toward currency
areas, with smaller states fixing their currencies to larger neighbors -- the dollar, the euro, the yen and, in the
case of parts of Africa, the French franc. Economic growth resumed in the U.S. and the world generally with
the Reagan supply-side policies. Even with a nine-month recession in 1990, since 1982 the U.S. has
experienced "one of the greatest expansions in the history of any country." Around the world, other nations
gradually "began to rediscover the policies that made the gold standard work and by the turn of the century,
had brought inflation under control."
So at the turn of the millennium, the world economy enjoys good growth and low inflation. But the danger
sign on the horizon is the instability of exchange rates among the three big currencies. "It would be a grave
mistake to believe that the closed nature of the three big blocs would make exchange rates less important or
that the dollar-euro rate can be treated with `benign neglect.'" Central bankers should not feel free to continue
the pursuit of domestic objectives oblivious to the international sector, as the Federal Reserve in particular
usually has.
A final theme in the Mundell lecture here was that an international monetary system "is governed in large part
by the power configurations of nations." And that "the most important fact of the twentieth century was the
rise to superpower status and even hegemony of the United States." The concluding words of his lecture
were these: "It remains for the next century to see whether a restoration of the international monetary system
is compatible with the power configuration of the world economy."
Obviously this is a plea for U.S. leadership in forging a new system to contain the threat of instability among
the leading currency blocs. And if a visiting journalist can be allowed an elaboration: There is no trace of such
leadership as the U.S. prepares to elect a new president. The discussion among the candidates, and among
the U.S. elites in general, shows no sign of understanding the impact of international monetary events, let
alone of devising initiatives to contain them.
---
Mr. Bartley is editor of the Journal.

Letters to the Editor:Mexico's Money-Supply Growth
Letters to the Editor: Mexico's Money-Supply Growth
467 words
8 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A23
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
I disagree with the details of the monetary policy analysis laid out in the Nov. 19 Americas column "In
Mexico, Too Much Money Still Chases Too Few Goods." Jim Dorn argues that Mexico's fast money-supply
growth is causing inflation. He concludes that the central bank should "bind itself to a monetary rule that
limits the quantity of money to a noninflationary growth rate" or else allow Mexicans to use other currencies.
I agree that Mexicans deserve to use a meaningful currency and that inflation at more than 12% in 1999 is
too high. It is true that Mexico's money supply has been growing fast. Monetary base grew 27.5% in the 12
months through September 1999, well above Mexico's nominal GDP growth.
However, I disagree that fast money-supply growth in 1999 is causing the 1999 inflation. Mexico's 1999
inflation is the result of peso weakness in early 1998. The peso held steady at 8 per dollar in 1996 and 1997,
causing inflation to fall rapidly. In 1998, the peso was allowed to weaken 20% to 10 per dollar. Gold and
commodity prices in Mexico increased even while they were falling in most other currencies. As a result of
peso weakness, inflation rose and peaked at 19% in the 12 months through January 1999. Money-supply
growth was relatively stable throughout this period and does not explain the changes in the inflation rate. (In
1996 and 1997, monetary base grew 26% and 29% per year yet inflation fell rapidly. Money growth slowed
somewhat in 1998 and 1999, yet inflation rose.)
I don't think money-supply growth is a proper target for monetary policy. The demand for money changes
drastically based on the public's expectations about the future value of the currency. Fast money
supply-growth is normal and noninflationary when a country is building confidence in itself and its currency. If
Mexico had limited the growth in money supply in 1996 and 1997, it would have choked off its economic
recovery. With the peso now strong at 9.3 per dollar, inflation is falling rapidly.
Mexico's money-supply growth should be regulated based on the value of the peso and the demand for
money, not an arbitrary money-growth target. If the peso weakens, as it did in 1998, its value should be
promptly restored by reducing the monetary base. Mexico's process of achieving low inflation and fast
growth could be accelerated through a declaration of a stable peso policy, a tax rate cut, deregulation, and a
new focus on productivity and competition.
David Malpass
New York
(Mr. Malpass is chief international economist at Bear Stearns and was deputy assistant Treasury secretary
for developing nations during the Reagan administration.)

Gold Rises on Bargain Hunting, Short Covering
Commodities
Gold Rises on Bargain Hunting, Short Covering
By Janet Whitman
Dow Jones Newswires
418 words
8 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C25
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold prices leapt after previous sell-offs encouraged demand, analysts said.
The market, which has suffered since a poorly received gold auction by the United Kingdom on Nov. 29,
slipped to a 10-week low Monday after the Dutch central bank said it would sell some of its gold reserves.
Bargain hunters in Asia jumped in overnight to take advantage of the weaker prices, forcing shorts -- or
traders who sold futures in expectation of lower prices -- to cover their positions by purchasing an equivalent
number of futures.
The active February gold future on the Comex Division of the New York Mercantile Exchange surged $7.20
to $285.40 a troy ounce, more than recouping Monday's $3.90 decline.
"Any time you flirt with these lows, people get interested [in buying metal]," said George Gero, first vice
president with Prudential Securities in New York.
A precious-metals trader in New York agreed, noting that, with the losses since the U.K. auction, "a $20
savings is roughly what these guys were getting."
He added that "the weak dollar against the yen helped."
A weaker U.S. dollar makes gold, which is traded in dollars, cheaper to buy. A strong dollar in recent weeks
has hindered demand for gold, according to analysts.
Analysts said the market overreacted to the Dutch central bank's plan to sell 100 metric tons of gold next
year and another 200 metric tons during the next five years. A metric ton is equal to 2,204.62 pounds.
"It's really not that significant," said William O'Neill, chief futures strategist with Merrill Lynch in New York.
Indeed, the sale was part of a previously announced plan, in which 15 European central banks intend to limit
their gold sales to a total of 2,000 tons during the next five years.
The Dutch announcement "was old news," said Prudential's Mr. Gero. "When the shorts realized it, they
covered. They overreacted [Monday], and the market got into what we call an oversold condition."
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures fell on the New York Mercantile Exchange, as traders booked profits ahead of the
release of the American Petroleum Institute's weekly inventory report. The January contract dropped 44 cents
to $26.22 a barrel. Traders said the market was vulnerable to bearish news after January crude gained more
than $2 a barrel in the prior four sessions.

Gold Falls on News of Dutch Central Bank Sales
Commodities
Gold Falls on News of Dutch Central Bank Sales
By Janet Whitman
Dow Jones Newswires
601 words
7 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold prices fell after the Dutch central bank said it intends to sell 300 metric tons of its gold
reserves during the next five years.
The Dutch announcement added to the gloomy tone in the gold market, which has been on a steep
downward curve since a poorly received Bank of England gold auction Nov. 29.
The active February gold contract on the Comex Division of the New York Mercantile Exchange slid $3.90 to
$278.20 a troy ounce, following declines of more than $12 an ounce last week.
"Obviously, the news from the Dutch central bank has reopened some of the old wounds in the bullion
market," said James Steel, metals analyst with Refco Inc., a New York brokerage house.
Mr. Steel was referring to the overhang of official-sector sales, which helped pressure the price of gold to a
series of 20-year lows this summer.
The Nederlandsche Bank announced it will sell 100 metric tons initially and an additional 200 metric tons
during the next five years. After the sales are completed, the Netherlands' gold reserves will total 700 metric
tons. A metric ton equals 2,204.62 pounds.
Unlike the United Kingdom, which in May announced it will sell more than half its gold reserves in a series of
auctions, the Netherlands won't announce each sale beforehand.
"We need to anticipate that they could start selling into any kind of rally," said Dave Meger, senior metals
analyst with Alaron Trading in Chicago.
The Dutch announcement shouldn't be a surprise to the market. The sales are part of the plan of the 15
European central banks to limit their total gold sales to 2,000 tons during the next five years and to freeze
gold-lending levels, market observers said.
That plan, announced Sept. 26, helped fuel a sharp rally in the price of gold. Since then, however, the gold
market, including futures, has suffered from poor demand for physical gold, underscored by the significantly
fewer-than-expected bids at last week's U.K. auction, market observers said.
"I think a lot of people are asking themselves: 'Why should we hold on to it if nobody wants it?,' " said one
precious metals trader. "Central banks don't want to own gold. They might try to support the price with a
quasibullish statement from time to time. It's in their interest to help the price."
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures rallied at Nymex, as the market priced in another week of absent Iraqi supplies and a
temporary pipeline stoppage between Louisiana and Illinois. The January crude-oil contract rose 85 cents to
$26.66 a barrel. On Friday, Iraq rejected the United Nations' one-week extension of the humanitarian
oil-for-food deal, under which all Iraqi oil is sold, keeping Iraq out of the market for at least another week.
"That supply is being kept out of the market at a time when we can least afford not to have it," said Andrew
LeBow, senior vice president for energy at ED&F Man in New York
COFFEE: Prices fell on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange division of the New York Board of Trade
because of heavier-than-expected weekend rainfall in Brazil, traders said. The March contract dropped 9.50
cents to $1.3455 a pound. Prices rallied sharply last week because of concerns Brazil's crop has been
damaged by recent dry weather.

White House Plans to ProposeChanges in IMF Lending Policy
White House Plans to Propose Changes in IMF Lending Policy
By Michael M. Phillips
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
857 words
6 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
RIO DE JANEIRO -- With the International Monetary Fund under attack in Congress, the Clinton
administration plans to propose significant changes in the way the international lender does business.
Treasury Secretary Lawrence Summers is expected to call on the IMF to focus more on emergency lending
than on helping ailing economies through long-term revolving loans. He expects to begin making his views
public in time for them to be discussed in Berlin on Dec. 16 at the inaugural meeting of the so-called Group of
20, a new international forum for discussing global economic issues. IMF member countries still would have
to approve any such reforms.
The administration believes this is a propitious time to take a new look at the IMF. The financial crisis that
swept the world over the past 2 1/2 years has largely dissipated, and IMF Managing Director Michel
Camdessus, who led the fund's response to that crisis, has announced his intention to step aside for a
still-unnamed successor.
"I've said many times the IMF is indispensable, but that doesn't mean we have to be satisfied with the one we
have," Mr. Summers said in an interview during a visit Friday to Brazil.
The move could help quiet criticism of the IMF on Capitol Hill, where Mr. Clinton had a rough time winning
approval this fall for the IMF to use its gold holdings to fund debt forgiveness for poor countries. Some
lawmakers and GOP presidential candidates have even called for a complete cutoff of U.S. funding for the
IMF, which is owned by 182 member countries and provides loans and economic advice to countries in
financial trouble.
Mr. Summers swapped views on the issues in private meetings last week with the economic team of
Argentine Presidentelect Fernando de la Rua in Buenos Aires, and in Rio with Brazilian Finance Minister
Pedro Malan and Central Bank President Arminio Fraga. Both countries are members of the G-20, created in
the wake of the financial crisis to give rich countries and major developing nations a place for informal debate
of global economic issues.
Beyond the Berlin G-20 meeting, the U.S. hopes Mr. Summers's ideas will help guide the continuing
campaign to redesign the world financial system to prevent and shorten future crises. As its major donor, the
U.S. has enormous influence in the way the IMF is run.
The Treasury Department hasn't released details of the plan. But Mr. Summers is likely to suggest that IMF
members consider focusing its lending more on emergency situations, such as recent crises in Thailand,
Ecuador and Brazil in recent years, rather than on recurrent loans to troubled economies, which U.S. officials
believe is better left to the World Bank.
Mr. Summers may also propose that the IMF streamline the kinds of loan programs it offers. The IMF
currently has a variety of packages, including financing to help countries adjust to commodity-price shocks
and to promote longer-term development, also the World Bank's home turf.
Mr. Summers also would like the IMF to take on a greater role in monitoring -- and making sure that private
investors are able to monitor -- countries' foreign obligations. That would be part of a larger discussion in the
G-20 of what the U.S. sees as a major cause of the recent global crisis: when countries face short-term debts
coming due that far exceed their foreign-currency reserves. Such a situation leaves them vulnerable to the
kind of financial chaos that struck East Asia in 1997, then moved through Russia and here to Brazil.
Developing countries traditionally assess their foreign-currency reserves in terms of how many months of
imports they will pay for. But, as South Korea and other countries learned to their despair, reserves can be
drained quickly if debts come due and investors lose confidence in the economy and the currency.

--- Page 52 ---
Page 52 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The IMF and developing nations, U.S policy makers feel, should judge the adequacy of a country's foreign
reserves by taking into account not just how much the government owes overseas, but also the foreign debts
of its banks and businesses, and the dollars the government has committed on the futures market. Countries,
they say, should assess their vulnerability by working through what might happen if, for instance, the price of
a critical export product -- such as oil or coffee -- were to drop.
One idea, proposed some months ago by Argentine Deputy Finance Minister Pablo Guidotti, is that countries
should keep enough reserves so that they could go a full year without having to borrow on international
markets.
Separately, IMF officials say they won't approve a $640 million loan installment for Russia -- already delayed
for several months -- until Moscow makes further progress on economic reforms. Mr. Camdessus met Friday
in Washington with Russia's envoy to the large industrialized nations and reviewed the steps the IMF requires
before it will release the second installment on a $4.5 billion loan.

Gold-Futures Prices Skid on Employment Report

Gold-Futures Prices Skid on Employment Report
By Janet Whitman
Dow Jones Newswires
569 words
6 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold prices plunged Friday, following the release of a benign U.S. employment report, which
calmed investor fears about inflation.
In addition to the report, which sparked a surge in U.S. stocks and bonds, gold continued to suffer from
disappointment about the Nov. 29 British gold auction.
The active February gold future on the Comex Division of the New York Mercantile Exchange fell $5 to
$282.10 a troy ounce, bringing its loss for the week to $12.90.
"The unemployment report translated into a surge in stocks and bonds. It showed no inflationary pressures,
which was negative for gold," said William O'Neill, chief futures strategist for Merrill Lynch.
The Labor Department reported that nonfarm payrolls added 234,000 workers last month, after a 263,000
increase in October. The slowdown kept the unemployment rate, already at a 29-year low, steady at 4.1%.
While largely within expectations, the figures eased concerns about inflation, and analysts said they
indicated that the Federal Reserve isn't likely to raise interest rates in December. Evidence that inflation is
at bay diminishes the likelihood that investors will turn to gold as a hedge.
The economic report came on the heels of the "main negative for the week," the British gold auction, said Mr.
O'Neill. "The market is still suffering a hangover from that." He added, "For anyone who was thinking there
was going to be some {year-2000} accumulation of gold, I think this auction completely eliminated that idea."
The ratio of bids to auctioned gold was 2.1 to 1, compared with 8 to 1 in the previous auction in September,
Mr. O'Neill said.
That a gold mine was awarded the bulk of the gold also was bearish, analysts and traders said, because it
showed other parties weren't major bidders and that the mine will have gold with which to balance any short
sales it has made. A surge in prices in September and October was fueled in part by short-covering, or the
buying of gold to meet selling commitments, among gold miners.
"People were cautiously bullish ahead of the auction, but when a producer of gold comes in and buys 40% of
the gold, it's not a very positive sign," said Tim Gardiner, head of precious metals with Mitsui USA in New
York.
In other commodity markets Friday:
COFFEE: Futures surged again on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange division of the New York Board of
Trade on forecasts of dry weekend weather in Brazil, the world's largest coffee producer, which has suffered
crop damage from dry weather. The March contract climbed 8.10 cents to $1.4405 a pound, bringing its gain
for the week to about 30 cents. Those forecasts prompted buying by speculators and kept most producers
from selling into the rally, traders said. Brazilian "producers are not selling and who knows better than
producers what the weather is like in their country," said one trader.
LUMBER: Futures fell at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange due to talk of excess supplies, participants said.
After a limit-down move in intraday trading, the leading January contract settled $10 lower at $334.10 per
thousand board feet. "There are wholesalers who have excess quantities of unsold lumber in their hands," a
Canadian-based trader said.

Time to Tame Exchange Rates
Time to Tame Exchange Rates
By David Malpass
1,023 words
3 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Major currencies have been gyrating in value of late. Since May the dollar has strengthened 6% against the
euro and weakened 18% against the yen. Other currencies have had even bigger changes in value.
Yesterday the euro dropped below $1 for the first time.
My reading is that the euro is stable, the dollar strong and the yen too strong. While the euro has weakened
sharply against the yen, it is stable relative to gold and other commodity prices and against its own price
index. Thus, while the volatility of the euro is one contributor to Europe's economic sluggishness, the level of
the euro is probably just about right. The strength of the dollar relative to the euro is probably a response to
Y2K concerns, faster growth expectations for the U.S. and Europe's persistent efforts to stop tax competition
on the Continent.
The yen, by contrast, is strong against gold and commodity prices, all world currencies and its own price
indexes, which have been registering severe deflation. Japan should acknowledge that the yen's strength is a
signal that its monetary policy is too tight. It should shift from its failed government spending policy to
monetary stimulus, expanding central-bank assets until deflation stops.
While currency gyrations create short-term winners and losers, the long-term loser is the entire global
economy. Unnecessary currency volatility is costly: It risks deflation for some, inflation for others. It helps big
businesses, which can partially insure the risk, relative to small businesses, which can't. It impedes
cross-border investments, slowing the spread of new-era productivity gains from the U.S. to other countries.
And it contributes to the polarization of global capital, creating surpluses of capital in some (mostly rich)
countries and shortages of capital elsewhere.
The latest volatility should prompt countries to recognize that long-term currency stability is achievable and in
their interests. When the economic theory of floating exchange rates was invented, it did not envision a world
in which major currencies would change value by 50% every few years, as we've seen with the yen.
America has contributed to the confusion over global exchange-rate policy. In recent years, the U.S. has
seemed to build its entire exchange rate view on the oversimplification that a strong dollar is in the national
interest. Yet it has declined to explain how a currency's strength should be measured or whether unlimited
strength is good. Meanwhile, through the International Monetary Fund, it has regularly recommended weak
currencies for poor countries.
A stable currency, not a "strong" or "weak" one, is appropriate during most of a country's economic life. A
strong-dollar policy made sense after a period of currency weakness and inflation such as the U.S.
experienced in 1993 and 1994. But by continuing the policy after a substantial strengthening of the dollar, the
administration created a momentum play into the U.S. dollar, adding to global currency volatility. Developing
countries have been whipsawed by the strength of the dollar and the vacuum of economic thought at the IMF.
While Brazil, Ecuador, Turkey and Colombia pursue weak-currency policies, Mexico and Southeast Asia have
pursued strong-currency policies this year. In all these countries, stable currencies with low volatility would
add dramatically to their long-term economic growth rates.
At the core of the economic confusion is the prevailing, and harmful, view that the value of a currency should
change with the business cycle to reflect economic fundamentals. The currency should weaken when an
economy slumps, the argument goes, and strengthen when it accelerates. This was the logic that has pushed
the euro to extreme weakness in early July and that has strengthened the yen to 102 per dollar, so strong
that it will choke off Japan's recovery.
We know from experience that currencies can be independent of a country's economic health. In May 1998
Japan's yen was sinking below 140 per dollar. The common analysis was that economic weakness and low
interest rates would push the yen to 160 per dollar. A year later, Japan's growth is limited to government
spending and exports, driving unemployment well above U.S. levels. Yet the yen is much stronger, the result

--- Page 55 ---
Page 55 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
of a policy of deflation caused by a contraction in the central bank's total assets. This dramatizes a country's
ability to control the value of its money regardless of economic conditions.
Europe should welcome a less jingoistic U.S. approach to exchange rates. Given its discomfort with the weak
euro -- actually, the strong dollar -- Europe may want to create a benchmark other than the dollar for
evaluating the euro. Otherwise, it will trap itself into viewing the world through American asset valuations.
This would cause mistakes in monetary policy and would reduce the attractiveness of investment in the
eurozone. My preference would be for the European Central Bank to evaluate the euro using the price of
gold or another indicator of the euro's absolute value. It would announce in advance that when the price of
the benchmark rises (meaning the value of the euro is falling), the quantity of euros will fall, and vice versa.
Evaluating the euro in absolute terms rather than relative to the dollar would allow Europe to refocus its
attention on structural reforms. Governments in Europe are massive and growing, at a time when world
competition will not tolerate this strategy. Taxes are stuck at very high levels, forcing the flight of jobs from the
Continent. Labor laws discourage employment, threatening to create a permanently unemployed and
unskilled European underclass. In order to focus on these true problems, the euro, like the dollar, should be
"strong and stable" and should be evaluated in absolute terms, not relative to its fluctuations against unstable
currencies elsewhere.
Wild swings in exchange rates are anti-growth and are the responsibility of government. The best policy for
growth -- in the U.S. and world-wide -- would be to seek currency stability rather than volatility.
---
Mr. Malpass is chief international economist for Bear Stearns.
