Cocoa Falls as Ivory Coast Producers End Strike
Commodities
Cocoa Falls as Ivory Coast Producers End Strike
By Angela Privin
Dow Jones Newswires
571 words
2 December 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Cocoa prices fell on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange on news that Ivory Coast producers
ended a strike that had paralyzed the country's cocoa shipments and exports, traders said.
Ivory Coast, which accounts for more than 40% of world cocoa production, liberalized the sector Aug. 12,
abolishing a system of fixed prices from farm to port and leaving farmers vulnerable to falling world prices.
Producers, who had been on strike since Nov. 22 in protest, returned to work when demands were met to
reintroduce fixed prices.
The March contract fell $32 to $867 a metric ton on CSCE, a division of the New York Board of Trade. It had
shot up $73 Monday on news that producers had threatened to continue the strike for one more week and
burn stocks. Some cocoa was burned -- estimates range from 10,000 to 70,000 metric tons.
Traders said the strike's end meant a resumption of physical sales and futures sales to hedge them.
Chocolate manufacturers' buying lent support as prices dropped, they added.
Cocoa will resume its downtrend now that the supply disruptions have ended, traders and analysts said, with
the March contract likely to retest the low of $812 a metric ton it registered in late November.
"The flow of cocoa should be smooth and continue picking up through the middle of February," said analyst
Boyd Cruel of Alaron Trading Corp. in Chicago.
Some saw a limit to the price decline.
"With the market coming off there is more reason to suspect there will be a market bottom, rather than a
steady decline," said Judith Ganes, an analyst with Merrill Lynch in New York, explaining that the strike
showed the market that prices can't fall indefinitely without a producer reaction. "Manufacturers will be keen
to buy on price breaks in fear that things could flare up in the Ivory Coast again," she added.
In an attempt to stabilize world prices, Ivory Coast producers said they will create a buffer stock of 150,000
metric tons. Market watchers were skeptical that the burning or stockpiling will make a dent in futures prices
as world supply far outweighs demand, a fundamental influence that has been pressuring prices for months,
traders added.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures rebounded from negative territory to settle with modest gains at the New York
Mercantile Exchange, as the heavy selling seen earlier in the week dried up. The January contract rose 41
cents to $25 a barrel, after falling to as low as $24.10. Traders said a sharp fall in prices left the market
oversold and prompted short covering, or the buying of futures to cancel previous sales. "We saw some short
covering late in the day, but given the rout in the market over the past two days, we're not talking about much
of a recovery," said John Kilduff, senior vice president, energy-risk management at Fi mat USA Inc. in New
York.
GOLD: Futures dropped on the Comex division of Nymex following Monday's disappointing U.K. gold
auction, analysts said. The February contract fell $1.10 to $291.90 a troy ounce. A stronger U.S. dollar, which
diminishes the appeal of gold as a hedge against inflation, contributed to the weakness, analysts said.

Gold Futures Prices Fall Amid Weak Auction
Commodities
Gold Futures Prices Fall Amid Weak Auction
By Enza Tedesco
Dow Jones Newswires
638 words
30 November 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold futures on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange took a nose dive
after tepid bidding at the latest United Kingdom gold auction, participants said.
In the third of five scheduled gold auctions, the Bank of England sold 25 metric tons of gold, or 803,600
ounces, at an allotment price of $293.50 a troy ounce. Each ounce of gold available drew only 2.1 times as
many bids, compared with an eight-to-one ratio at the last auction in September. Comex's February contract
slid $8.30 to $293.50 an ounce.
"It was a poor auction and that certainly shows that there's not an overwhelming demand for physical gold
here over the short term," said William O'Neill, senior futures strategist with Merrill Lynch in New York.
Mr. O'Neill said the main reason for price weakness was the outcome of the sale didn't match expectations.
"People were anticipating a decent auction, a better-received one, and clearly it didn't turn out that way," he
said.
David Meger, a senior metals analyst with Alaron Trading Corp. in Chicago, said given the large amount of
net long positions, bets that prices would rise, larger demand would have been needed to move prices higher.
"Because it was relatively neutral to probably unfriendly, a large amount of those net longs jumped out of the
market," by selling futures, Mr. Meger said.
George Gero, first vice president at Prudential Securities in New York, also attributed gold's drop to
participants' exiting the Comex December contract ahead of its first notice day today, when contract holders
will begin declaring their intention of making or taking delivery. Pure speculators are usually unwilling to hold
contracts during that period for fear of being involved in the physical-commodity trade underlying the futures
contract.
Most participants said they expect gold prices to consolidate within the range of $288 to $300 an ounce until
year end.
"For the most part, it's going to be difficult to find demand until next year . . . Y2K fears have dissipated
because it turns out that most of the western world is Y2K-prepared and there's not that anxiety that existed a
month ago . . . that lessened demand also," Mr. Gero said. "Plus, there's a rebound in the stock market,
interest rates are going up, and that's bearish, because it makes gold more expensive . . . so all these
things came at a wrong time like they usually do."
In other commodity markets:
COCOA: Futures surged on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange division of the New York Board of Trade in
reaction to protests against the recent liberalization of the market by producers in Ivory Coast, traders said.
The March contract leapt $73 to $927 a metric ton. Ivory Coast producers began striking Nov. 22 and have
threatened to burn cocoa stocks if their demands for higher fixed prices aren't met. Traders said concerns
about a lack of supply from the world's largest producer led to the covering of shorts-the buying of futures to
balance previous sales-by participants concerned they might not have the cocoa to make delivery
commitments.
CRUDE OIL: Futures fell at Nymex on signs that Iraq will soon return to the export market. The country
recently rejected an extension of the oil-for-food deal with the United Nations. The January contract dropped
91 cents to $25.96 a barrel. Sources said Iraq pumped 300,000 barrels Monday through the Iraq-Turkey
pipeline as it has done every day since Wednesday. Although the pipe can handle one million barrels a day,
the limited restart was viewed as a sign that Iraq will fully return to the export market.

Government Starts Minting New `Golden Dollar'
Commodities
Government Starts Minting New `Golden Dollar'
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
836 words
19 November 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Pretty soon, you may not recognize the money in your pocket.
The U.S. Mint began full-scale production of a new $1 coin yesterday, a gold-colored depiction of the Native
American explorer Sacagawea that replaces the Susan B. Anthony coin. The new coin is part of a revamping
of U.S. money that includes new $5 and $10 bills next year, which have the presidents' images set off-center.
(Twenty-dollar bills and larger denominations already have been changed.)
With the new $1 coin, dubbed the "Golden Dollar," the Mint is banking on an ambitious marketing plan, a new
design and 20 years of inflation since the "Susie's" introduction to help the new coin succeed. Officials and
even the coin's proponents say its chances of replacing the paper dollar are virtually nil. But coin-dependent
entities such as mass-transit systems, the Postal Service and the $23 billion vending industry are still hoping
the Sacagawea dollar will provide a boon.
"We're coming to a time when the dollar coin will probably be more useful than people would like to admit,"
said Rep. Michael Castle (R., Del.), who wrote the 1997 law paving the way for the new coins. "Because the
cost of things is higher, there are more items you simply have to spend a dollar on, so this is a good solution."
The new coins are about the size of Susan B. Anthony dollars, will be in circulation by early 2000, and are
made of an alloy of manganese, brass and copper.
Believe it or not, this all started because the country was running out of Susan B. Anthony $1 coins.
U.S. Mint Director Philip N. Diehl said that as coin-operated machines have proliferated, the need for Susan
B. Anthony dollars has grown from about 12 million coins to 60 million in less than five years. With supplies
dwindling after 20 years of the older coin being in circulation, the Mint decided it had to revamp one of its
most unsuccessful products.
"We found that consumers never really liked the coin because it looked too much like a quarter, and there
was no marketing to create any excitement for it," Mr. Diehl said.
Neither of those factors will be the case with the new coin.
The Mint will begin an aggressive four-month advertising campaign in March and also has arranged for the
new dollars to be distributed in a limited number of cereal boxes.
An eight-member committee of experts, government officials and citizens chose Sacagawea to be depicted
on the coin. She was a Shoshone Indian woman who acted as a guide and translator for explorers Lewis and
Clark on their expedition to the Pacific Ocean.
The choice was one fraught with political overtones, particularly in the early stages of the decision making.
One side, led by Rep. Castle, vocally backed the Statue of Liberty, while another pushed for Sacagawea.
But from the outset, then U.S. Treasury Secretary Robert Rubin told the committee their choice had to be a
woman.
"Ultimately, we decided that we wanted someone who had actually lived, not an allegorical figure. After all, no
one ever says we need to have mythical men on our currency," said committee member Patricia A. McGuire,
who is president of Trinity College, a Catholic women's school in Washington.
The Mint estimates that demand for the coins will total $100 million next year, much of which will be spent on
coin-dispensed goods and services ranging from sodas to bus fares.

--- Page 61 ---
Page 61 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In commodity trading yesterday:
WHEAT: Forecasts for precipitation in the arid U.S. Great Plains pushed futures to new contract lows at the
Chicago Board of Trade. Front-month December wheat futures fell 5.25 cents to $2.4050 a bushel, the lowest
settlement for a nearby CBOT wheat contract since July. "Conditions are very dry in the wheat belt [but] there
are forecasts for a better chance of rain," said Steve Koch, analyst with the Stewart-Peterson Group in West
Bend, Wis. Forecasters expect two to three inches of rain across the wheat belt over the weekend and into
early next week. It would be the first rain in more than a month for much of the region.
COFFEE: Futures slipped at the Coffee, Sugar and Cocoa Exchange division of the New York Board of Trade
amid forecasts for rain in Brazil's coffee-growing regions and on talk of ample supplies. Nearby March futures
fell 13.25 cents to $1.167 a pound. "The weather pattern in Brazil has changed; beneficial rains look to be
ahead of us," said James Cordier, a trader with Liberty Trading Group in St. Petersburg, Fla. Separately, a
report Wednesday by the Association of Coffee Producing Countries forecast a heavy world coffee surplus for
the period from April 2000 through March 2001.

As Good Times Roll, So Does Tradition of Social Reform
Politics & Policy
As Good Times Roll, So Does Tradition of Social Reform
By Bob Davis
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,143 words
18 November 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A28
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Late this month in Seattle, thousands of protesters organized by area churches plan to ring a World Trade
Organization conference and make a plea for rich nations to forgive the debts of poor ones. "We need to
correct the imbalances," says the Rev. John Boonstra, who heads the Washington Association of Churches.
The protest reflects the most contemporary of concerns: whether the poor benefit from the global economy.
But it also taps a tradition that runs deeply through U.S. history. During times of prosperity, social movements
focusing on moral issues come to the fore.
Abolitionism gained momentum during the gold rush and railroad booms of the 1840s and 1850s, prohibition
during the Gilded Age at the turn of the century, and civil rights during the post-World War II era. Some of the
movements seem deplorable today; during the Roaring '20s, blacks, Asians and other ethnic groups were
repressed because they didn't seem sufficiently "American."
"Prosperity gives people who want to start movements a sense that they can get the resources together,"
says Michael Kazin, a Georgetown University historian. "Movements aren't usually made up of people who
are desperate."
Social movements don't appear merely because a society is wealthy, of course. But economic growth is an
important element. When personal income rises and individuals become increasingly confident about their
future, economic worries begin to fade, and social issues are spotlighted. "Values trump economics in
economic good times," says David King, a Harvard University political scientist.
For the past few years, personal incomes have been rising, inflation is down and unemployment is at a
decades-long low. That is hardly enough time for '60s-style mass movements to develop. But historians say
that a number of activist causes today have the potential to capture national attention.
Churches and other religious organizations are often involved-institutions that the largely secular mass media
tend to overlook. For instance, many conservative and evangelical churches have made commitments to help
women once on welfare to find and hold jobs. The Christian Women's Job Corps in Birmingham, Ala., recruits
and trains volunteers in about 110 locations around the country. They serve as "mentors" for at least one year
to help former welfare recipients find child care, arrange transportation and navigate the other problems that
crop up in the job world.
Religious instruction plays an important role. "When someone is introduced to Bible study, they find a support
system they never had before," says Trudy Johnson, director of Job Corps.
Although the mixture of social counseling and religious instruction makes some secular advocates of the poor
uncomfortable, Vice President Al Gore and Texas Gov. George W. Bush have courted the activist groups.
Appearing at a Salvation Army adult-rehabilitation center in Atlanta, Mr. Gore talked of a "dramatic
transformation in America," which he identified as "a newly vigorous grass-roots movement tied to nonprofit
institutions, many of them faith-based and valuesbased organizations."
Church members' welfare activism makes them aware of other political issues-politicizing them, in the lingo of
the '60s. Many of the new issues involve the global economy. "There is an overlap," says Ms. Johnson,
ticking off issues such as debt relief and sweatshop conditions abroad. "We have had to learn a lot about fair
trade."
On U.S. campuses, improving working conditions in foreign sweatshops is a burgeoning cause. Organized
under the banner of United Students Against Sweatshop, activists are pressuring college administrators to
guarantee that T-shirts and other goods printed with college logos aren't imported from factories that violate
basic labor rights. The effort is led by several student activists who learned organizing techniques during

--- Page 63 ---
Page 63 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
summer internships in 1997 with Unite, the textile-workers union. As part of their campaign, students at the
University of Michigan held a sit-in at an administrator's office.
Eric Brakken, the group's national organizer, says the students want universities to disclose the names and
locations of the factories and dismiss a Clinton administration-led effort to get clothing makers to agree on
codes of conduct as a "whitewash." But Terry Collingsworth, general counsel of the International Labor Rights
Fund, a church-backed group, says that the student activities could backfire by prompting importers to switch
factories -- and leave poor workers jobless.
Todd Gitlin, a New York University sociologist who studies social protest, says the sweatshop movement
echoes '60s student causes because both made a link between "moral passion and the university
environment."
Starting Nov. 29, globalization issues will be at the forefront of protests, as the WTO, the international body
that sets trade rules, meets in Seattle to launch a new round of global trade negotiations. Thousands of
activists are expected, with the bulk consisting of union members who worry about a classic pocketbook
issue: Expanded imports could threaten their jobs.
But many of the others expected in Seattle will focus on predominantly moral issues: Do rules crafted to
expand trade internationally conflict with environmental laws in different countries? Will worker protections
become obsolete if companies find it even easier to invest abroad? Do nations themselves become
outmoded if capital and goods controlled by private investors determine a nation's economic prospects?
Mike Dolan, who is coordinating the protests, says he relies on local churches to help organize teach-ins and
seminars on the subjects. "It's not coincidental that the principal venues we have organized for our parallel
critique of the WTO are all churches," he says.
During another period of prosperity, the Gilded Age, another group of reformers asked similar questions
about the role of big business in the economy. The Progressives successfully pushed for stiff enforcement of
laws to break up monopolies and to regulate food and drugs.
But the protesters in Seattle face a more difficult challenge. The Progressives could argue that government
ought to contain corporate power. But now that corporate power is transnational, few are arguing for
transnational bodies to regulate them. Indeed one of the themes of the antiglobalization protesters is that
international bodies, such as the WTO, already have too much power.
But the dilemma hasn't dimmed the enthusiasm of protesters. "Economic justice is strongly biblical," says the
Rev. Boonstra. "It's a uniting point and brings together people of faith."
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Revolutionary Women" -- WSJ Dec. 7, 1999)

A Hard YearIn CommoditiesLeads AdviserTo Close Funds
A Hard Year In Commodities Leads Adviser To Close Funds
By Mitchell Pacelle
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,161 words
18 November 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
As an emergency-room physician, David Druz aided victims of trauma. But as a money manager, he couldn't
rescue himself from a string of disasters in the futures market.
On Oct. 29, after 18 years as a commodity-futures trading adviser, Dr. Druz told investors he was shutting
down the four futures funds run by his Tactical Investment Management Corp., which began the year with
more than $100 million, then plunged 38%, including 18% in October. Pulling the plug "was the saddest day
of my life," the 46-year-old doctor-turned-futures trader, speaking from his office in Hawaii, said with a sigh. "It
was like putting your dog to sleep, or pulling your relative off a respirator. October was a rough month for our
industry. Everything in October seemed to reverse. It pushed us over the edge."
Dr. Druz is a veteran commodity-trading adviser, or CTA, one of a risk-taking cadre of money managers who
make directional bets on everything from gold and pork bellies to stock futures and foreign currencies, and
generally keep 25% of their investors' profits. Last year, many commodity-trading advisers thrived in the
market turmoil that pounded hedge funds, with the average CTA gaining 12.2%, according to TASS
Investment Research. Through October of this year, the average CTA is down 1%, according to TASS, while
the average hedge fund is beating the S&P 500.
The major commodity indexes, in fact, have been edging up this year. But futures traders like more
pronounced volatility, because they make money betting on price trends. Major trends have been in short
supply this year. To make matters worse, veteran traders have been hurt by continual "noise," the violent
up-and-down, short-term price movement unconnected to long-term trends. Such noise can plan havoc with
systematic traders such as Dr. Druz, who feed commodity-pricing data into custom-programmed computers,
which determine the prices at which to buy and sell commodities.
"Noise has stayed up for a long time now," Dr. Druz complained. "This will play havoc with a highly leveraged
program." For example, the coffee market in October experienced "a tremendous up and down that came out
of nowhere and made no sense whatsoever," he recalls. "That hurt."
Dr. Druz's Tactical Investment, Henderson, Nev., isn't the only firm that has been battered.
Commodity-trading adviser John W. Henry, owner of the Florida Marlins, saw his largest trading program, the
$900 million Financial & Metals Portfolio, decline by 8.2% in October, and 14% for the year. "It's been a
difficult market for all trend-followers," says Verne Sedlacek, the company's president. "There's been
short-term volatility, but not a lot of long-term trends."
The $2 billion portfolio of Millburn Ridgefield Corp. is down about 5% for the year, mostly because of its poor
performance in October, says Co-Chief Executive George Crapple, who is also chairman of the Managed
Funds Association, a CTA trade group. "It was going along as a sort of boring, nonexceptional year until this
happened," he said, citing an unexpected run-up in energy prices sparked by the Organization of Petroleum
Exporting Countries' decision to continue its production limits. Most systematic traders had been betting on
continued weakness in the energy market.
"The environment is not trending well," noted Christopher Pia, head of trading at veteran trader Louis Bacon's
Moore Capital Management, which had losses of about 2% in October in several funds, although they have
since recovered, and remain marginally up for the year. Many of the most popular global macro bets made by
CTAs -- such as wagers on a strong euro, and against the S&P 500 index and gold -- "didn't pan out," he
said.
The Barclay Futures Index, which tracks 25 of the most liquid futures markets, was down 2.9% during a
"brutal" October, says Sol Waksman of Barclay Trading Group, which tracks the performance of
futures-trading advisers. That was the largest monthly decline since the 1980s, he said. Barclays is based in
Fairfield, Iowa.

--- Page 65 ---
Page 65 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Dr. Druz, whose four funds averaged returns of between 15% and 21% annually over the lives of the funds,
had about 150 investors, ranging from small individual accounts to sizeable institutional ones. He focused on
physical commodities: gold, food, grains, petroleum, metals, and the like. He used up to twice as much
leverage as the industry norm, he said.
Dr. Druz said his firm's quantitative trading system closely tracked such internal trading statistics as the
win/loss ratio, the distribution of wins and losses, and the size of wins in relation to the capital put at risk. The
rules of the four Tactical Trading funds required those statistics to stay within certain boundaries.
After generating a lackluster 13% return last year, he said, the firm faced "slow erosion" this year. "There
were more frequent losses than we would normally have, and the wins we had were smaller."
In October, "all the trades that we were trying to get going reversed," he said. First bets on petroleum and
metals went bad, he explained, then certain agricultural bets soured, and finally interest rates reversed. "The
last part of October was horrific," he recalled. On Oct. 28, the firm's internal trading statistics moved outside
of their mandated limits. The next day, he closed the funds.
"We are all quite in shock, since only a month ago things seemed to be looking up after over a year of very
tough trading conditions for our system," he wrote investors. "But markets have a way of astonishing you, and
they deteriorated precipitously during October. In the more than 18 years that we have been managing these
funds, I have never seen such a prolonged, brutal period for our particular trading style. It is simply time to
say, `Thank you very much, but I don't like the way this game is being played any more. I have already
soundly won many times over. I am going home. It's been fun!'"
Dr. Druz unwound all four funds, and shipped the proceeds to investors last week. "If you are one of the few
investors who joined us in the last couple of years, I feel very badly that we will never be able to recoup all of
your money," he wrote. "Please accept my sincere apology that things turned out this way."
Dr. Druz said he now plans to turn his attention to a systematic stock-trading project, in which he aims to be
able to feed stock prices into a computer, which will churn out buy and sell instructions. He says he expects
some of his former commodities investors to partake.
"This is not a case of burnout," he said. "I love this business."
---
Peter A. McKay contributed to this article.

Crude-Oil Prices Rise for Third Straight Session
Commodities
Crude-Oil Prices Rise for Third Straight Session
By Matthew F. Gallagher
Dow Jones Newswires
838 words
17 November 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C25
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Crude-oil prices climbed for the third straight session at the New York Mercantile Exchange to
set a 34-month high of $25.75 a barrel, as analysts began to express some concern about year-2000 supply
glitches.
The December contract settled just under that high at $25.70 a barrel, with a 57-cent gain.
The Y2K talk was new to the market conversation, but even some skeptics conceded there were signs that
petroleum stockpiling related to the new year was under way.
"We definitely have the sense homeowners are stockpiling heating oil ahead of Y2K," said Bill O'Grady, an
energy analyst with A.G. Edwards in St. Louis. "And I think we'll see a run on gasoline in December."
One piece of data analysts focused on was the sharp drop in U.S. distillate stocks, which include heating oil,
over the past month despite moderate to warm weather. Inventory data from the American Petroleum Institute
released after the Nymex closed yesterday showed distillate stocks down 10 million barrels since the week
ended Oct. 15.
The potential for problems with computer chips in oil and petroleum-product pumping equipment, and with
navigational instruments on tankers are some of the more prominent glitches talked about in the market.
Mr. O'Grady sees current fundamentals and Y2K concerns pushing nearby crude oil as high as $28 a barrel
by the end of the year. Any actual Y2K disruptions in oil-producing nations could push prices well above $30
a barrel, he said.
The backbone of the most recent leg of the 1999 uptrend, though, has been evidence that output cuts by the
Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries are allowing strong demand to eat into world crude supplies,
analysts said.
The API data provided further proof of that trend. In addition to the distillate decline, they showed a
2.29-million-barrel drop in U.S. crude-oil stocks to a tight 308.97 million barrels, and a large 4.95million-barrel
decline in gasoline stocks to 189.46 million barrels.
If these trends continue, energy prices are likely to be extremely vulnerable to any disruption in the product
delivery system at the start of next year, A.G. Edwards's Mr. O'Grady said.
Crude-oil bulls reacted yesterday to an expected meeting in Riyadh today of Saudi Arabian oil minister Ali
Naimi, Mexican minister Luis Tellez and Venezuelan minister Ali Rodriguez. Ministers from these three
countries first met in 1998 and engineered the output cuts that sparked crude's powerful recovery this year.
This time around, some analysts think the ministers are laying the ground work for an extension of those cuts
beyond their phaseout date in March 2000. That speculation was bolstered by comments from several OPEC
ministers supporting just such an extension.
With OPEC now expected to keep its cuts in place past a March 2000 phaseout date, concerns about supply
shortages heading into the first quarter of 2000 are growing by the day, analysts said.
There is some talk in the market that higher oil prices will tempt OPEC members into cheating on their
agreements. But one analyst said the group seems determined to further reduce world stocks, which are still
above levels seen before the 1998 Asian financial crisis sharply reduced world oil demand.

--- Page 67 ---
Page 67 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"We're getting to a point where you hope OPEC does cheat to keep prices from the point where they damage
the U.S. economy," said Tim Evans, an energy analyst with Pegasus Econometrics Group, a market research
company in New York.
And analysts are rethinking the notion that OPEC wants prices no higher than $21 a barrel to prevent new
sources of production from coming on line.
"The big drop in oil prices in 1997 and 1998 spooked a lot of people out of the oil market. There's a window
now for OPEC to raise prices by cutting back production and not risk losing market share," said A.G
Edwards's Mr. O'Grady.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Futures prices rose on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, shrugging off news
of a rise in U.S. official interest rates, traders and analysts said. The December contract rose $2.80 to
$295.30 a troy ounce. Demand for physical gold supported the market despite a sharp rise in the U.S. stock
market, which often reduces the appeal of gold as an investment.
COTTON: Futures prices fell on the New York Cotton Exchange as speculators sold ahead of the expiration
of the December contract, traders said. The contract slipped 1.34 cents to 49.22 cents a pound. First-notice
day for the December contract is Nov. 22, when holders will announce their intention of making or taking
delivery of the physical commodity. Open interest, or the number of contracts outstanding, remained at a
relatively high 23,581 in December as of late Monday.

Nasdaq's Surge Is Fueled by a Mere Few---Some Say Laggards Must Shine for Gains to Continue
Abreast of the Market
Nasdaq's Surge Is Fueled by a Mere Few --- Some Say Laggards Must Shine for Gains to Continue
By E.S. Browning
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,829 words
8 November 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
What comes after Nasdaq 3000: Nasdaq 4000 or Nasdaq 2500?
All the hoopla surrounding Nasdaq 3000 last week masked some interesting aspects of the Nasdaq
Composite Index's rise to stock-market dominance: The advance has been going on for longer than many
investors realize, but of late it has been driven by a surprisingly small group of stocks.
That raises a question: When a stock index becomes so dominated by a handful of names, how long can the
gains continue? "Some of those companies are going to be worth it, but I think the majority aren't going to be
worth it," says David Mead, chief investment officer at Harris Bank. "Ultimately you will have more democracy
in the stock market," he adds, meaning that a wider group of stocks will start to perform better. Then,
investors will realize that "many of the top companies are priced too high." He still likes networking colossus
Cisco Systems, for example, but he has his doubts about software mammoth Microsoft.
The antitrust ruling against Microsoft, announced after markets closed on Friday, throws an additional cloud
over the stock, which as it happens was added to the Dow Jones Industrial Average just last week.
Of course, experienced investors have been warning for years now that the highfliers could fall to earth. Up to
now, it hasn't happened. And certainly, no one was worrying much last week.
In the wake of much-awaited data released Friday showing only modest wage inflation last month, the Dow
Jones Industrial Average jumped 64.84 points, or 0.61%, to 10704.48 on Friday. It still was down 25.38 for
the week. The Nasdaq composite, by contrast, soared 1.5% Friday to finish at a record 3102.29, up 4.6% in
five trading days.
It was an eye-popping week and just the latest of many. The biggest Nasdaq gains have come in the 1990s.
But in the 28 years since it was born, the Nasdaq composite has surpassed the performance of all three of its
major competitors, the Dow Jones industrials, the Standard & Poor's 500stock index and the New York Stock
Exchange composite, in 15 of those yearsmore than half. It looks likely to lead the pack again in 1999.
During that time, the Nasdaq composite has risen more than 3,000%, a gain that is hard to grasp in concrete
terms. It means that if you had been able to invest $10,000 in the index in 1971, it would be worth more than
$310,000 today. A similar investment in the Dow Jones industrials or in the composite index of the New York
Stock Exchange or the S&P 500 would be worth less than half as much.
It seems remarkable that such heady gains are being posted by an index that tracks an entire stock market
and not just a select group of stocks. That is true, but it is a deceptive conclusion to draw. In fact, a coterie of
technology stocks has become so large and powerful that those stocks dominate the Nasdaq composite.
The composite tracks the performance of all 4,845 stocks listed on Nasdaq. But it weighs the gains and
losses of each share of stock, not each company. So a company with 10 million shares weighs 10 times as
much as one with just one million shares.
Since Oct. 8 of last year, when the stock market began to shake off worries about the world financial crisis, it
isn't really the Nasdaq composite that has more than doubled in value. It isn't the Nasdaq that has eclipsed
the Big Board. It is a group of about 65 Nasdaq stocks that account for almost the entire gain. Without those
stocks, the Nasdaq-listed stocks literally have gone nowhere this year.
Just three Nasdaq stocks, Microsoft, Cisco Systems and Intel, alone produced almost 40% of the index's gain
since Oct. 8. Add MCI WorldCom and Sun Microsystems, and the five account for almost half the total gain,
according to Birinyi Associates in Westport, Conn. As long as those five soar (Sun is up more than 400% in
that period), the index is healthy. If they sink, as they did 15 months ago, the index could get clobbered.

--- Page 69 ---
Page 69 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Believers in the Nasdaq composite would counter that encouraging the growth of young companies into
market movers is the whole point of Nasdaq. Even during the Microsoft era, new powerhouses, such as
Qualcomm, have been able to roar into action. But the doubters note that the strength of many of the Nasdaq
stocks has been based on the bull market's bias toward "growth" stocks, which generate big earnings and
profit growth and command high prices. If investors shift back toward "value" stocks, which have slower
earnings growth and much lower prices compared with their earnings, then the Nasdaq highfliers could suffer.
Already, Microsoft, Intel and Dell Computer, for example, have been gaining ground less rapidly than the
overall Nasdaq composite itself. The fastest growers since October 1998 have been stocks such as
Qualcomm, Sun and Cisco.
James Weiss, chief investment officer for stocks at Boston mutual-fund group State Street Research, thinks
out-of-favor stocks could shine more brightly next year. When the world economy began roaring again in the
spring, out-of-favor industrial stocks and small stocks took off, then cooled. "As we move into next year, and
investors get comfortable that we do have global economic growth" and relatively stable interest rates, he
says, "that will encourage investors to begin to broaden their exposure." He says this will benefit a broader
array of issues than the big tech names -- notably industrial stocks and small stocks.
Perhaps, says Michael Weiner, portfolio manager, at Banc One Investment Advisors in Columbus, Ohio. But
like it or not, "the single best predictor of future returns over an extended period is how well a stock has
done," he says. "The stuff that has done well continues to do well and it does well longer than you think."
Laszlo Birinyi of Birinyi Associates says the real objective for everyone except index investors isn't to figure
out which index will do better, but which stocks will do better, and buy those.
"It isn't unusual for a small group of stocks to be so dominant," he says. "In 1965, AT&T and General Motors
together accounted for almost 15% of the S&P. In the past three or four years, 25% to 30% of the buying on
the New York Stock Exchange has been concentrated on the Dow 30," and that continues. He adds: "This
market is more narrow than it has been in 40 years, and yet from the middle of January to now we have had a
nice move. The answer is that you have to pick stocks well. If we are having a bear market for the majority of
stocks, fine, buy the minority."
---

Gold Declines on Investor-Friendly Labor Figures
Commodities
Gold Declines on Investor-Friendly Labor Figures
By Janet Whitman
Dow Jones Newswires
502 words
8 November 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Gold prices fell Friday, following the release of U.S. October employment data, which eased investor
concerns about inflation.
Market participants viewed the data as a good opportunity to sell gold, noting that the market has been
struggling since its surprise rebound in late September and early October, sparked by news that 15 European
central banks intend to limit their gold sales and lending over the next five years.
The nearby December future on the Comex Division of the New York Mercantile Exchange slipped $2.70 to
$291.00 a troy ounce. While gold has eased considerably since its Oct. 5 spike to $339.00 an ounce, it
remains well above the 20-year low of about $253 it held close to during much of the past summer.
Traders focused on news of a rise of just one cent in average hourly earnings of nonfarm workers to $13.37
in October, viewed as proof that there is little wage inflation in the economy.
Evidence that inflation is at bay diminishes the likelihood that investors will purchase the precious metal as
an inflation hedge. The data also contributed to a rally in Treasury bonds, an alternative investment to gold.
Gold "did fall right after that number," said Robin Alssid, senior dealer with Mitsubishi International Corp. in
New York. "It was probably more of an excuse to sell. {Gold has} sort of been in a downtrend."
The gold market has "quieted down" since prices skyrocketed at the end of September and the beginning of
last month, said William O'Neill, chief futures strategist with Merrill Lynch & Co. in New York.
Lease rates, which reflect the cost of borrowing gold, have returned to typical levels, reducing some of the
liquidity concerns in the market, analysts said. "Tightness is no longer in play, so the market has taken on a
defensive tone," Mr. O'Neill said.
In other commodity markets:
CRUDE OIL: Futures reversed a morning rally at Nymex and ended with a moderate loss as traders booked
profits ahead of the weekend. The December contract slipped 14 cents to $23 a barrel. The morning rally was
sparked by news that an emissions release shut a 67,000-barrel-a-day catalytic cracking unit at the Deer
Park, Texas, refinery of Shell Deer Park Refining Co., a joint venture of Royal Dutch Petroleum Co.'s Shell Oil
Co. and Mexico's Petroleos Mexicanos. But one trader said the market was knocked off its highs by reports
that compliance by the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries with output-cut commitments had
fallen to 80%.
COCOA: Futures fell to a new 7 1/2-year low on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange on speculative selling,
traders said. The December contract fell $6 to $830 a metric ton, after hitting a low of $825. Weakness in the
London market, which was pressured by producer selling, also weighed on New York prices, analysts added.

A Special Weekly Report FromThe Wall Street Journal'sCapital Bureau
Washington Wire
A Special Weekly Report From The Wall Street Journal's Capital Bureau
By John Harwood
924 words
5 November 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Corrections & Amplifications
REP. PHIL CRANE (R., Ill.) gave $38,000 to a Republican fund-raising drive, not $4,000 as reported in
Friday's Washington Wire. The incorrect information had been supplied by Mr. Crane's office. (WSJ -- Nov. 8,
1999)
GOP LEADERS WRITE big checks to protect their slender majority.
House Speaker Hastert, Majority Leader Armey and Whip DeLay kick in $50,000 each for a war chest to
bolster Republicans in 10 targeted races. Six shaky incumbents and four candidates for promising open seats
will get $100,000 apiece. One beneficiary: Rep. George Nethercutt of Washington, who faces a threatened $1
million attack from term-limit advocates after flip-flopping on his pledge to serve no more than six years.
The campaign profits from jockeying among senior Republicans for prime committee posts. Reps. Oxley of
Ohio and Tauzin of Louisiana -- dueling for the Commerce chairmanship that is coming open because of
GOP caucus term limits -- give $30,000 and $20,000, respectively. California Rep. Thomas, seeking to head
the tax-writing Ways and Means panel, tosses in $20,000.
Thomas's rival, Crane of Illinois, donates $4,000 so far, but an aide says, "There will probably be more."
GORE LABORS to capitalize on AFL-CIO endorsement in his White House bid.
The vice president uses union help in gathering petition signatures to qualify for New York's primary ballot.
Gore canvasses door to door with labor leaders in New Hampshire, where he wil address an AFL-CIO dinner
this weekend. Draft union fliers for key primary states contrast Gore's record with that of GOP front-runner
Bush, but don't mention Democratic rival Bradley.
Meeting with Teamsters leaders, who have withheld their endorsement, Gore says the administration hasn't
given labor and environmental concerns sufficient priority in trade talks. The White House dispatches a
deputy trade representative to India in a vain attempt to persuade New Delhi to support including labor issues
in WTO talks.
SOME OFFICIALS say Albanians inflated casualties to spur NATO action.
One American in the region now pegs civilian deaths in Kosovo last spring at no more than 2,500, blaming a
"disinformation campaign" by ethnic Albanian leaders for much higher earlier estimates. But another official
says that scores of suspected mass graves have yet to be examined, as investigations wind down amid bitter
weather.
The administration predicts that winter suffering in the region won't be as bad as feared. Since not all
refugees have returned, Kosovars won't need as much housing as once expected; some families also are
doubling up. "Winter will be bad, but not that bad," says one U.S. military officer back from Kosovo duty.
Every European head of state except Milosevic is invited to a meeting on emerging democracies during
Clinton's coming trip to Turkey.
FADED GOLD MINE? Banking lobbyists wave goodbye to a decades-long meal ticket with the expected
enactment of new financial-services legislation. One consolation: Wall Street lobbyist Tom Quinn jokes that
he asked lawmakers not to replace the Depression-era Glass-Steagall Act until his three sons finished
college; all now have.

BOOBY PRIZE: U.S. negotiators in Bonn fail to delay final talks on the divisive Kyoto climate-change treaty.
They sought to give Clinton's successor breathing room by pushing talks into 2001, but Saudi Arabia and
other opponents insisted they begin Nov. 13, 2000 -- six days after the U.S. election.
SUPPLY-SIDE DEMOCRAT? Veteran Kemp booster David Smick hosts a $1,000-a-head fund-raiser for
Bradley's presidential bid, touting the candidate's knowledge of international economics and "ability to
transcend traditional politics" to would-be donors. A new Center for Responsive Politics study shows the
ex-Knick reaping $209,000 from partners at Goldman Sachs, more than twice either Bush or Gore.
HOUSE BOUND: Clintonites brace for flak over the nomination of Amy Comstock to head the Office of
Government Ethics. The career civil servant headed the White House ethics program when the First Family
tapped a top fund-raiser to back their $1.35 million home mortgage, a deal later scotched amid criticism.
A NEW BATTLE LOOMS over ways to prevent racial discrimination in lending.
A 1974 Federal Reserve regulation bars banks from asking borrowers about race, gender or nationality.
Backing a Fed proposal to change that rule, the White House and civil-rights groups now argue that banks
should collect such data to detect patterns of bias. Changing the rule will "allow us to monitor the progress we
are making," writes Union Bank of California.
The Fed says it still could pull back the proposal after considering public comments. Leading bank trade
groups will file a protest next week arguing against the change. Bush's top economic adviser, former Fed
governor Lawrence Lindsey, submits two missives attacking the shift.
Writing to Greenspan, House GOP Leader Armey says the proposal "flies in the face of our efforts to create a
more `colorblind' society."
MINOR MEMOS: Alpha wonk? Former Congressional Budget Office chief Robert Reischauer says he has
"kibitzed with" Bradley's team, but "I'm not a Naomi Wolf." . . . Satirist Al Franken says his dream 2000
scenario "is starting to happen" as ATM fees, centerpiece of his faux-campaign book, gain as a national
issue. . . . After Gore's campaign moves into a former Nashville medical office, controversial fundraiser Peter
Knight works behind a door marked "Clean Linen."

ASIA/PACIFIC
Mitsubishi Heavy
Joins Long List
Of Struggling Firms
Japan's heavy-machinery and shipbuilding companies suffered big losses in the fiscal first half ended Sept.
30, and analysts said the strong yen will continue to weigh on businesses in the second half. Mitsubishi
Heavy Industries said that it suffered an unconsolidated pretax loss of 29.74 billion yen ($285.7 million), the
first parent half-year loss since the company was established in a merger more than three decades ago.
Mitsubishi Heavy blamed the loss on increased competition at home and abroad, the poor Japanese
economy and reduced exports to Asian economies. Reflecting the poor demand environment, its sales
plunged by nearly 18% to 916.36 billion yen. A strong yen can hurt results both because of currency
translation of sales made abroad and because of reduced imports by other countries.
If misery loves company, Mitsubishi has got plenty. Kawasaki Heavy Industries last week posted its first
interim pretax loss in 12 years. With Asian economies back on track toward recovery, Japanese machinery
makers and shipbuilders find themselves confronting increasingly competitive rivals in neighboring countries
such as South Korea, where the cheap won allows exporters to sell products more inexpensively overseas. In
response to the conditions gripping the industry, Mitsubishi Heavy also unveiled the details of a four-year
restructuring plan. It said it would reduce group staff by 7,000 to 64,000 by March 2004 through attrition. At
the parent company, payrolls will be trimmed to 35,000 from the current 40,000.
Taiwan Quake Causes Little Damage
Taiwan's latest big earthquake, a 6.9-magnitude tremor centered off the island's southern coast, caused little
damage and, despite persistent aftershocks, isn't expected to disrupt the island's closely watched shipments
of computer chips and other electronics, industry executives said. That's a very different story from the
island's last major earthquake, on Sept. 21, which left more than 2,400 people dead and 100,000 homeless
and shut down the island's computer-chip industry. Then, prices for computer chips soared around the world
as the disruption cut supplies just as the industry was heading into its peak season. But the epicenter of
September's 7.6 quake was the middle of the island, while the latest one was in the ocean, far from high-tech
manufacturing areas in northern Taiwan.
Mou Enters Plea in Fraud Case
A well-known Chinese entrepreneur pleaded not guilty to charges that he defrauded a state-run bank of $75
million. Mou Qizhong, head of Land Economic Group and once one of China's richest men, told the Wuhan
Intermediate People's Court he had legitimately borrowed the money to launch satellites in Kazakstan. If the
loan was not legal, Mr. Mou said, that was the fault of the Bank of China, among others, as the bank issued
the letter of credit. The one-day trial was notable for its lack of witnesses. One co-defendant, Mr. Mou's
former chief financial officer, turned state's witness. A verdict is expected in eight days, and Mr. Mou could
receive a life sentence, which would likely result in his serving 10 years in prison.
Japan Forecasts Tax Shortfall
Japan said its tax revenues will fall more than a trillion yen ($9.6 billion) short of the budgeted amount in the
current fiscal year, forcing the government to use funds from a supplemental budget to fill the gap. Reflecting
the sluggish business environment, the government recently had to return 400 billion yen in prepaid corporate
taxes to companies whose earnings fell short of expectations in the past fiscal year. Meanwhile, Japan's

--- Page 75 ---
Page 75 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
economy grew by a real 0.1% in the July-September quarter compared with the previous period, according to
a report released by Nomura Research Institute. Japan's Economic Planning Agency isn't expected to
release its preliminary findings for July-September GDP until early December.
BRIEFLY:
-- Morgan Stanley Capital International has delayed the return of Malaysia to its benchmark indexes by three
months until May 31, 2000, to avoid market uncertainty over possible leap-year computer glitches. Market
watchers said the move could prove troublesome for anyone betting on an MSCI-inspired market rally in
Malaysia at the start of next year.
-- Citigroup said it acquired 19% of Nikko Beans, a Japanese Internet brokerage firm that began operations
last month. Specific financial terms weren't disclosed. Citigroup said the acquisition is part of an alliance with
the Tokyo firm.

EUROPE/AFRICA
Anglo American-Tarmac Talks End
Anglo American, a London mining and financial conglomerate, announced that talks about a possible
takeover of British building-materials maker Tarmac ended Friday after Tarmac rejected an offer of 550 pence
($9.01) a share, or about GBP 1.13 billion ($1.86 billion). Tarmac had previously rejected an offer of 535
pence a share, or about GBP 1.1 billion. Among other things, Anglo American is the parent of Anglogold of
South Africa, the world's biggest gold-mining company. Anglogold recently bid for Ghana's Ashanti
Goldfields, Africa's third-largest gold producer, and Australian group Anaconda Nickel. Anglogold said it is
interested in Ashanti Goldfields' biggest operating mine, Obuasi, in Ghana and a promising new mine, Geita,
in Tanzania. The Ghanaian gold miner remains in takeover talks with its biggest shareholder, Lonmin, a U.K.
mining group. Separately, Gold Fields, South Africa's second-largest gold miner, said it will pay a 62%
market premium to acquire the shares it doesn't own in junior producer St. Helena Gold Mines. Gold Fields
currently holds 46% of St. Helena's equity.
Internet Bank Pushes Rate Envelope
Losing money has never been so popular. First there was EGG, the internet-banking unit of British insurer
Prudential PLC, which offered unusually high interest rates on savings accounts. Others followed. Now
First-E, a recently launched Internet bank, has gone one step further. Its new interest rate on savings
accounts, an aggressive 6.25%, is higher than the 6.00% being offered by EGG and well over the current
base rate of 5.25%. First-E -- the result of a joint venture between France's Banque d'Escompte and Enba, a
technology group -- isn't saying how much it expects to lose by aiming so high. Like other Internet-based
groups, First-E's strategy involves luring customers with attractive prices before trying to cross-sell other,
more lucrative banking services. Analysts warn that the arrival of high savings rates has brought with it a new
investor -- the "rate tart," as one described the breed -- who monitors weekly tables and switches his money
back and forth depending on which bank is offering the best returns.
BAA Results Cheer Analysts
Fiscal first-half earnings at BAA, the British operator of airports, showed that the deterioration of retail sales
because of the end of duty-free sales in Europe has stopped. BAA said its pretax profit before exceptional
items for the six months ended Sept. 30 was GBP 306 million ($503.5 million), compared with GBP 307
million in the first half of last year. Analysts had braced themselves for gloomy first-half figures after the
company issued a profit warning in early October. Duty- and tax-free shopping was scrapped in Europe at the
end of June, but BAA pledged to maintain duty-free prices for travelers within the European Union.

THE AMERICAS
Uruguayan Center Left Advances
Uruguay's center-left Popular Front increased its share in Congress in elections on Sunday, becoming the
body's biggest political force, preliminary results showed. The Popular Front should increase its standing in
the 99-seat lower-house Chamber of Deputies to 40 from 31 seats and its forces to 12 senators instead of
nine in the 30-seat upper house. "The Popular Front is now absolutely necessary for laws which might require
the passage of two-thirds of the Congress, like naming judicial powers," said Oscar Botinelli, a political
analyst with Factum pollsters. The Popular Front's candidate Tabare Vazquez won 39% of the presidential

--- Page 76 ---
Page 76 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
vote and, since none of the four candidates won a majority, will go to a runoff vote Nov. 28 against Jorge
Batlle of the center-right, incumbent Colorado party.
Globo Unit Sets Brazilian Internet Launch
Brazilian pay-television provider Globo Cabo expects to launch its Virtua Internet service provider within the
next two weeks, pending regulatory approval. It will be Globo Cabo's first major project launch since the
company, a unit of media concern Globo Comunicacoes & Participacoes, sold an 11.5% stake to Microsoft, of
Redmond, Wash., in August for $126 million. Globo Cabo's two-way cable-modem service allows cable
customers to access the Internet at high speeds without tying up their phone lines. The company expects
Virtua to attract 50,000 subscribers in the first 12 months.
Pemex Official Is Sanctioned
Mexico banned an official of state-owned oil monopoly Petroleos Mexicanos from holding any public post for
10 years, after discovering contract irregularities. A watchdog agency said Miguel Angel Fuentes was found
to have simulated tender processes on contracts for the leasing of machinery and equipment at a Pemex
refinery in 1993 and 1994. There was no response from Pemex.

View / Food:Made in the U.S.A.: Japanese Beef
WEEKEND JOURNAL
View / Food: Made in the U.S.A.: Japanese Beef
By Susan G. Hauser
1,098 words
29 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
W8
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Redmond, Ore. -- As cows go, Wagyu are not much to look at. They're smaller than your ordinary cow, with a
narrow snout and a hide in basic black, rusty brown or variations thereof. But there's gold in them thar cows:
They're the Japanese breed that becomes Kobe beef. And if it weren't for the plentiful piles of manure and the
swarms of flies, I'd swear I was at Tiffany's.
But in fact I was surrounded by snow-capped mountains and rolling range at the central Oregon ranch owned
by R.L. and Susie Freeborn. Their company in nearby Bend (about 170 miles east of Portland), Kobe Beef
America Inc., is the only company to raise superior Japanese beef strictly for the American palate, but at
prices roughly half of the $120 or so that a pound of top-grade Kobe beef from Japan commands on the retail
market. The Freeborns had invited me to spend a weekend at the ranch to get a feel for the way of the
Wagyu in America, and by Sunday I had herded, inoculated and even artificially inseminated some of these
bovine gold mines.
Mr. Freeborn ("just call me R.L.") usually performs the insemination duty himself, with a priceless pipette of
what you might call liquid gold, and he takes his job seriously. "As Dr. Laura says, `I am my kids' dad,'" he
deadpans, eyes twinkling from under his cowboy hat. "Once I did 1,000 cows in one day. I called the
operation `The Love Boat.'"
He is on the board of directors of the American Wagyu Association, which now includes 55 ranchers devoted
to finding the perfect recipe, through genetics and feeding, for the heavily marbled, rich and flavorful beef that
is in such high demand in Japan. Most of the members are dedicated to propagating the breed; only about a
half-dozen raise the beef for export to Japan, and only Mr. Freeborn raises Wagyu specifically for the U.S.
market. Wagyu breeding has also taken off in Australia, New Zealand, Canada and Europe.
Only a small percentage of Kobe beef, named for the city where the cattle were first bred 170 years ago,
reaches the pinnacle of Japan's lofty standards for the quantity and quality of intramuscular fat. There, the
highest grade of beef, a rarely achieved 12, is so smooth and rich it is likened to foie gras, or even butter, and
is usually served in thin slices that have been rapidly cooked in broth.
"Our customers don't want foie gras," says Mr. Freeborn, who caters to folks just wanting to sink their
choppers into a great steak. So with a Wagyu-Black Angus mix, raised without hormones and fed on barley,
he strives for a range of marbling that would rate from five to eight in Japan (still acceptable to most
Japanese diners). Measured by USDA standards his beef is off the charts, well above the top prime rating. By
comparison, normal prime cuts would be ranked no higher than three on the Japanese scale.
Although he treats his cattle lovingly, he draws the line at the sort of pampering some Japanese Wagyu
ranchers shower on their precious livestock. Personally, he thinks the massages (thought to relax the cattle --
not, as commonly believed, to work more fat into muscle) and the sake rubs (thought to beautify their hides)
are a waste of time and energy.
"There's absolutely no scientific data that shows that a 120-pound man rubbing a 2,000-pound steer makes
any difference," he opines. "But if it makes the guy who's rubbing the steer feel any better, I'm all for it."
A fourth-generation cattle rancher with experience exporting high-grade American beef to Japan, Mr.
Freeborn was invited to a 1992 Wagyu symposium in Texas by Washington State University's Animal
Sciences Department and then asked to participate in a study of Wagyu genetics that WSU had begun in
1989. The Pullman, Wash., scientists had determined that the propensity for high marbling was in the
Japanese cows' genes, and with cross-breeding they hoped to produce top-quality beef in America that would
fetch top dollar in Japan.

--- Page 78 ---
Page 78 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Freeborn sent his first Wagyu beef to market five years ago. Although he exports some beef to Japan
under the Wabei Beef label, he concentrates on the American market, where Kobe Beef America products
are sold to high-end restaurants and even to the White House through a network of 17 distributors. One of
them sells individual steaks online, for about $28 for an 8-ounce filet mignon and $35 for a 12-ounce New
York steak, at www.farm-2-market.com. Authentic Kobe beef from Japan is available for a king's ransom at
many U.S. restaurants, but an American Kobe steak will set you back only about 50 bucks.
With his beef commanding such high prices, Mr. Freeborn can afford to keep a relatively small herd of several
hundred cattle. They seemed like a whole bunch to me when I was in the middle of them, though, helping to
separate older calves from their mothers for inoculations and weaning. I was congratulating myself for being
such a good sport as I courageously (and somewhat squeamishly) administered the shots in the calves'
leathery necks, when Mr. Freeborn, after explaining the mechanics of artificial insemination, declared, "By
golly, that would be a good learning experience!"
He outfitted me in overalls and a plastic glove that extended to my shoulder. With a gulp, I followed Mr.
Freeborn's careful instructions until my arm was strangled from the elbow down by an exasperated cow's
internal muscles.
When I yelped, Mr. Freeborn nodded knowingly. "That's what we call `the wave.' It's our on-the-ranch
surfing."
For our last night together, R.L. and Susie cooked up an extraordinary spread. Mrs. Freeborn, who was a
California surfer girl until she met her future husband at a polo match (his previous claim to fame was being
Omar Sharif's stand-in for polo scenes in the 1975 film "Funny Lady"), went along with the joke of serving me
a potato-sized rock and watching me try to cut into it.
But all was forgiven when the steak came off the barbecue and I got to bite into this treasure trove of
tenderness and flavor. It made my toes curl up in my cowboy boots.

Letters to the Editor:The High-Tax Monster Will Get Us
Letters to the Editor: The High-Tax Monster Will Get Us
386 words
27 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
My friend Brian Wesbury ("Have No Fear, Inflation Isn't Here," editorial page, Oct. 21) argues that "This
mixture of tight money and low taxes, promoted by Robert Mundell, winner of this year's Nobel Prize in
economics, has worked." I certainly can't argue with the tight money part of Mr. Westbury's argument. And, as
a fellow supply-sider, I certainly have no quarrel with his belief in the efficacy of low taxes in promoting
non-inflationary growth. But how can "low taxes" be working in an environment of high taxes?
Federal taxes currently consume about 23% of our nation's GDP, a record. Not a peacetime record, an
all-time record. That's right -- a greater share of a much larger GDP is being spent feeding the federal
spending machine than was spent defeating Hitler, Mussolini and Tojo. How can this be construed as a
low-tax environment? One could argue legitimately that marginal tax rates are way down from the 70% that
prevailed in 1980, but they are way up from the 28% that was instituted as the top rate in the Tax
Simplification and Reduction Act of 1988.
As a supply-sider, I agree that low taxes are conducive to, if not essential for, strong economic growth without
inflation. But also as a supply-sider, I marvel at how well our economy has done under a regime of high
taxes. Can the boost to the economy from lowering tax rates be so powerful and so long lasting that we are
still enjoying the residue of the Reagan tax cuts of the 1980s? Perhaps. But one has to think that the tailwind
the economy has been enjoying from these tax reductions cannot last forever. It eventually has to be calmed
by the stultifying effects of the Bush/Clinton high-tax regime.
Taxes are at an all-time high. If one combines that fact with the supposition that gold's being up 18% over the
past three months is evidence that the Fed has perhaps been lax in its husbandry of the money supply of
late, one can easily see why the argument that stagflation could be imminent is gaining currency among
some of us supply-siders.
Mark M. Quinn
Naperville, Ill.

Dollar Sinks Against Yen on WeaknessIn Markets but Ends Flat Against Euro
Foreign Exchange
Dollar Sinks Against Yen on Weakness In Markets but Ends Flat Against Euro
By Umberto Torresan
Dow Jones Newswires
609 words
26 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar lost ground against the yen as market declines in the U.S. caused investors to put
their money to work elsewhere.
Late yesterday in New York, the dollar was trading at 105.32 yen, down from 105.71 yen late Friday. The
euro was trading at $1.0675, little changed from $1.0692 at the end of last week.
Because the euro traded in a tight range against the dollar, most of the action took place in the dollar/yen
arena, according to analysts. "Three fundamental factors have taken the dollar down" yesterday, said Jeremy
Fand, foreign currency strategist at FleetBoston.
First, the realization that the Bank of Japan's monetary policy meeting, scheduled Wednesday, will not result
in changes in monetary policy helped stabilize expectations on trading levels of the yen, he said.
Second, talk of "pork barreling" agreements on the next fiscal-stimulus package by Tokyo's government
fueled expectations the yen will receive a fiscal boost soon.
Third, September's trade numbers for Japan, which showed the national surplus shrinking by about 10%,
confirmed that Japanese consumers are buying more imported goods, something good for the economy
there.
As a result, the dollar broke support of 105.45 yen on continued buying interest of Japanese currency ahead
of Wednesday's BOJ policy meeting. The next support is seen at 104.70 yen.
Meanwhile, the euro continued to trade in a tight range against the U.S. currency. Earlier in the day,
comments by European Central Bank Chief Economist Otmar Issing supported the euro, when he said he
saw risks changing from the downside to the upside.
Mr. Issing's comments have refocused attention on the possibility of a half-percentage-point interest-rate
increase on Nov. 4 and placed greater emphasis on the release of euro-zone M3 money supply data due out
later this week. But the euro's muted reaction gave weight to speculation that there is no broad-based
demand for the single currency ahead of the year end.
Adding to the mixed euro-zone scenario were recent inflation data coming out of Germany, which barely
showed any pickup in consumer prices other than the volatile energy sector, FleetBoston's Mr. Fand said.
In the short term, the euro is expected to trade range-bound against the dollar, with a bias for an upward
move on technical rebound. Resistance is pegged at $1.0720, and support is pegged at $1.0650, according
to analysts.
Yesterday was a better day for sterling and the Canadian dollar, which posted decent gains throughout the
day, said Andrew Busch, foreign currency trading director at Bank of Montreal in Chicago.
The British pound gained new ground on better prospects for the United Kingdom economy, Mr. Busch said.
Good economic data showing a narrowing trade deficit in both August and September pointed to a strong
recovery of U.K. manufacturing activities and exports. Late yesterday, sterling was quoted at $1.6640, up
from from $1.6567 Friday.
Meanwhile, the Canadian dollar was supported by gold-related sales of Australian dollars against the
Canadian currency. The two currencies are sensitive to price swings of commodities such as gold, because
large parts of the Canadian and Australian economies rely on raw commodity exports. As a result, the two
currencies are often sold against each other in line with gold-price fluctuations.

The Mexican peso and Brazilian real both weakened against the U.S. dollar late yesterday, mostly tracking
losses in U.S. stock and bond markets.

Ode to a Scapegoat
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
Ode to a Scapegoat
741 words
26 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Somewhere in dim memory, a representative of the City of London came to explain his plans to undermine
the dominance of Wall Street. Playfully asked how the U.S. could best protect its leadership in the world of
finance, his reply was instant: "Repeal Glass-Steagall."
In its wisdom, the U.S. government is finally on the verge of repealing the 1933 law that splintered the
business of finance, separating commercial banking from investment banking from insurance. The law
prohibited money-saving combinations, entrenched various monopolies and created a complex skein of
regulatory issues that impeded change. The splintering spirit of the 1930s also produced disasters-in-waiting,
such as the Savings & Loan and Farm Credit systems; the heart of financial security, we repeatedly and
painfully learned, is diversification.
In the dead of night Friday morning, our solons finally came to agreement on reversing the mistakes of their
predecessors. The final dispute came down to the "sunshine" provision of the Community Reinvestment Act,
under which regulators review whether banks have been lending enough in poor neighborhoods. Even
frivolous protests on CRA grounds can delay mergers or expansions, and banks have taken to paying
"community groups" to go away. Senator Gramm succeeded in inserting a provision that banks will have to
reveal such payments, and the groups will have to reveal how they spend the money. The Administration had
been threatening a veto to preserve shakedown opportunities for its political allies, until it apparently dawned
on someone that scuttling banking reform was no way to run for Senator from the state of New York.
This overtly political denouement to Glass-Steagall is entirely fitting, since it was originally cut from the same
cloth. It was passed in the depths of the Great Depression, in the wake of the great stock market crash 70
years ago this week. As Ron Chernow wrote in his "The House of Morgan": "Ultimately, Glass-Steagall was
as much an attempt to punish the banking industry as it was a measure to reform it. It was Main Street
striking back at Wall Street, rounding out the 1929 disaster."
More pointedly, it was the politicians making banks the scapegoats for the Depression. Franklin Roosevelt
proclaimed, "The money changers have fled from their high seats in the temple of our civilization. We may
now restore that temple to the ancient truths." Senate Banking Committee hearings under counsel Ferdinand
Pecora looked in the nooks and crannies of banking for abuses, and predictably found some, such as the
House of Morgan cutting politicians and business allies into hot new issues. The point of Glass-Steagall was
not to make banks safe for depositors, but to limit their political power.
At this remove there is still debate over the true causes of the crash and the Depression. Mistakes in Federal
Reserve policy are now the most frequently cited cause, following Milton Friedman and Anna Schwartz in
their "Monetary History of the United States." The Smoot-Hawley Tariff and Hoover's 1932 tax increase surely
did not help. And international finance was unable to digest the issues of war debts and reparations, or the
breakdown of the gold standard under wartime inflation. Without deciding the relative merit of these
explanations, they are not the province of private bankers but of politicians.
Events are the ultimate educator, we suppose, and time the universal solvent. Over recent decades industry
after industry, starting with airlines back during the Carter Administration, has been deregulated to the great
benefit of consumers. The illogic of a splintered financial industry has become increasingly manifest, leading
regulators and legislators to forge loopholes in the barriers erected in 1933. Simple repeal has been
repeatedly promised, but frustrated; the huge Citigroup merger was simply done assuming the law finally
would be repealed.
The declining market share of commercial banks gives lie to the fear of inordinate political power. They now
hold less than a quarter of U.S. financial assets, compared with more than half in the 1950s. Corporations
issue securities directly to the markets; mortgages are securitized. Households invest in mutual funds and
401(k) accounts. And competition now reaches across borders, from Wall Street to the City and beyond. In
the face of this, Glass-Steagall's splinters became unsustainable.

--- Page 83 ---
Page 83 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The remarkable thing, indeed, is that it took so long to perish. It takes a lot of time to dissolve a good
scapegoating.

Compiled by David I. Oyama

EUROPE
Britain's Medeva
Confirms Talks
On Possible Deal
British pharmaceuticals company Medeva confirmed it is in discussions that may lead to an offer for the
company. It is the third time in recent months Medeva has been involved in merger talks. Medeva rejected
the previous bids, saying they didn't fairly value its drugs under development. A report yesterday in the
Sunday Times in London said the new suitor is Celltech Chiroscience of Britain.
In London trading Friday, Medeva shares closed up 17% at 166.5 pence ($2.79). Medeva said it will make a
further announcement to the London Stock Exchange when an outcome to the discussions becomes clear.
Boeing Chief Sees Deeper Europe Ties
The chairman of U.S. plane maker Boeing, Phil Condit, predicts the prospective transformation of European
aircraft consortium Airbus Industrie into a single company will be good for competition between the two
companies, and he expects Boeing will deepen its European ties in both civilian and military operations. Mr.
Condit also said Friday in Brussels that he expects to see greater cooperation between aerospace companies
on both sides of the Atlantic.
Deutsche Telekom, MediaOne Deal Set
Deutsche Telekom of Germany confirmed it will purchase cellular-phone assets in Poland, Hungary and
Russia from MediaOne Group of the U.S. for around $2 billion. The deal follows Deutsche Telekom's
purchase in August of One2One, the United Kingdom mobile-phone operator, from Cable & Wireless of the
U.K. and MediaOne, which is being acquired by AT&T of the U.S. The deal will double the German
company's 22.5% stake in Polska Telefonia Cyfrowa, Poland's largest cellular-phone operator, to 45%.
Deutsche Telekom will also acquire MediaOne's 49% stakes in Westel Radiotelefon's Westel 450 and Westel
900 units, which control about two-thirds of Hungary's cellular market. Hungary's Magyar Tavkozlesi Vallalat,
or Matav, in which Deutsche Telekom and Ameritech of the U.S. own a combined 59.58% stake, has an
option to buy Deutsche Telekom's Westel stakes for $885 million during a 15-month period from next July.
Deutsche Telekom will also acquire a majority stake in Russian Telecommunications Development.
Krugerrand Exports to U.S. Resume
South Africa has resumed exports of gold Krugerrands to the U.S. The Rand Refinery said exports started
this month with a millennium coin, the Krugerrand 2000. More than 15 million ounces of Krugerrands were
exported to the U.S. from 1975 to 1985, before international sanctions against the former apartheid
government barred trade with South Africa. Rand Refinery is owned by the country's main gold-mining
companies.
BRIEFLY:
-- German electronics company Siemens confirmed it has sold its Nixdorf Retail & Banking Systems GmbH
unit to U.S. investors Kohlberg Kravis Roberts and GS Capital Partners, the private equity arm of Goldman
Sachs Group, for 1.44 billion marks ($795.6 million).

--- Page 85 ---
Page 85 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
-- European personal-computer sales surged 19.3% in the third quarter from a year earlier to 6.46 million
units, according to market-research firm Context.
-- Italy's government said it may boost the 12 billion euro ($12.97 billion) initial public offering of state electric
utility Enel because of heavy demand.
-- Telepizza said it plans to sell a 30% stake held by its chairman, Leopoldo Fernandez Pujals. It said Mr.
Fernandez will sell a 5.4% stake in Telepizza to fellow Spanish fast-food company TeleChef and will sell
another 25% in a public offering.
-- The European Commission blasted France for allowing rendering plants to sell sewage sludge to
animal-feed producers and ordered the government to crack down on anyone still engaged in the practice.
Britain's agriculture minister resisted calls to retaliate against France's continuing ban on British beef imports.
-- Swedish telecommunications group Telefon AB L.M. Ericsson reported pretax profit for the first nine
months of 1999 topped analysts' estimates. Pretax profit fell 35% from a year earlier to 7.91 billion Swedish
kroner ($973.3 million), above the 7.53 billion kroner forecast by analysts surveyed by SIX Market Estimates.
-- French industrial production rose 1.5% in July and August from June and was up 1.7% from output in the
year-earlier two months, the government said.
-- Britain's gross domestic product at constant market prices rose a seasonally adjusted 0.9% in the third
quarter from the second quarter, and was 1.8% higher from a year before, according to preliminary
government estimates Friday.
