Green With Envy:Here's Why Bad StuffOften Hits Good TimesLike Scam-Rich 1990s---This Decade's Spate of FraudRecalls Patterns, PlayersOf Othe...
Green With Envy: Here's Why Bad Stuff Often Hits Good Times Like Scam-Rich 1990s --- This
Decade's Spate of Fraud Recalls Patterns, Players Of Other Golden Ages --- Isaac Newton:
Twice-Bitten
By E.S. Browning
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
2,048 words
14 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Future generations no doubt will look back on the 1990s as a golden age of prosperity in the U.S. -- and
probably also as a golden age of financial fraud.
It was in this decade that a Canadian gold-mining company called Bre-X Minerals Ltd. saw its stock value
soar to $4.5 billion, until its miracle Indonesian mine was exposed as a hoax. Fraudulent trading brought
down two eminent investment banks in succession, Kidder, Peabody & Co. and Baring Bros.
Just this fall, reclusive, would-be insurance mogul Martin Frankel and garrulous market guru Martin
Armstrong were arrested within days of each other, each charged by federal prosecutors with swindling
investors out of hundreds of millions of dollars. A San Antonio group called InverWorld Inc. in August was
accused by the Securities and Exchange Commission in a civil lawsuit of defrauding Mexican clients of the
bulk of their $475 million in investments.
And while these scandals have gotten the big headlines, regulators say the most devastating fraud for
ordinary people has been the long-running epidemic of small-stock scams. Mushrooming faster than
prosecutors can shut them down, they feature pushy telephone and Internet salesmen stealing billions from
gullible investors each year.
The amounts at stake today certainly dwarf past swindles, both because of inflation and because technology
helps money flow at a rate never seen before. But some of the characters at the center of allegations today
bring to mind characters of the past. Mr. Armstrong, for example, is accused of using a method popularized
by Charles Ponzi, whose pyramid scheme based in Boston took in $15 million in 1920, equivalent to about
$125 million today. When the fugitive Mr. Frankel was found this summer hiding in a Hamburg, Germany,
luxury hotel with a cache of diamonds and cash, he looked like a character in a novel by Agatha Christie or
Dashiell Hammett. (Mr. Armstrong denies any wrongdoing, and Mr. Frankel's lawyer says his client is
contesting the extradition charges against him.)
Financial historians don't find it surprising that so many swindles and alleged swindles are blossoming now.
Periods of affluence, they say, are precisely when ordinary people and sophisticated investors alike, seeing
friends and relatives striking it rich, put aside their good sense and fall victim to greed or envy. But the
historians are intrigued by the parallels they see between this era's frauds and those from past periods of
financial frenzy.
"It happens year after year after year after year; it never changes," says Samuel Hayes, professor emeritus of
finance at the Harvard Business School. "Often the victims are people who are supposed to be very
sophisticated investors who shouldn't get caught like that. But they do."
Consider Sir Isaac Newton, one of history's most brilliant thinkers. He lost the equivalent of a modern-day $1
million in an 18th-century scam called the South Sea Bubble. Mark Twain, that great 19th-century skeptic,
repeatedly squandered his writing income on questionable investments, including a turn-of-the-century
version of biotechnology.
Or consider the unusual case of William Simon, the former Treasury secretary and Wall Street power broker,
who says he lost $1 million in a charity fraud involving something called the Foundation for New Era
Philanthropy. New Era promised to line up anonymous donors to match people's multimillion-dollar donations
to charity if they would just funnel the money through New Era.
Mr. Simon, Laurance Rockefeller, hedge-fund manager Julian Robertson and John Whitehead, former
Goldman, Sachs & Co. co-chairman, all fell for it.

--- Page 112 ---
Page 112 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But Mr. Simon insists he wasn't really the victim of a scam. He has visited New Era founder John Bennett in
jail and has offered him a job if he is paroled. Mr. Simon says that, while Mr. Bennett committed a fraud by
failing to match the money, he simply got in over his head and couldn't meet commitments. "People have said
to me, 'Well you were fooled,' and I suppose it is true that I was fooled because I don't believe he did anything
to defraud me," says Mr. Simon, who today devotes most of his time to philanthropy. "To this day I believe his
intentions were correct."
Mr. Simon's willingness to forgive a million-dollar loss may touch on something else about financial frauds:
They burgeon at times of great prosperity, when normal skepticism is overcome by the sight of enormous
wealth.
"The bull market creates conditions that are favorable to pulling off a fraud," says Richard Sylla, professor of
financial history at New York University's Stern School of Business. "Ten years ago people weren't interested
in jumping into the market," he continues, but "it begins to burn you when you know some people who are
getting rich. You say, 'There is nothing special about them, I know them, they were C students and I was a B+
student, so I'd better get in.'"
The Netherlands was rising to the height of its mercantile power when tulipmania struck in the 17th century.
Investors briefly paid more for a tulip bulb than for a house. Stocks were booming and empire was building in
early 18th-century Britain, when smooth operators, relying at times on commentaries written by such
renowned authors as Jonathan Swift and Daniel Defoe, pumped up the shares of a trading group called the
South Sea Company. And it was at the start of the Roaring '20s, with the U.S. entering a period of
unprecedented prosperity, that Charles Ponzi ran his fraud.
Does that suggest that today's golden age for fraud may herald an end to the bull market? NYU's Prof. Sylla
says he has a "feeling in my bones that something bad could happen."
But past experience is mixed. The popping of Britain's South Sea Bubble did lead to a period of economic
decline. The speculative excess of the 1980s culminated in the '87 crash, but stocks bounced back with
amazing speed. Dutch tulip-mania, although it destroyed some fortunes, didn't much affect the broader
economy. And while the Roaring '20s certainly ended badly, it wasn't Charles Ponzi's fault; he went to jail
nine years before the crash.
Either way, the bull-market mentality seems to foster fraudulent schemes. It may have driven Japanese
financial institutions to seek big commodity and currency gains through Mr. Armstrong's Princeton Economics
International, which according to court documents filed in federal court in New York may have lost a big part
of the institutions' several hundred million dollars in investments. Mr. Armstrong strenuously denies stealing
any money and says he will be vindicated. "This case is far more complicated than anyone expects," he said
outside a courtroom this month after pleading innocent.
The sky's-the-limit mentality also may have helped spur the New Era charity scheme, as wealthy donors saw
their donations doubled. It certainly fanned the fires of the Bre-X gold mania, and put pressure on traders at
Baring and Kidder to falsify accounts.
That kind of euphoria also appears to have clouded the vision of Sir Isaac Newton, a pioneer in explaining the
laws of gravity who apparently forgot that they also can apply to the stock market. Newton was a savvy public
figure, active in university life and at one point a member of Parliament. When 18th-century promoters
developed a complicated scheme to help pay off the national debt through the South Sea Company, Newton
acquired some shares.
As talk spread in London that the company was a huge bubble, manipulated by the promoters, the share
price leveled off. Newton then apparently sold out at a big profit, the present-day equivalent of hundreds of
thousands of dollars.
But the great man's skepticism was clouded by the sight of the stock continuing to soar. Although he is said to
have considered the whole thing a bubble, Newton apparently found it too tantalizing to avoid. Economists
call this the "bigger fool" theory of investing. An investor buys a stock -- maybe nowadays an Internet issue
with no profits but a skyrocketing market value -- knowing it is of dubious value but confident of selling it to
someone even more foolish before it falls. "They call it momentum investing now," Prof. Sylla says.
In a 1990 anthology called "Crashes and Panics," Larry Neal, professor of economics at the University of
Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, estimates that South Sea stock rose more than 640% in the space of six
months at the start of 1720. Some of today's fraudulent boiler-room stocks, and some Internet-related stocks
as well, have risen faster.
In any case, Newton bought back in and the South Sea stock collapsed. His losses totaled 20,000, which
economist John Kenneth Galbraith has calculated as the current equivalent of more than $1 million, wiping
out the earlier gains and much more.

--- Page 113 ---
Page 113 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
What makes many of these swindles work devilishly well is that they often begin, or appear to begin, as
legitimate investment projects, turning into frauds only after the initial plan goes bad.
Some victims of the New Era scheme, for example, point out that anonymous donors, matching gifts and
relationships of trust are common in the charity world. Even Ponzi's scheme appeared to be based on a
perfectly legitimate plan to benefit from exchange-rate differences.
Ponzi found that he could buy a postal-reply coupon in Spain for a penny and redeem it, in the U.S., for a
dime. Trouble was, there never were enough such coupons to cover the millions of dollars he got from
investors who formed lines outside his office. He simply paid big profits to old investors with money flooding in
from new ones. That pyramid system already had been used many times before, notably by a
turn-of-the-century hustler nicknamed "520% Miller," who promised his dupes 10% profit a week for 52
weeks. But Ponzi's investors were so faithful that, even after press exposes were published, people continued
putting in money until he was arrested and jailed.
Famous names also can lull investors into a false sense of security. "There will always be this piling on and
piggybacking on respectable names who you assume have done the homework, and who haven't," says
Harvard's Prof. Hayes. He speculates that the famous-name factor played a role in Japanese investments
with Mr. Armstrong. "The Japanese investors were substantial firms with good connections and plenty of
opportunity to do their due diligence, and apparently they didn't," Prof. Hayes says. "Someone gave them
assurance that there was imprimatur and they just got in."
Mr. Bennett, New Era's founder, played up an association with mutual-fund legend John Templeton, himself a
philanthropist, who invited Mr. Bennett to serve on the boards of some of his funds. Mr. Templeton stresses
that he never put any money into New Era.
"We believed in him," former Treasury Secretary Simon says, referring to Mr. Bennett. "When you get people
like John Templeton, Ray Chambers, the Rockefellers, who are all people who are involved in the world of
charity, it looks like this is real."
Mark Twain appears to have been swayed by investments linked to well-known businessmen or politicians.
His losing bets ranged from a health-food company to a new printing process and an Austrian carpet-weaving
machine. At least he was able to make fun of himself, and his experiences spawned some classic Twain
one-liners.
"There are two times in a man's life when he should not speculate: when he can't afford it, and when he can,"
he grumbled in "Following the Equator," an 1897 travel book. At around the same time, in "Pudd'nhead
Wilson," he issued this still-topical lament: "October. This is one of the peculiarly dangerous months to
speculate in stocks in. The others are July, January, September, April, November, May, March, June,
December, August and February."

Mundell on Supply-Side Economics
Mundell on Supply-Side Economics
1,602 words
14 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A26
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Robert A. Mundell, a Columbia University economist, won the Nobel economics prize yesterday. Following
are excerpts from an article he published in the July-August 1990 issue of the Rivista di Politica Economica,
an Italian economics journal. (Reprinted with permission.)
Supply-side economics emerged as a political force partly as a reaction against the steep progressivity of
personal and corporate income taxes and partly as a result of the breakdown of the international monetary
system in 1971. The principal features of the supply-side program were a reform of the tax system, featuring
a drastic slashing of marginal tax rates, and a reform of the international monetary system to one based on
stable exchange rates anchored by gold or some alternative international asset.
The United States had enacted high income tax rates after it entered World War I, and they were only
gradually lowered, to a maximum rate of 25% under {Treasury} Secretary {Andrew} Mellon. But in June 1932,
during the last year of the Hoover administration, marginal income tax rates were suddenly raised again to a
maximum level of 60%. They remained at or (frequently) considerably above this level for half a century. The
grand bargain entered into by both Democratic and Republican administrations up to 1981 was the exchange
of punitive marginal income tax rates to satisfy the political left in exchange for sweeping tax loopholes to
placate the political right.
The collapse of monetary discipline with flexible exchange rates in 1973 and the subsequent oil embargo and
increase in oil prices led to an inflation that further exacerbated the progressivity of the tax system. At the
same time the absence of a stable monetary environment led to rising interest rates and exchange rate
fluctuations that, almost arbitrarily, altered relative labor costs and international competitiveness. The
exchange rate was determined increasingly by volatile international capital movements rather than the
requirements of international trade. Reform of the international monetary system was therefore the second
pillar in the platform of supply-side economics.
Supply-side economics addressed itself directly to policy considerations rather than theoretical abstractions. It
would be a mistake, however, to believe that it lacks any less of a scientific foundation than the older
demand-side schools of monetarism and Keynesianism, or the new classical school. Its own academic
credentials lie in the solid allocation-theoretic literature of neo-classical economics and the policy-oriented
models of global monetarism and macroeconomics.
Supply-side economics shares much in common with each of the three schools but nevertheless rejects
certain features of each and adds elements of its own. With Friedmanism, it accepts the importance of the
quantity theory of money but rejects the policy program of fixed growth and flexible exchange rates so dear to
the hearts of monetarists. With Keynesianism it accepts the multiplier and the possibility of using tax cuts to
spur growth in the midst of a recession; but it takes account of reverse multiplier effects associated with
bond-financing of budget deficits and it rejects inflation as counterproductive. With neo-Ricardianism it
accepts the importance of expectations and time-consistency, but rejects the extreme super rationality and
altruistic form of the equivalence proposition.
Supply-side economics achieved major victories in monetary and tax policy in an amazingly short period of
time. From its beginnings in 1971 through its first success in indexing tax brackets in Canada in 1973, to the
first credited implementation in the United States in 1981, considerable success was achieved in less that a
decade. The Economy Recovery Act in 1981, based on the Kemp-Roth bill, cut income taxes by about 25%,
and its sequel, the Tax Reform Act of 1986, made sweeping changes in the tax code that included an
elimination of many loopholes and a slashing of tax rates in the highest income brackets to 28%.
One of the important tools of analysis, and a strong selling point of supply-side analysis was the Laffer curve,
relating tax rates and tax revenues. . . . The proposition that tax rates increase revenue up to a point of
maximum revenue after which they fall was by no means new. It must have been known to wise chancellors
of the ancient world devising tax systems. David Hume, referring to indirect taxation wrote that "a duty upon

--- Page 115 ---
Page 115 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
commodities checks itself; and a prince will soon find that an encrease of the impost is no encrease of his
revenue." ...
Nevertheless, if not new, it was certainly forgotten in the half century between 1932 and 1981 when marginal
tax rates were never less than 60% and hovered at times near 100%. . . .
The Carter administration had followed an easy money policy along the lines of the neo-classical synthesis. It
aggravated inflation and, because of the progressive income tax, tightened fiscal policy. The result was a
steep depreciation of the dollar.
The neo-classical synthesis proved to be an appropriate policy mix as it had been in the early years of the
Kennedy administration. The Reagan administration reversed the Carter policy mix in favor of the
Mundell-Fleming policy mix of tight money (to stop the inflation) and fiscal ease (supply-side tax cuts to spur
economic growth and increase defense spending).
Because the tight money was implemented before the tax cuts could take the effect, the economy moved into
a steep recession, reaching bottom in November 1982. From this point on, the economy moved into an
expansion that has continued into the Bush administration, the longest peacetime expansion on record.
In the first term of the Reagan-Bush administrations, the dollar appreciated strongly in the face of the
strengthening economy (after 1982), having the beneficial effect of slowing the inflation at the same time the
economy was accelerating. In 1984, however, growth in the economy slowed and the Federal Reserve
shifted to a more expansionary monetary policy. The dollar peaked in February 1985 at about DM 3.4 and
230 yen. It then began a descent that continued through the 1987 stock market crash. Nevertheless, in 1988
and 1989, with the economy still expanding, the dollar became strong again, recovering against the yen and
the European currencies, only to depreciate again in 1990.
Supply-siders had recommended a reform of the international monetary system and a movement to stable
exchange rates. This would have prevented the overshooting of the yen and mark in 1987. Only a limited
success of the supply-side program was achieved in the field of international monetary reform with the Plaza
and Louvre agreements to coordinate exchange rates. Missing from this arrangement was an anchor for the
major currencies (to prevent all countries from inflating together under fixed exchange rates). In lieu of an
anchor, the Group of Seven attempted to devise a criterion in the form of a price index to adjudicate whether
surplus countries should expand or deficit countries contract. Intensive discussions were held at the IMF
meeting in September 1987, but progress in this field got side-tracked after the 1987 stock market crash. . . .
I have already noted that supply-side economics stressed the usefulness of a fixed exchange rate system tied
to a stable monetary unit. In the early years of international relations, it was not possible to think in terms of a
world currency because there was no possibility of organizing one among nations who went to war against
each other. No international monetary system is warproof. In lieu of an international currency, trading nations
settled on one or more of the precious metals, and throughout the ages, the coins of a major economic power
became important units of account.
The gold standard represented such a system and the gold sovereign, the currency of the leading economic
power, became the most important unit of account. But the gold standard broke down in 1914, and despite
attempts to reconstruct it, conditions were never appropriate. The gold exchange standard established in the
1920s broke down because it was erected on too slender a gold base, a casualty of World War I inflation.
History repeated itself in the post-World War II period after inflation had again undervalued gold. This led to
a run on the gold stock of the United States, the only power with even a partially gold-convertible currency.
In the decade of the 1970s, the decision by IMF members to adopt a managed flexible exchange rate system
was accompanied by a commitment to emphasize the role of the SDR and to reduce and banalize the role of
gold. . . .
In the days of the Cold War, national security took priority over monetary systems. But in the post-Cold War
era there is likely to be both less opposition and more to gain from a stable international currency. The
emerging multi-polar world has in many respects more in common with the patterns of world power in the
heyday of the gold standard than with the recent bi-polar past. It presents a better opportunity to create a
world central bank with a stable international currency than at any previous time in history. There is no
reason why the gold stocks held by governments and central banks should not be utilized to create an
international money.
There are numerous options. A trillion-dollar central bank, producing its own currency, backed by gold and
foreign exchange reserves (and, if necessary, other convertible currencies) could be made the central
institution around which a stable international monetary system could be erected.

Dollar, After Surging Against the Yen,Finishes the Session Mixed in New York
Foreign Exchange
Dollar, After Surging Against the Yen, Finishes the Session Mixed in New York
By Marianne Sullivan
Dow Jones Newswires
588 words
14 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C22
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Investors didn't take long to conclude that the elation following Japan's announcement to
boost liquidity was premature.
The dollar rallied a yen and a half Wednesday after the Bank of Japan announced it would expand its
money-market operations, but that optimism faded quickly. By the end of the U.S. trading session, the dollar
was being sold alongside U.S. stocks and bonds. It closed slightly higher against the yen but lower against
the euro.
After its Wednesday policy meeting the Bank of Japan said it would expand money-market operations.
Keeping short-term interest rates near zero, it announced it would start the outright purchase of short-term
government bonds in an effort to "assure further permeation of the effects of monetary easing."
T.J. Marta, currency analyst at Citibank in New York, said markets initially saw the Bank of Japan's move as
an offering of an "olive branch" to critics who had urged the central bank to loosen monetary policy and to
weaken the yen.
By day's end, traders were again turning their attention to interest-rate worries, concluding that little had really
changed in Japan's monetary policy.
Late Wednesday in New York, the dollar was trading at 106.54 yen, up from 106.21 yen late Tuesday. The
euro was buying $1.0811, its peak level since Aug. 5 and up from $1.0762 the day before.
It was almost a repeat of Tuesday's market action. The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 184.90 points
yesterday after suffering a 231.12-point drop Tuesday. The yield on the Treasury's 30-year bond moved
briefly to its highest level of the year at 6.29%, but ended at 6.27%.
Markets have been falling all week as inflation worries mounted in the wake of last Friday's jobs data,
improving global growth prospects and rising commodities prices. And speculation is mounting that the
Federal Reserve will boost interest rates by a quarter of a percentage point at its Nov. 16 meeting.
The concurrent tightening of monetary policy could have a neutralizing effect in currency markets, but most
likely will cause volatility with market players "hanging on the lips of policy markets," Mr. Marta said.
While the euro is supported by the specter of higher interest rates in the euro-zone, the dollar follows the
reaction of U.S. assets to interest-rate concerns.
Of late, stocks and bonds have both sold off on inflation fears, which for the U.S. signals an impending
slowdown. In Europe, higher rates are seen as a sign of recovery and one which will make eurozone
investment more attractive.
Elsewhere, the South African rand hit a four-week low against the dollar, damping positive sentiment toward
the currency which had traded at higher levels since gold began to rally three weeks ago.
A large order for dollars sparked a round of technical selling which drove the rand lower, traders said. Late
Wednesday, the dollar was quoted at 6.1279 rand, up strongly from 6.0755 rand late Tuesday.
The Canadian dollar fell to five-week lows against the U.S. dollar, weighed down by the specter of higher U.S.
interest rates, which would make Canadian assets less attractive. Late Wednesday, the U.S. currency was
trading at 1.4821 Canadian dollars, compared with $1.4754 Canadian dollars late Tuesday.

Abreast of the Market
Citigroup, Chase and P&G Slide; Technology Issues Also Take Hit
By Robert O'Brien

1,382 words
14 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal

English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Blue-chip issues reeled under another onslaught from rising interest rates, while earnings
disappointments sent technology issues skidding in a session of sharp, broad-based losses.
Shares of Citigroup fell 1 11/16 to 43 13/16, while Chase Manhattan lost 2 1/16 to 71 3/16, and J.P. Morgan
dropped 4 3/8 to 109 1/2. The losses in the group came as the steady rise in Treasury yields propelled the
bellwether long bond's yield past the 6 1/4 % threshold, a level where Treasury returns become more
competitive versus equity returns, which could persuade some investors to swap stock holdings for bonds.
At the very least, the signs abounded that investors raised lots of cash in the session. Blue-chip stocks of
every stripe turned lower. Consumer names, such as Procter & Gamble, off 2 1/16 to 95 1/8, and Campbell
Soup, down 1 7/16 to 39 7/8, suffered in the same measure as cyclical names, which saw AlliedSignal drop 2
1/16 to 58 5/16 and Deere fall 1 5/8 to 36 3/8.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, logging its second straight session of steep losses, tumbled 184.90, or
1.77%, to close at 10232.16. The decline brought its two-session total of losses to 416.02 points.
A few commodity plays finished higher, including Barrick Gold among precious metals, which increased 1
7/16 to 21 3/4, and Atlantic Richfield among oils, which gained 1 9/16 to 88 1/2.
This time there would be no sanctuary in the high-technology sector. Not after Intel disappointed Wall Street
with another underwhelming quarterly profit performance late Tuesday. Intel (Nasdaq) fell 4 9/16 to 72 1/8, as
the chip maker jarred confidence in the third-quarter earnings outlook, especially for high technology.
"We don't have any clarity on interest rates, and we're learning that earnings aren't going to be the catalyst
for this market after all," said Richard Cripps, market strategist at Legg Mason. "Where do you get the
conviction to be a buyer under those circumstances?"
The Standard & Poor's 500-Stock Index, which fell 27.49, or 2.09%, to 1285.55, ended the session 9.4%
below its closing high of the year. Analysts said that, if there are to be further losses, the technology sector is
going to suffer more than the broad market.
As investors lost some of their confidence in the superior earnings outlook for technology stocks, they started
to sell off their holdings in stocks scheduled to report third-quarter results this week.
Shares of Sandisk (Nasdaq) fell 8 to 46 1/4; after the close of the session, the Palo Alto, Calif., developer of
data-storage products reported its fiscal third-quarter results. Results matched Wall Street forecasts.
Apple Computer (Nasdaq), which also came out with stronger-than-expected numbers after the close of
trading, fell 3 21/32 to 64 1/32. Earthlink Networks (Nasdaq), also on the schedule to report yesterday,
declined 3 to 41 15/16.
Meanwhile, other chip names fell in sympathy with the selloff in Intel. Linear Technology (Nasdaq) lost 2
23/32 to 55 25/32, despite posting stronger-than-expected fiscal first-quarter results ahead of the trading
session.
Motorola fell 4 9/16 to 90 3/8; the maker of communications electronics posted results in line with estimates
late Tuesday.
By the close of trading, the broader technology and telecommunications sector turned lower. Nextel

Investors Sell Off Bonds Again in Weak Market,Propelling Interest Rates to Near Peak for Year
Credit Markets
Investors Sell Off Bonds Again in Weak Market, Propelling Interest Rates to Near Peak for Year
By Gregory Zuckerman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
955 words
14 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C24
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Bonds continued their painful sell-off, sending interest rates near their highest level of the
year.
This weakness in the bond market that has continued apace in recent weeks has put upward pressure on
various other interest rates such as credit cards and mortgages, pinching consumers in the pocketbook.
The bond-price declines come amid worries about rising inflation and the strength of the global economy,
and a growing view among traders the Federal Reserve is likely to raise interest rates in the months ahead.
Even marked weakness in the stock market did little to add luster to Treasury bonds, as investors willing to
buy fixed-income securities focused instead on more than $8 billion in agency and corporate debt hitting the
market, ignoring the Treasury market in the process.
In late trading, the bellwether 30-year Treasury bond fell 20/32, or $6.25 for a bond with $1,000 face value, to
97 29/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction of its price, climbed to 6.271%. The rout
was worse early in the day, but prices came back somewhat near day's end. The yield challenged the high for
the year, set in August, of 6.28%.
Medium-term bonds also dropped sharply. Ten-year Treasurys fell 12/32 to yield 6.097%.
"We went through [price] levels we all thought would bring in buyers, like 6.25% on the bond, but nothing
happened," said Mike Clorherty, market strategist at Credit Suisse First Boston.
Instead, selling picked up in afternoon trading, as gold, oil and other commodities moved higher, sparking
fear the Federal Reserve will have to boost rates soon to stem inflation. Weakness in the Canadian bond
market also spilled over south of the border, while some investors took another look at Friday's employment
figures and didn't like what they saw.
"People are much more concerned about inflation and about [Fed Chairman] Alan Greenspan being behind
the curve," said William Lloyd, head of market strategy at Barclays Capital in New York.
Traders are focusing on producer-price and consumer-price data, to be released tomorrow and Tuesday,
respectively.
"If the [inflation] figures are as high as people expect they will be, thoughts of inflation being a problem will
return, even if it's not here right now, and the market will have to price in one more Fed move and the
possibility of another," said Jim Nealis, head Treasurys trader at PaineWebber.
Figures today on retail sales, and industrial production data tomorrow, also will be scrutinized by investors.
A move by the Bank of Japan to promote growth by purchasing short-term securities and to expand its
repurchaseagreement operations to inject liquidity in the market raised fears global growth will pick up,
putting pressure on rates around the world.
"The market looks oversold, but we're not buying" because in the longer term the "seeds have been sown for
higher inflation, due to higher commodity costs," says Jim Somers, a portfolio manager at Martindale Andres
& Co. in West Conshohocken, Pa.
Here are the details of yesterday's 52-week bill auction. All bids are awarded at a single price at the
market-clearing yield. Rates are determined by the difference between that price and face value.

The bills are dated Oct. 14 and mature Oct. 12, 2000.
Corporate Bonds
Despite longstanding fears the bond market would shut down in the fourth quarter ahead of 2000, investors
were clamoring for the $2 billion inaugural global bond issue from DuPont.
"To all those people who were worried about Y2K, this is another signal that the markets are not as illiquid as
people had predicted," said Mitchel Penn, portfolio manager at Legg Mason Capital Management in
Baltimore, who participated in the offering.
DuPont priced $1 billion of five-year securities and the same amount of 10-year securities via co-lead
managers and joint bookrunners Credit Suisse First Boston and Morgan Stanley Dean Witter.
The five-year notes were priced at a yield margin of 0.83 percentage point over comparable U.S. Treasury
securities, while the 10-year notes priced at a yield margin of 0.98 percentage point over. The bonds later
firmed in secondary dealings.
Proceeds from the offering are expected to be used to reduce short-term debt, according to DuPont's
treasurer, Susan Stalnecker.
Agency Issues
Freddie Mac tapped burgeoning demand for U.S. agency securities overseas, selling $5 billion of reference
notes with a yield of 0.525 percentage point over twoyear Treasurys.
Freddie Mac said more than half of the offering was sold overseas. Investors outside the U.S. tend to favor
shorter maturities because that minimizes currency and interest-rate risk while providing ample yield levels.
Many have been buying agency debt as an alternative to Treasurys. "A lot of accounts look to the
government agencies to provide liquid alternatives to Treasurys," said Drew Ertman, principal on the
syndicate desk at joint lead manager Morgan Stanley Dean Witter.
Although agencies don't have the full faith and credit of the U.S. government, they are congressionally
chartered entities and their debt enjoys top triple-A ratings.
ABN Amro Securities and Warburg Dillon Read also were joint lead managers of the offering.

NEW YORK -- Volatility climbed on the options market, as the stock market sold off.

tumbling. In the options market, the Chicago Board Options Exchange Market Volatility Index gained 2.55 to
24.60. The VIX tracks certain Standard & Poor's 100 options prices to determine investor sentiment.

dramatic rise, but nonetheless he called yesterday's VIX action a "fairly decent move up."

November 35s. Alaska Air's stock closed down 1 3/8 , or 3.3%, at 39 3/4 , also on the Big Board.

By Steven M. Sears

NEW YORK -- Traders ground it out, without guidance from the bond market.
In the absence of information on interest rates, a critical component of options and stock prices, most
traders concentrated on the mundane responsibilities of preparing their portfolios for Friday's expiration of
October options contracts.
The option market's fear gauge, the Chicago Board Options Exchange Market Volatility Index, or VIX, gained
0.86 to 22.06, keeping the barometer within its usual range of 17 to 25.
A lot of traders rolled October options contracts into November. Bold trades on the direction of stocks or the
stock market weren't apparent with the bond market closed for Columbus Day.
Traders who felt compelled to take positions used conservative approaches. In Barrick Gold Corp., a firm
sold 4,000 January 25 calls and bought 2,000 January 22 1/2 calls to create a ratio-bull spread that would
increase in value if the stock increases from its current 20 13/16.
Many traders used the lull to sell call options against stock in their portfolios.
Thousands of February 30 call options were sold on Harrah's Entertainment Inc. With the stock up 5/16 at 29
11/16, the February 30 calls fell 1/4 to 2 1/8 on volume of 3,003 contracts, compared with open interest of
1,214 contracts. The calls traded at 2 1/8 when the market was 2 1/8 to 2 3/8 and the stock was at 29 5/16.
A number of large call trades helped push the CBOE's equity put/call ratio to 0.67. The ratio is traditionally
interpreted as a contrarian sentiment indicator, which holds that if too many traders are bullish, the smart
approach is bearish. The index historically has been considered a bullish sentiment indicator at around 0.75
to 1; neutral from 0.40 to 0.75; bearish below 0.40. The CBOE's index put/call ratio was 1.70. The ratio is also
seen as a contrarian sentiment indicator. The index is considered bullish if the ratio is 1.5 or higher; neutral
from 0.75 to 1.5; bearish below 0.75.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Jakarta Discloses
Pertamina Losses
May Be $4 Billion
Indonesian state oil company Pertamina had losses of as much as $3.97 billion between April 1996 and
March 1998, and could lose an additional $721 million stemming from unfavorable contracts it entered during
the two years, Finance Minister Bambang Subianto said. Announcing the results of an audit of Pertamina
carried out by Pricewaterhouse-Coopers, Mr. Bambang said inefficiencies cost the company an estimated $2
billion; an additional $1.27 billion to $1.97 billion was lost as a result of what the report said were missed
opportunities. The government also announced the results of an audit of state commodity board Bulog by
accounting firm Arthur Andersen. That audit, covering five years ended in March 1998, found that the agency
incurred a loss of 6.7 trillion rupiah ($866 million) resulting from inefficiencies. "This was because of
unfavorable contract terms for Bulog, irregularities, incorrect practices and a weak supervision system," the
minister said. The government agreed to the audits of Pertamina and Bulog, as well as of electric utility
Perusahaan Listrik Negara, as part of the International Monetary Fund's rescue package for Indonesia.
Findings of the audit of PLN are expected this month.
The actual audits of Pertamina and Bulog weren't released, and government officials said they won't be made
public. Mr. Bambang said the auditing firms had used "sampling methods" that involved reviewing only some
of the companies' contracts. Because of this, he said, "the auditors are uncomfortable to make conclusions
about corruption."
Anglogold Bids for Australia's Acacia
South African gold-mining concern Anglogold launched a friendly bid valued at 832 million Australian dollars
($544.8 million) to acquire Australian gold miner Acacia Resources. The move by the world's largest
gold-mining company effectively trumps a hostile A$670 million bid for Acacia launched last month by
Australia's Delta Gold. Analysts said Anglogold's bid may herald a new round of takeovers in the sector as
acquirers move to beat rises in gold stocks triggered by soaring bullion prices. Anglogold is offering 3.5 of its
shares for every 100 Acacia shares, valuing Acacia shares at A$3.30 each, a 24% premium to Friday's
closing price of A$2.66. Acacia's board intends to recommend that shareholders accept Anglogold's offer, in
the absence of a higher bid, Acacia said. Anglogold shares are listed in Johannesburg, London, Brussels and
Paris, as well as on the New York Stock Exchange. Anglogold's formal offer is expected next month.
China's CNOOC to Launch Offering
Oil exploration group CNOOC, China's first major oil company to seek an overseas listing, said it plans to
offer two billion shares at 8.46 to 9.61 Hong Kong dollars (US$1.09 to US$1.24) a share in a dual listing in
Hong Kong and New York. The offer runs today through Friday; the price will be fixed on Oct. 20 in Hong
Kong. Trading in the shares there will begin Oct. 21 and is expected to begin in New York on Oct. 20.
Daewoo Foreign Creditors to Meet
Foreign creditors of South Korea's Daewoo Group are to meet Oct. 22 in Hong Kong, where they will either
accept or reject the ailing conglomerate's request for a freeze on an estimated $5 billion in foreign debt. A
person close to the negotiations said the foreign creditors' steering committee met with Daewoo and its
international advisers last week, spelling out the prerequisites that need to be included in any debt freeze and
workout plan. If the demands -- which include unwinding global transactions after a certain date and giving

--- Page 127 ---
Page 127 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
foreign creditors more say in a workout plan -- aren't met, the committee will disband and recommend that
Daewoo's 200-plus creditors pursue claims in international courts, he said. Separately, Daewoo Motor is in
talks with "two to three" European companies to sell its truck and bus manufacturing operations, a company
spokesman said. He declined to describe the status of the talks, saying Daewoo Motor, an affiliate of Daewoo
Group, had agreed not to reveal the content of the talks publicly.
BRIEFLY:
-- Thailand's trade surplus narrowed to $731.7 million in September from $1.30 billion a year earlier, the
government said in a preliminary report. Exports rose 8.3% to $5.06 billion, while imports surged 29% from a
year before to $4.33 billion.

EUROPE/AFRICA
U.K. Producer Prices Show Uptick
Tentative signs that rising producer input prices in Britain are beginning to feed through to inflation at the
factory gate set off few alarm bells yesterday. Analysts said the data won't cause too much concern at the
Bank of England because manufacturers are still having difficulty passing on rising costs to consumers. The
government reported producer output prices rose 0.3% in September from August and were up 1.7%
year-to-year, stronger than expected rises of 0.1% and 1.5%, respectively. The annual rise was last higher in
December 1996, when it was 1.9%.
Lonmin Bids for Rest of Ashanti
British mining company Lonmin has offered to buy the 68% of shares in Ashanti Goldfields that it doesn't
already own. Ashanti, a gold-mining company based in Ghana, has been struggling to come up with about
$270 million to pay creditors for margin calls on futures contracts for the sale of gold; the company, which
had hedged 40% of its reserves against a fall in gold prices, was surprised by gold's recent surge. Under its
offer, Lonmin would pay 32 new Lonmin shares for every 43 Ashanti shares it doesn't own. People familiar
with the offer said the price for the stake is expected to be about GBP 335 million ($553 million). Ghana's
government holds 20% of Ashanti and has the power to veto a deal.
Britain's GEC to Become Marconi PLC
Britain's General Electric Co. PLC said it will change its name to Marconi PLC, following the planned merger
of its Marconi Electronics Systems business with British Aerospace. GEC said it expects that Marconi PLC
will be listed on the London Stock Exchange and will be a constituent of the FTSE-100 index. The United
Kingdom electronics company, which isn't related to General Electric of the U.S., said it also plans to seek a
U.S. listing next year.
Baird to Acquire Granville of Britain
British investment bank Granville struck a deal with Robert W. Baird & Co., a unit of Northwestern Mutual Life
Insurance of the U.S., that will let Granville keep its name, management and a degree of independence. The
deal values Granville at GBP 33 million ($54.5 million) and will create a bank with combined assets of $4.4
billion. The new bank, to be called Granville Baird, will in effect become Baird's European arm.
BRIEFLY:
-- South Africa and the European Union, after more than four years of negotiations, signed a free-trade pact.
The agreement proposes abolishing tariffs on about 90% of two-way trade between South Africa and the EU
that exceeds $17 billion annually.
-- Italian bank Unicredito Italiano is likely to form a joint venture with Spain's Banco Bilbao Vizcaya that will
result in a share swap, people close to the banks said. The swap would be based primarily on Unicredito
buying BBV's 10.1% stake in Banca Nazionale del Lavoro. Unicredito is Italy's largest bank by market value,
while BBV is Spain's No. 2 bank.
-- The U.S. should drop its opposition to discussing antitrust policy in the framework of the next round of
world-trade talks, the European Union's competition commissioner said. The U.S. opposes such talks on the
grounds they are premature as long as many countries have no functioning antitrust agencies.
-- French chefs protesting against value-added taxes pelted surprised Paris riot police with eggs and flour,
prompting police to respond with tear gas to keep the protesters away from Parliament.

Mexican Agency to Probe Bad Loans
Mexico's bank-deposit insurance agency, known as IPAB, said it is conducting an in-depth investigation into
about 48 billion pesos ($5.07 billion) of bad commercial-bank loans the government assumed in the 1995
bank-bailout program. "Depending on the results of these reviews, the cases that have been determined to be
based on some irregularities will be turned over to the competent authorities," IPAB said. Under a Mexican
congressional agreement, only loans of legal origin are to be assumed by IPAB.
Nexfor to Acquire Rest of CSC Forest
Canadian forest-products company Nexfor said it will boost its interest in CSC Forest Products to 100% from
50% as part of its continuing strategy to be more focused and pursue growth of its panelboard businesses.
Nexfor said CSC Forest is a joint venture in Britain with Glunz of Germany. It said it will exercise its right
under the joint-venture pact to acquire Glunz's 50% stake in CSC for GBP 50 million ($83 million) and will
assume Glunz's share of CSC's debt of about GBP 25 million.

English

NEW YORK -- The dollar weakened slightly against the yen and euro in moribund holiday-thinned trading.
Financial markets were closed in Japan and Canada, and U.S. bond markets were closed for Columbus Day,
though the New York Stock Exchange was open.
In Europe, currency players shrugged off data showing euro-zone second-quarter gross domestic product
was revised up to 0.5% growth from the 0.3% growth reported earlier.
Volume may prove light today as well, if market participants choose to sit on the sidelines ahead of an
important Bank of Japan meeting tomorrow.
Some Japanese newspapers have speculated that the central bank might ease policy by purchasing
short-dated bonds, but many analysts see this as highly unlikely.
"I just think the BOJ feels things are on track fundamentally . . . that there's enough macro stimulus," said
Larry Hatheway, global head of strategy at Warburg Dillon Read in Stamford, Conn., pointing out that Japan's
banking system is past its worst point and another huge fiscal-stimulus package is in the works.
But, "the market still has uncertainties," Mr. Hatheway added.
Since Group of Seven finance ministers issued a statement against yen strength on Sept. 25, the dollar has
stabilized off 44-month lows against the yen. The dollar, which was in free fall for the latter part of the third
quarter, bottomed out at 103.20 yen on Sept. 15 but has more recently recovered to a range around 107 yen.
If that dollar recovery was predicated on expected changes in Japanese monetary policy, the dollar risks
sinking on no-change announcements, according to analysts.
The dollar is supported, however, by signs that asset reallocation into yen-based investments is nearly
finished. In the week ended Oct. 1, non-Japanese investors were net sellers of Japanese stocks for the first
time on a weekly basis since Aug. 13, the Tokyo Stock Exchange reported last week.
The dollar is also helped by stability in U.S. stocks, despite the Federal Reserve's bias toward raising interest
rates.
Investors will look to U.S. retail-sales data on Thursday and producer-price data on Friday for more clues on
the likelihood of an imminent rate increase.
Meanwhile, "the euro is still essentially undervalued," Mr. Hatheway said. "As long as the German data
continue to print higher, the euro will continue to grind higher."
But the euro's progress is hampered by heavy positioning and clouded by concerns about German
Chancellor Gerhard Schroeder's ability to deliver promised reforms. The ruling Social Democrats on Sunday
suffered election losses in Berlin, following setbacks the past several weeks in state elections across
Germany.
Late Monday in New York, the dollar was trading at 106.82 yen, down from 107.39 yen late Friday. The euro
was trading at $1.0647, up from $1.0628. The euro was also changing hands at 113.72 yen, below 114.30
yen.
Sterling was quoted at $1.6528, up from $1.6517.

Party's recent strong victory in federal elections.

and United Kingdom, especially with Japanese economic policy making still shrouded by obscurity.

Bond Prices Fall as Fed Leaves Rates UnchangedBut Signals That It Might Tighten in the Future
Credit Markets
Bond Prices Fall as Fed Leaves Rates Unchanged But Signals That It Might Tighten in the Future
By Gregory Zuckerman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
957 words
6 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C26
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Bond prices tumbled as the Federal Reserve left interest rates alone, as expected, but
indicated there may be enough inflationary pressure building in the economy to necessitate raising interest
rates in the months ahead.
In late trading, the bellwether 30-year Treasury bond was down 1 7/32, or $12.1875 for a bond with a $1,000
face value, at 99 7/32. The bond's yield, which moves inversely to the price, climbed to 6.173% from 6.083%.
Medium-term bonds also dropped sharply. Ten-year Treasurys, for example, fell 24/32 to yield 6.029%.
The jump in yields puts pressure on various other rates, like credit-card and mortgage rates, pinching
consumers in the pocketbook.
Investors are worried bond prices will keep going lower. "People are focusing on the next move, as they
always do with the Fed, and there's an expectation that there will be one more move by the Fed [to raise
rates] before the end of the year," said Robert Kapito, who manages $142 billion at BlackRock in New York.
"The tightening bias affirms that the Fed could [raise rates] on or before the November meeting," said Chris
Fitzmaurice, head trader at Salomon Smith Barney, who says the 30-year bond's yield could hit 6.75% in next
year's first quarter.
The Fed left its short-term interest-rate target -- the federal funds rate -- unchanged, as expected by Wall
Street economists and traders. (The federal-funds rate governs overnight loans between banks.) But the Fed
indicated it was leaning toward raising rates in the future, a so-called tightening bias that came as something
of a surprise to some bond investors nervous and instantly sent the market tumbling after the 2:15 p.m. EDT
announcement.
"The Federal Open Market Committee will need to be especially alert in the months ahead to the potential for
costs to increase significantly in excess of productivity in a manner that could contribute to inflation
pressures and undermine the impressive performance of the economy," the policy-making panel said in a
statement.
Some said the lack of a move on interest rates, and the shift in the Fed's bias, created an imposing cloud
that will hang over the market in the weeks ahead.
"We're back to being worried about oil, gold and the economy, and now the Fed is putting us on notice that
they think they're going to tighten in the future," said Bill Hornbarger, chief income strategist at A.G. Edwards
& Sons in St. Louis. "That's the way the market perceives it, even if the Fed didn't mean to be so hawkish."
Indeed, investors immediately began to worry about a rate increase when the FOMC meets again on Nov. 16.
Though year 2000 is around the corner, and many remain worried about the effects any computer-related
glitches will have on world economies, the Fed has indicated that it is less concerned about the economic
effects of such computer problems, and could in fact raise rates next month, analysts noted.
Some traders began to anticipate the yield on the 30-year bond heading past 6.3% if the September
employment numbers, due out Friday, prove strong.
"The key report is employment on Friday, and the wage-gain component in particular, because the Fed is
now indicating that they'll keep a close eye on whether there's a labor shortage, and it's appropriate for the
market to as well," said Richard Schwartz, who manages $23 billion at New York Life Asset Management,
Parsippany, N.J. Mr. Schwartz is avoiding buying bonds before the report comes out. "The market has taken
on a negative tone."

--- Page 136 ---
Page 136 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Corporate Bonds
Sprint debt saw heavy trading in the wake of MCI WorldCom's deal to acquire the company.
Yield margins to Treasurys for 30-year Sprint securities, which had narrowed recently, widened slightly to
1.42 percentage points on profit-taking, traders said. Yield margins on MCI WorldCom's 30-year debt were
unchanged around 1.35 percentage points over Treasurys.
Meanwhile, Moody's Investors Service Inc. confirmed ratings on MCI WorldCom's long-term debt and put
Sprint long-term ratings on review for possible upgrade. Standard & Poor's Ratings Services put MCI
WorldCom and Sprint on CreditWatch with positive implications.
WorldCom is rated single-A-3 by Moody's and single-A-minus by S&P while Sprint is rated Baa1 by Moody's
and triple-B-plus by S&P. Most analysts said ratings of the combined company should hover around MCI
WorldCom's single-A levels.
Mortgage-Backed Securities
Mortgage-backed securities ended lower along with Treasurys. Among benchmark issues, Fannie Mae 7.5%
securities were off 14/32 at 99 26/32.
The sector had performed more strongly than Treasurys until an investor made a major late-session sale of
7% and 7.5% securities, traders said.
Issuance of mortgage-backed securities has been ebbing as rising interest rates have discouraged
refinancings of home mortgages, resulting in fewer loans to securitize.
However, this week, Fannie Mae announced a $1.175 billion stripped MBS trust, or securities that are broken
into separate portions paying either principal or interest.
Fannie Mae SMBS Trust 303 was expected to see good demand because it is the first such transaction in
about 18 months to have a 7.5% coupon, reflecting recent increases in interest rates. Other transactions
recently have had 7% coupons.

Dollar Retreats From Its Early SurgeAfter Fed Makes No Change in Rates
Foreign Exchange
Dollar Retreats From Its Early Surge After Fed Makes No Change in Rates
By Stephanie Hoo
Dow Jones Newswires
690 words
6 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar, which spent much of the day edging higher against the yen and euro, shed those
gains in a hurry following the U.S. Federal Reserve's afternoon announcement of no change in interest rates,
but a bias toward raising them in the future.
When the dust settled, the dollar finished Tuesday up slightly against the yen and euro from levels late
Monday, but with subdued gains overall.
The Fed decision is expected to weigh on the dollar -- especially against the resurgent euro -- as long as U.S.
assets struggle in a rate-tightening environment.
"There's no new incentive to buy financial assets at this stage of the game," said Kathy Jones, currency
analyst at Prudential Securities in New York.
The change in bias means investors must remain on guard against creeping price and wage pressures -- on
the possibility the Federal Open Market Committee might raise interest rates when it next meets on Nov. 16.
"The outlook for long-term yields is negative, which is problematic for foreign investors," said Kathleen
Stephansen, chief international economist at Donaldson Lufkin and Jenrette in New York, anticipating a
continued rebalancing of portfolios away from U.S. assets.
The Fed's decision was right in line with consensus expectations. Fed officials, in their statement, noted the
FOMC "will need to be especially alert in the months ahead to the potential for costs to increase significantly
in excess of productivity in a manner that could contribute to inflation pressures and undermine the
impressive performance of the economy."
Helping keep dollar losses in check Tuesday was a bit of market nervousness ahead of a European Central
Bank meeting later this week and signs that the euro's recent strong rally needs a some technical
consolidation before proceeding further.
The euro, which was trading at around $1.0690 right before the FOMC announcement at 2:12 p.m. EDT, shot
to a high of $1.0763 in the hour following the announcement, but never seriously challenged Monday's high of
$1.0772.
The euro had taken a knock in early European trading Tuesday after worse-than-anticipated German
employment data damped speculation that the ECB might raise rates as soon as Thursday.
While the German jobless rate fell to 10.1% in September from 10.3% in August, the number of German
jobless rose 9,000 in the month on the heels of a 4,000 gain in August and far exceeding forecasts.
The dollar held even firmer against the yen. The U.S. currency fell to a New York low of about 106.30 yen
after the announcement, from 106.95 yen just before the announcement, but its decline was well within the
day's range.
In late afternoon New York trading, the dollar was at 106.47 yen, up from 106.26 late Monday. The euro was
at $1.0735, down from $1.0738. The euro was at 114.30 yen, up from 114.14 yen. Sterling was at $1.6557,
essentially flat from $1.6553.
Elsewhere, the South African rand strengthened as gold resumed its strong rally. The U.S. dollar was worth
5.9875 rand late Tuesday in Johnannesburg, down from 6.0025 rand late Monday.

--- Page 138 ---
Page 138 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Australian dollar, which finds support from rising gold prices, continued to struggle amid heavy
positioning. It set a three-month high of 66.62 U.S. cents Tuesday but fell to 66.29 U.S. cents by the New
York close, nearly flat with 66.27 U.S. cents a day earlier.
The Thai baht spiked higher after Thailand's central bank tightened the rules governing onshore loans to
offshore markets. The U.S. dollar closed in Bangkok worth 39.500 baht, down from 40.425 baht late Monday,
and the tighter liquidity is expected to continue to buoy the baht.
The Bank of Thailand announced it would impose a 50 million-baht ceiling on loans to nonresidents, and all
branches of an onshore head office are now considered one counterparty.

Commodities' Role as Inflation Harbinger Ebbs
Commodities
Commodities' Role as Inflation Harbinger Ebbs
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,478 words
6 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Corrections & Amplifications
THE DOW JONES-AIG COMMODITY Index, which isn't as energy-reliant as some other measures of
commodity prices, is up about 17% for the year. Yesterday's Commodities column incorrectly stated that the
index was down 26% for the year. (WSJ Oct. 7, 1999)
Copper prices have risen about 22% this year. Nickel is up more than 70%, gold 13%, platinum 11%, silver
11% and oil a startling 92%. Overall, the Bridge/CRB index of 17 commodities has risen about 6%.
But the U.S. core consumer price index is up just 1.9% from a year ago. And while that may have edged up a
bit from the inflation rate earlier this year, most economists say inflation appears to be well under control.
How can that be? Haven't higher commodity prices historically translated quickly into higher inflation?
True. But for two main reasons, it is different now -- so far, anyway. In general, many experts say
commodities' role as an inflation indicator has diminished in recent years as the economy has become more
service-oriented and technology-driven.
"What does the price of crude oil have to do with the Internet?" Jeff Applegate, chief investment strategist at
Lehman Brothers asks rhetorically. "Not much."
And in particular, economists say the latest commodity boom has left behind some resource prices, notably
agricultural products such as wheat and soybeans that have remained flat. Others, such as oil and gold, had
been so deflated that their recent price spikes have been taken more as a return to price levels that are
simply more normal, not necessarily inflated.
"Commodity prices are still at the low end of what we've seen for the 1990s as a whole, so I wouldn't be
worrying about inflation quite yet," said Jim Glassman, senior U.S. economist at Chase Securities Inc. in
New York. In the energy sector, Mr. Glassman said Americans since the 1970s are using about 40% less
power per dollar of gross domestic product -- a sign that fuel costs hold much less sway than they used to
over the rest of the economy.
Many of the latest price increases don't have to do with demand increases that might spur more-serious
inflation worries. With copper, for example, production cuts are the issue: Prices have risen steadily in part
because of consolidation among major mining firms. Gold, meanwhile, has moved on supply cutbacks:
Prices jumped a total of $56.50 since last week to $324.40 an ounce after 15 European central banks said
they wouldn't sell or lease additional gold for the next five years. Although that was music to many gold
traders' ears, economists said it had little to do with consumer purchasing power.
But in recent years, that has increasingly been true of commodity prices in general, as the price of everything
from grains to lumber has become less of a component in the price of finished goods, says Bill O'Neill,
director of commodity research for Merrill Lynch.
"If you buy a loaf of bread, I think the cost of the wheat to make it is something like five cents of the price," he
said. "It's just not the factor that it used to be." Mr. Glassman says another good example of a commodity
whose inflationary role has diminished is natural gas.
Instead, inflationary indicators such as the price of computers, which has been falling, and labor, which
makes up 70% of the cost of many finished goods, have become more important, says economist Chris
Varvares, of Macroeconomic Advisers in St. Louis.

--- Page 140 ---
Page 140 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Commodities prices now are more of an indicator of how the world economy is doing, particularly in
commodity-producing countries that have not had economies as strong as the U.S. lately," Mr. Varvares says.
"The prices we've seen lately are less an indicator that the U.S. is falling behind than that those other
countries' economies are doing better."
Economists are also quick to note that many agricultural commodities have either decreased in value, remain
in oversupply or are near historic low prices even if they have gone up.
"There's a complete dichotomy," Mr. O'Neill says. "Yes, commodities will probably trend modestly higher next
year, and there's probably some more inflation in the pipeline," he says. "But there's not acute inflation."
Unease about inflation has smoldered since the Federal Reserve's two interestrate increases this summer.
However, the central bank decided not to increase interest rates at its meeting yesterday, despite higher
commodities costs.
Recent moves in major commodities indexes demonstrate who the winners and losers have been in the
industry. Since hitting a low of 183.22 July 13, the Bridge/CRB Index has been rising steadily, punctuated by
its reaching a high for 1999 on this past week's gold rally. The index closed at 203.16 yesterday, 4.90 below
its peak at 208.06 of a week ago, says Bob Hafer, the index's managing director.
The energy-heavy Goldman Sachs Commodity Index closed at 2619.26, up 33% for the year. By contrast, the
more agricultural-based The Dow Jones-AIG futures index was at 90.916, down 26% for the year. The
Goldman Sachs index has been buoyed by an increase in crude oil and other energy prices that began in the
spring. Many analysts said those prices -- at least for now -- are better indicators of inflationary pressure than
gold.
"The gold price is going to fluctuate wildly for a little while as the mining companies and investors sort it out
for themselves," says Donald Ratajczak, director of the Economic Forecasting Center at Georgia State
University in Atlanta. "Once it finds its level, you could then start to use it again as more of an inflation
barometer."
But he cautions that one inflationary worry that is reflected in the latest gold surge, to a limited degree, is the
lack of a single currency to truly impress investors as a store of value.
"Not everyone wants to jump on the yen bandwagon," Mr. Ratajczak says, referring to the Japanese
currency's recent rally against the U.S. dollar. He said lingering concerns about the Japanese government's
monetary policy and the permanence of the country's recovery have limited investors' excitement about the
yen.
The irony is that the moratorium on new gold sales and the rally it created are to a certain extent reflections
of the governments' own recent success in keeping inflation low, says Wayne D. Angell, chief economist at
Bear Stearns. "The reason why the Bank of England decided to sell [its] gold is that the Federal Reserve was
doing such a good job keeping inflation down," says Mr. Angell, who also is a former Fed governor; he was
referring to a British government plan announced in May to auction 415 tons of its gold.
The trucking industry provides a useful example of the relative effect commodity prices now have on
companies, compared with the effect from other economic changes, said Bob Costello, economist for the
trade group American Trucking Associations Inc. He said surging oil prices have been a concern to truckers.
But the tight labor market has been an almostequal worry.
The annual turnover rate for truckers has swelled to 95% recently, forcing some carriers to spend more to
entice workers with better benefits, more comfortable vehicles, and in some cases, higher wages, Mr.
Costello said.
"What we're seeing in labor is very similar to what you'd see in other elements of the economy, even the
high-tech industry," he said. "People are changing jobs much more than they used to."
In other commodity markets:
HOGS: Futures fell the daily limit on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange as U.S. slaughter levels mounted. The
October contract fell two cents to 48.2 cents a pound. The Agriculture Department estimated yesterday's U.S.
slaughter at 388,000 head, the same as Monday's, putting this week's slaughter 4,000 ahead of a week ago
and 16,000 ahead of the same week last year. "We're killing too many," said Leo Burns, trader with Iowa
Grain at the exchange. The fear is that product demand won't keep up with rising pork supplies.
COFFEE: Futures settled sharply higher at the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange division of the New York
Board of Trade as speculators bought amid nervousness about dry conditions in Brazil. Nearby December
futures rose 2.2 cents to 85.05 cents a pound. Rainfall in Brazilian coffee-growing areas has been scattered

--- Page 141 ---
Page 141 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
during this period, and the parched soil conditions could reduce harvest for the 2000-2001 crop year, traders
said.

Tokyo's Over-the-Counter Stock Market ContinuesTo Gain, Suggesting the End of a Bear Market
World Stock Markets
Tokyo's Over-the-Counter Stock Market Continues To Gain, Suggesting the End of a Bear Market
By Craig Karmin
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,288 words
6 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Global markets turned in a mixed performance on a day when the U.S. Federal Reserve left interest rates
unchanged, but Tokyo's over-the-counter stock market extended its recent tear.
The Nikkei OTC index closed higher for the seventh consecutive session -- a gain of 12.3% over that period --
continuing to defy skeptics who believe that the market's threefold increase from a year ago makes it ripe for
a significant correction.
Tokyo's blue-chip benchmark, meanwhile, gained just 0.1% with most investors adopting a cautious stance
as the market closed ahead of the Fed policy meeting. Investors were bolder in Malaysia, where news of
possible early elections helped lift the index 4%, and in South Korea, where new charges of tax evasion
against some of the country's most prominent businesses sent stocks plunging again.
European markets, which also closed before the Fed elected to leave rates the same but adopted a
tightening bias, generally finished higher with some strong performances from the auto and technology
sectors. Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 0.10, or 0.05%, to 218.55. Excluding the U.S., the
index rose 0.49, or 0.30%, to 164.85.
The Nikkei 225 Index has surged 39% since last October, but those gains have been trounced by the OTC
market of Japanese small-stock companies. Many investors say a correction is long overdue. But recent
trends still seem to favor these stocks over the blue-chip exporters, which remain much more vulnerable to a
strengthening yen.
"Smaller companies are more closely correlated with a recovering economy," notes Eric Ritter, portfolio
manager of the Driehaus Asia-Pacific Growth Fund in Chicago. "So as [gross domestic product] figures come
in positive, people are turning to these stocks."
What's more, the recent deregulation of brokerage trading fees is expected to lure more Japanese individual
investors back into the Tokyo market, where many are already active in OTC stocks.
In fact, OTC volume on Oct. 1, the day the new trading rules came into effect, was 124 billion yen -- the
heaviest figure of the year, according to Daiwa Securities. The firm also notes that cumulative trading volume
since January stands at more than 7 trillion yen. That exceeds the total over the same period during 1990's
record year.
If signs that Japan's recession has bottomed out helped ignite the OTC rally, investors say the initial catalyst
was the government's loan-guarantee package established last October to encourage banks to lend to small
companies. At the time, smaller firms had been especially prone to bankruptcy, and with nonperforming loans
running high, banks were reluctant to extend credit to these riskier companies.
Unlike the Nasdaq Stock Market in the U.S., Japan's OTC market is not thoroughly dominated by high-tech
start-ups; manufacturing companies account for a good portion of the listings. But like the U.S., health-care
and technology-related companies have been among the smallstock market's biggest gainers.
Trend Micro, an anti-computer-virus software company, fell 2.1% to 14,000 yen on profit-taking yesterday
after soaring more than 70% during the recent OTC rally. Yahoo Japan also slipped yesterday, 4.7% to 45
million yen. But as a primary online advertising vehicle, the company is expected to profit from online broker
advertisements under the more competitive era of deregulation.
Meanwhile, the share price of Goodwill, a temporary employment agency, has more than doubled to 70
million yen following its July initial public offering.

--- Page 143 ---
Page 143 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
More broadly, Alexander Kinmont of Morgan Stanley Dean Witter said the surge in OTC stocks suggests that
Tokyo's 10-year-old bear market is over. The Japanese equity strategist noted during a speech in Tokyo
yesterday that three bear markets in the U.S. ended when smallstock gains outpaced those of larger stocks.
The Tokyo market, he said, is "getting into a change between a bear and bull market."
In SEOUL, the Kospi Index plummeted 26.82, or 3.3%, to 791.55. The market initially gained on ratings
upgrades of five South Korean banks by Moody's Investors Service. But stock prices turned sharply lower in
the afternoon on concern about a stream of tax-evasion charges and other allegations by the government
against some of the country's most prominent businesses and their managers. The National Tax Service said
it will ask prosecutors to investigate Hanjin Group for alleged tax evasion and foreign currency violations.
Hanjin Heavy Industries fell 15% to 5740.
In KUALA LUMPUR, the Composite Index jumped 27.37, or 4%, to 710.23. With an improving economy and
a tired opposition, Prime Minister Mahathir Mohamad is expected to call for elections soon. Interest in some
banking stocks was fueled by Mr. Mahathir's remarks over the weekend, which suggested the number of core
banks in the merger program could be revised. RHB Capital shares rose 30 sen to 2.94 ringgit. Multi-Purpose
Holdings ended up 12 sen at 1.85 ringgit.
In LONDON, the FTSE 100 Index closed 31.6 points higher at 6084.5, a gain of 0.5%. News that mining
group Lonmin is in merger talks with African gold producer Ashanti Goldfields, combined with a rally in the
price of gold to lift the sector. Billiton shares climbed 13.75 pence, or 5.4%, to 268.25 pence; Anglo American
added 101 pence, or 2.9%, to close at 3541 pence; and RioTinto rose 46 pence, or 4.4%, to 1089 pence. The
deadly rail crash at London's Paddington station yesterday tuesNo hit shares in Railtrack, which owns and
operates the rail infrastructure. Railtrack fell 12 pence to 1319 pence.
In FRANKFURT, the Xetra DAX Index gained 82.99 points, or 1.6%, at 5301.85. Shares of auto maker
DaimlerChrysler posted a 3.6% gain to 68.25 euros on a report that the company may relocate its
headquarters to the U.S., where it would enjoy certain tax benefits. The company later denied the report.
Deutsche Telekom shares got some support from news it would be willing to sell its 10% stake in Sprint Corp.
to pursue further expansion. Its shares gained 1.7% to 39.83 euros. MCI WorldCom reached an agreement to
take over Sprint in a deal valued at more than $115 billion.
In PARIS, the CAC 40 Index rose 1.3%, or 57.89 points, to close at 4661.13. France Telecom shares soared
3.7% to 84.25 euros. Tech stocks also rallied in response to early tech gains in the U.S. Nasdaq Stock
Market-listed data carrier Equant climbed 6.4% to 82.20 euros, while memory-chip maker STMicroelectronics
ended 5.3% higher at 78.85 euros.

Financial and Growth Stocks RiseAmid Optimism for Fed's Plans
Abreast of the Market
Financial and Growth Stocks Rise Amid Optimism for Fed's Plans
By Robert O'Brien
Dow Jones Newswires
1,353 words
5 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C2
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Investors betting that Alan Greenspan will take it easy on interest rates at today's policy
meeting bid up shares of financial and growth stocks.
American Express rose 6 3/16 to 137 9/16, and Chase Manhattan, despite some pessimism from a Brown
Bros. analyst, rose 3 11/16 to 77 5/16.
Blue-chip technology stocks progressed, as investors anticipated that the Federal Reserve won't crunch the
multiples by raising rates at Tuesday's Federal Open Market Committee meeting.
Sun Microsystems (Nasdaq) gained 4 17/32 to 96 17/32. International Business Machines moved ahead 2
1/2 to 119 7/8. EMC gained 3 5/8 to 76 1/2.
Other growth stocks also made some improvement, as General Electric, expected to report its third-quarter
results later this week, advanced 2 11/16 to 120 1/16, Wal-Mart Stores rose 2 to 50 5/16, and General Motors
increased 1 15/16 to 64 3/16.
Some consumer stocks also rose. Colgate-Palmolive gained 2 1/8 to 49 15/16, after a J.P. Morgan upgrade.
Procter & Gamble rose 2 13/16 to 96 1/4. Philip Morris, trading near a 52-week low, rose 5/8 to 34 5/8.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished the session 128.23 points better, a gain of 1.25%, at 10401.23.
Volume totals for the session didn't show an all-out rush to get into the market ahead of the Fed decision.
With 795.9 million shares changing hands on the New York Stock Exchange, the volume trailed the 891.8
million shares that changed hands Friday, and nothing near the more than one billion shares that
accompanied Thursday's 123.47-point improvement in the Dow industrials. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock
index increased 21.79, or 1.7%, to close at 1304.60.
Some glaring holes opened. Consumer blue-chip stocks worked their way higher. But Coca-Cola traded at
another 52-week low, closing at 47 9/16, down 1 1/2.
Shortcomings on the earnings front also arose. Hewlett-Packard fell 4 1/8 to 83 1/4, after Merrill Lynch said
the computer maker's fourth-quarter results could be even lower than the company projected in its conference
call Friday.
Meanwhile, prices of precious metals continued to rise, and analysts said they wouldn't be surprised to see
the rally last longer, and take prices higher, than anybody anticipated a week ago. Gold prices haven't
sustained significant gains for most of this bull market in equities. Shares of several metals stocks increased,
as well. Barrick Gold rose 1 5/8 to 24, Newmont Mining increased 3 3/16 to 29 3/4, and Placer Dome rose 1
3/16 to 16 9/16.
Meanwhile, merger activity -- something that has been part and parcel of the bull market -- contributed some
measure of optimism to the session.
Sprint gained 3 7/8 to 60 7/8 as BellSouth, attempting to thwart MCI WoldCom, made a bid to buy the
telecommunications-service firm. BellSouth fell 2 11/16 to 42 11/16; MCI WorldCom (Nasdaq) increased 1 1/8
to 71 5/8. Last night, Sprint agreed to be acquired by MCI.
A host of other telecommunications stocks perked up, including Bell Atlantic, which gained 3 1/8 to 69 1/2,
GTE, which rose 1 to 77 3/4, and AT&T, which rose 2 to 43 3/4.

Clear Channel Communications fell 3 7/8 to 76 1/2. The San Antonio broadcasting and media concern
agreed to buy broadcaster AMFM in a deal valued at $16.58 billion. AMFM, Irving, Texas, rose 1 5/16 to 65
3/16.
RCN (Nasdaq) rose 7 21/32 to 45 23/32. Paul Allen, the investor and co-founder of Microsoft, is investing
