NEW YORK -- The dollar finished higher against the yen, helped by stronger U.S. stocks and Japan's
less-than-glittering tanken survey of business confidence.
But reports in the U.S. afternoon of falling German employment, leaked ahead of today's official release, gave
the euro a late-day boost, pushing it once again over the $1.0700 level.
That the euro could show such a burst of strength in the midst of a rally for U.S. stocks indicates how
enamored the market has become with the single currency, traders said.
Earlier in the day, the euro rose to a fresh two-month high of $1.0772, but was beaten back by gains in the
Dow Jones Industrial Average.
Europe's improving growth potential and the outside chance that the European Central Bank might raise
interest rates as soon as its meeting Thursday have turned many euro bears into bulls in recent weeks.
Last week's hawkish comments from European Central Bank officials have increased the expectation that
the bank could follow the pre-emptive policy taken by the Bank of England.
Late yesterday in New York, the dollar was trading at 106.26 yen, up from 105.13 yen late Friday in New
York.
The euro was buying $1.0738, up from $1.0723. Sterling was priced at $1.6553, up from $1.6550.
As for the Japan's tankan, market players still aren't sure what to make of it.
The survey, released early yesterday, was a mixed bag, but proved yen-negative by the end of the day. While
it indicated business sentiment continues to improve, it also showed large companies remain cautious about
putting new money into plants and equipment.
But few players dared push the major currencies too far in either direction in a week chock full of
central-bank meetings.
"It's been a volatile couple of days," Stephen Jonathan, director of the foreignexchange desk at Merrill Lynch
in New York, said. "And now a lot of people are sidelined, because of all the central-bank meetings in the
next couple of days."
Today's meeting of the Federal Reserve's Open Market Committee will be followed by meetings of the ECB
and Bank of England later in the week.
The expectation that the Fed will keep the rate unchanged at 5.25% isn't having much of a direct impact on
the dollar itself, but is lending support to the currency through the gains it has helped generate in U.S. stocks.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average finished the day 128.23 points higher at 10401.23 from Friday's close of
10273.00.
In Latin America, Ecuador's sucre plunged 8.5% against the dollar as market players bought dollars, fearing
further financial turmoil will result from debt restructuring talks involving Ecuador's Brady Discount bonds.
On Friday, creditors demanded immediate repayment of $1.4 billion in Discounts. The demand was seen as
the latest salvo in what many believe will be a protracted battle between creditors and Ecuador, which is
attempting to restructure $6 billion in Brady debt.

Bond Prices End Higher as Investors SpeculateThat Fed Policy Panel Won't Lift Rates Today

Bond Prices End Higher as Investors Speculate That Fed Policy Panel Won't Lift Rates Today
By Gregory Zuckerman and Umberto Torresan
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
807 words
5 October 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C22
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Bond prices rose as investors, betting the Federal Open Market Committee will refrain from
raising rates when it meets today, took advantage of beaten-down prices to scoop up Treasurys. Strength in
the dollar, and weakness among some key commodities, also helped the market's tone.
In late trading, the bellwether 30-year Treasury bond was up 19/32, or $5.9375 for a bond with a $1,000 face
value, at 100 14/32. The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction of its price, fell to 6.083% from
6.127%.
Medium-term bonds also rose. Ten-year Treasurys, for example, climbed 8/32 to yield 5.927%.
Bonds tumbled on Friday, after the release of stronger-than-expected economic data. But yesterday some
investors saw value in Treasurys.
"The market was a little bit oversold, and while a small minority of people expect rates to be raised [today], it's
still a minority," said Bill Kirby, Prudential Securities' co-head of government trading.
While some investors took heart in the dollar's strength, and weakness in oil prices, most long-term investors
stayed on the sidelines, expecting that the Federal Reserve's policy-making committee won't raise interest
rates for the third time this year.
Long-term investors such as mutual-fund portfolio managers "tend to be cautious ahead of the FOMC
meeting," said Michael Moran, chief economist at Daiwa Securities America Inc. in New York. "Only a few
people would make a bold move" before the U.S. monetary policy makers meet.
The FOMC is expected to release its decision at about 2:15 p.m. EDT.
The fed-funds futures -- a proxy of the market's expectations on the federal-funds rate target for overnight
banking loans -- points to a less than 20% chance that rates will be raised today, traders said.
Still, stronger-than-expected National Association of Purchasing Management data on Friday have caused a
growing number of economists and investors to expect Federal Reserve policy makers to adopt a bias toward
higher rates -- also known as a policy directive.
"There's a strong probability that they will take a tightening bias, because they will want to put on record they
are concerned" about rising prices, said Carol Stone, deputy chief economist at Nomura Securities
International in New York.
Oil prices have more than doubled since the beginning of the year, while gold has increased by $37 per
ounce, or 14%, since a week ago Friday, noted Barclays Capital.
As a result, "the bond market remains fearful of strong growth and the potential for more rate hikes to come,"
said Brian Wesbury, chief economist at Griffin, Kubik, Stephens & Thomson in Chicago.

Bond Prices Slip for a Third Day Amid WorriesAbout Inflation, Fed's Stance on Interest Rates
Credit Markets
Bond Prices Slip for a Third Day Amid Worries About Inflation, Fed's Stance on Interest Rates
By John Montgomery
Dow Jones Newswires
1,048 words
30 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Treasury prices fell for a third consecutive day as strong economic data and higher commodity
prices continued to spur concern about inflation ahead of a Federal Reserve policy meeting.
People in the market don't expect Fed policy makers to raise interest rates when they meet Tuesday, a Dow
Jones/CNBC survey found. But some think the Fed might signal that another rate increase could come in the
near future by announcing it has altered its policy stance, or bias.
Late yesterday, the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond was down 18/32 point, or $5.625 for a bond with
$1,000 face value, at 99 29/32. Its yield rose to 6.122% from 6.081% late Tuesday, as bond yields move
inversely to prices.
Prices came under some pressure early on news that durable-goods orders jumped 0.9% in August,
compared with expectations for a 0.5% decline. Commodity prices also remained a focus. Although gold
prices fell, November crude oil ended 36 cents a barrel higher at $24.69.
Despite the sell-off in bonds, however, the Treasury found reasonably good demand for about $15 billion in
new two-year notes, people in the market said.
This week's gains in commodity prices have helped spur nervousness in a cautious bond market. But broad
gauges of U.S. inflation haven't shown much change recently -- an important reason why economists at 28
primary dealers in U.S. securities don't expect the Fed to raise rates, the Dow Jones/CNBC survey found.
Another reason cited by economists in the survey is that in August, after increasing short-term rates for the
second time this year by 0.25 percentage point, the Fed said that rate move should significantly reduce the
threat of inflation.
But, economists added, the Fed clearly remains concerned about the sustainability of economic growth and
the potential for higher inflation resulting from a strong job market and voracious consumer demand. Policy
makers could acknowledge concern Tuesday without raising rates by announcing they had shifted from a
neutral policy stance to a "tightening bias," which could foreshadow an increase.
"We think, on economic grounds, the Fed should raise rates," said Joe Lavorgna, senior economist at
Deutsche Bank Securities in New York, who didn't rule out an October rate move. "I don't find any very
compelling reasons to sit and wait. At minimum, that means the Fed is going to a tightening bias," he said.
Some analysts cited events in 1994 -- when the Fed engineered a series of rate rises -- as comparable.
"I think they're in the same kind of tightening mode as they were in 1994," said David Greenlaw, chief U.S.
fixedincome economist at Morgan Stanley Dean Witter in New York. "The sequence was, when you
tightened, you went to a neutral bias. Then, at the meeting where you didn't tighten, you adopted a tightening
bias."
This year, the Fed began immediately announcing changes in its policy bias, which gives its another way of
influencing rates and expectations. Previously, such changes weren't disclosed for weeks.
But not everyone expects the Fed to exercise that option this time around.
Policy makers might feel announcing a move to a tightening bias would require them to push rates upward at
their next meeting, in November, said Bear, Stearns economist Melanie Hardy. Making no statement on the
policy bias might help the Fed keep its options open, she noted.

--- Page 151 ---
Page 151 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Even though it is part of the Fed's pattern to switch its bias if it's not moving, we think the fact that it would
have to announce that may make it a little bit leery" of doing so, Ms. Hardy added.
Some analysts said the Fed could assume a tightening bias with no announcement. But the bond market
expects an announcement after every meeting, noted Ms. Hardy. With no announcement, people likely would
assume policymakers had kept neutral. That could spur a rebound in financial markets, which the Fed might
want to avoid, she said.

But trading volume is tapering off as the end of the third quarter approaches, allowing small trades to cause
exaggerated price swings, analysts said.
In late-afternoon trading in New York, the dollar was changing hands at 107.01 yen, up from 106.24 yen late
Tuesday. The euro was trading at $1.0648, up from $1.0531. The euro also was at 113.87 yen, up from
111.87 yen. Sterling was at $1.6446, down from $1.6487. In early trading in Tokyo Thursday, the dollar was
at 106.94 yen.
Yesterday, the euro rallied to its highest level in more than three weeks against the dollar and touched a 2 1/2
-week high against the yen at one point, even though no major euro-supportive economic data or remarks
emerged during the day.
U.S. investment funds provided the catalyst for the euro's strength as they bought euros and sold yen to
reverse recent bets that the European currency would weaken further, traders said.
The euro's climb accelerated after it rose above a pivotal technical level of $1.0630; it looks set to rise toward
the next crucial level around $1.0800, according to analysts. "Everyone's getting more bullish on the euro,"
said Avinash Pesaud, head of global research at State Street Bank in London.
The dollar's direction against the yen was less clear after the U.S. currency trimmed its earlier gains following
a failed attempt to break through resistance around 107.30 yen.
Although possible action by the G-7 nations to stop the yen's appreciation continued to pressure the
Japanese currency, attention was increasingly shifting to the quarterly Tankan survey of business sentiment,
due out Monday in Japan. G-7 nations include the U.S., Japan, Britain, Germany, France, Italy and Canada.
Trading in dollar/yen was further complicated by uncertainties over whether the Federal Reserve will keep
interest rates steady at its policy meeting Tuesday.
"Nobody's going to take a fundamentals-based position until they see the Tankan numbers and until they see
what the Fed's going to do," said Greg Anderson, financial economist at BankBoston.

Mercantile Exchange, after soaring as high as $321.00 intraday Tuesday.

Stocks and Bonds Decline; S&P 500 Hits `Correction'
By E.S. Browning
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
580 words
30 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Jittery investors pushed down stocks another notch, leaving the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, the
broadest measure of big-stock performance, in what Wall Street calls a "correction."
Although bonds fell sharply for a second day and the Dow Jones Industrial Average pulled back for the
seventh time in eight trading sessions, the actual stock declines were mild. The dollar continued its rebound
against the yen.
But the S&P 500, which fell just 13.83 points, or 1.08%, to 1268.37, now is down 10.6% from its July 16
record close of 1418.78. Analysts often refer to a 10% drop as a correction. The Dow industrials fell 62.05, or
0.60%, to 10213.48, leaving them down 9.82% from their Aug. 25 record close of 11326.04.
While that kind of retreat is hardly a disaster, it is a sign of how nervous investors have become, nine years
into the bull market.
"The market is in a precarious position right now, especially with the Fed meeting coming up on Tuesday,"
said Warren Epstein, head trader at Richard A. Rosenblatt, referring to the Federal Reserve policy meeting
on Oct. 5. "The market is going to have trouble rallying for a while, with any significant force."
Most analysts now anticipate the Fed will leave interest rates alone on Tuesday, but they fear it could raise
them later. Those fears were stoked by news that August durable-goods orders were stronger than expected.
In addition, the quarter ends today, and that sometimes produces unusual selling.
Rumors circulated again that big investment funds were selling stocks to cover losses in the gold market.
And talk is building about the risk of weak technology sales and technology-stock declines as 2000 nears.
"One of the fears that the stock market has is that it is over for the year and that the focus should be on
profit-taking or profit-protecting," said Bill Schneider, head of block trading at Warburg Dillon Read. "There is
kind of a cocoon mentality going into Y2K."
The Nasdaq Composite Index, heavy in technology names, fell 25.98 points, or 0.94%, to 2730.27. cqNo That
leaves it 5.43% short of its Sept. 10 record but up 24.52% for 1999. The industrials are up 11.24% since the
year began. The S&P 500, heavily exposed to weak bank and drug stocks, is up just 3.18%.
Small stocks bucked yesterday's trend and regained some ground. The beleaguered Russell 2000
small-stock index, down 0.1% for 1999, advanced 3.03 points, or 0.72%, to 422.15. Some Internet-related
stocks gained.
World-wide, stocks declined in dollar terms. Excluding U.S. stocks, the Dow Jones World Stock Index fell
0.45 point to 160.64.

Major stocks indexes declined. On the Big Board, where 850.9 million shares traded, 1,610 stocks advanced
and 1,465 declined.
Bonds declined. The bellwether 30-year Treasury bond fell 18/32, or $5.625 for a $1,000 bond, pushing the
yield up to 6.122%.

NEW YORK -- In the absence of distracting rumors about rising gold prices hurting leveraged positions held

Buying options to profit from the future direction of stocks took a back seat to worries the Federal Open
Market Committee would raise interest rates when it meets next week.
"This market is so overdone. The question is where is the bottom," said Scott Fullman, Swiss American
Securities' chief options strategist.
Jay Shartsis, R.F. Lafferty & Co.'s director of options trading, thinks the stock market has established a
bottom. He advised his clients to "go long" because total open interest in S&P 100 calls exceeds puts, which
he said has reliably marked a buy-signal for the past 12 years.
"Here's a scenario for the current market," Mr. Shartsis advised clients in a report. "We will now rally into next
week, when the Fed will not raise interest rates. That, of course, gives us a further sharp rally for a day and
we go short then. Go long for now."
The use of defensive index puts to hedge stock portfolios increased yesterday, but it wasn't enough to
persuade Mr. Fullman that investors have lost their complacency about the market.
He said the S&P 500 index found support at 1280 when the market closed Tuesday, only to open below that
level in the current session. "A lot is going to depend on where it closes. If it's below 1270, that's negative," he
said before the market closed.
The index declined 13.85 to 1268.35. The most actively traded S&P 500 put, the October 1275, gained 4 1/4
to 25 1/4 on volume of 4,567 contracts, compared with open interest of 20,558 contracts.
The option market's fear gauge, the Chicago Board Options Exchange Market Volatility Index, or VIX, gained
0.93, or 3.4%, to 27.90.

South Korean Index Plunges on Rate Concern; Taiwanese Issues Make First Gains Since Quake
By Craig Karmin
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,137 words
30 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
South Korean stocks fell sharply again as concern spread that the central bank may be turning more
hawkish.
The benchmark Kospi index plunged 3.54% as interest-rate worries compounded concerns about the impact
of troubled Daewoo Group and a fall in the price of microchips. The decline brought the loss since a week
ago to more than 9%.
Other Asian markets were also mostly lower, though Taiwanese stocks managed to close with gains for the
first time since reopening after the earthquake. Major markets in Europe were mostly flat as investors
nervously monitored the U.S. market following Tuesday's erratic session. Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock
Index fell 1.33, or 0.62%, to 213.12. Excluding the U.S., the index fell 0.45, or 0.28%, to 160.64.
In Seoul, the benchmark index has fallen nearly 15% since news of Daewoo Group's financial troubles broke
July 19. Yesterday, the market also began to worry that the central bank might disturb the low-interest-rate
environment that helped share prices rebound strongly from their lows of a year ago. Local news reports said
the Bank of Korea may shift monetary policy toward a tightening bias later this year, though bank officials
denied the report.
Even so, foreign investors were big sellers for the third straight session, and, unlike in previous weeks, the
Korean government hasn't tried to prod local institutions to buy.
"There are two things going on here," Brian Gendreau, an emerging-markets strategist at Salomon Smith
Barney, said. "The first is that investors have decided to take money off the table, believing that most of this
year's gains are probably behind us. The second is that the Daewoo situation shows that there is still a big
debt overhang that's yet to be worked out."
Many of Korea's investment-trust companies, which are similar to mutual funds, have exposure to Daewoo,
the financially troubled conglomerate that is Korea's second-largest business group. That exposure has led
many investors to seek redemptions from these trusts. The investment trusts in turn have been selling bonds
to raise cash to meet these redemptions, which has led to higher interest rates.
Further weighing on the Korean market was a fall in the global price of 64-megabit dynamic-access random
memory chips. Taiwan's massive earthquake damaged some chip factories, but more-recent reassessments
suggest the destruction is less than initially feared.Samsung Electronics fell 11,500 won to 206,00 won, while
Hyundai Electronics slid 4,450 won to 34,700 won.
A rise in the share prices of securities companies proved a lone bright spot. Analysts said they are optimistic
about halfyear earnings due early next month. LG Securities gained 200 won to 23,500 won, while Tong
Yang Securities rose 50 won to 9,600 won.
Beating them all was Good Morning Securities, which rose the daily limit of 15% to 7,990 won on market
speculation that the firm may be targeted for takeover by Chase Manhattan.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei 225 Index ended down 43.42 points at 17282.28. Traders said sentiment was cautious
and volume thin ahead of the Bank of Japan's quarterly survey of business sentiment, or tankan, to be
released Monday, and the U.S. Federal Reserve's policy-board meeting next week. The end of the first half of
Japan's fiscal year likely also slowed activity. Sumitomo Metal Mining, the day's most actively traded issue,
rose 28 yen to 520 yen amid the recent rise in gold prices. Mitsubishi Motors ended up seven yen at 540 yen
after the auto maker said it would continue negotiations with Volvo about production of next-generation
models at their joint venture in the Netherlands.

gave its approval to BP's merger with Atlantic Richfield.
In FRANKFURT, the Xetra DAX ended down 0.32%, or 16.52 points, at 5135.62. Siemens fell 1% to 78.50
euros following a Tuesday announcement that it was selling its electro-mechanical components unit to Tyco
International Ltd. for $1.1 billion, as part of its group restructuring effort to trim holdings. Deutsche Bank
gained on reports it was in talks with France's Societe Generale about a partnership in investment. Deutsche
Bank closed 0.7% higher at 62.55 euros.
In PARIS, the CAC 40 fell 8.84 points, or 0.19%, to 4535.08. Eurotunnel gained 3.7% to 1.140 euros after the
"chunnel" operator said its has bought back a package of its debt securities, at a substantial discount to face
value, which it will finance with a rights issue.

Euro Admirers Are Growing as RegionRecovers and Dollar, Yen Fears Mount
Foreign Exchange
Euro Admirers Are Growing as Region Recovers and Dollar, Yen Fears Mount
By Michael R. Sesit
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
776 words
29 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
LONDON -- Could the euro be the next star of the global currency market?
Recently, talk was of the euro falling to parity with the dollar. Now, the odds of that are "very, very minimal,"
says Tony Norfield, global head of foreign-exchange research at ABN Amro Bank in London. Although the
euro is down 10% so far this year, Europe's nine-month-old currency is well above the $1.0108 low set July
13.
James O'Neill, chief currency economist at Goldman, Sachs & Co. in London, predicts the euro will be more
than 6% higher at $1.12 by Christmas and a whopping 16% higher at $1.22 in a year.
He points to signs of renewed economic growth in the 11-nation euro zone, a narrowing of the difference in
yields on U.S. and German government bonds and other euro-boosting data. There also is the argument that
it is the least ugly of the world's three major currencies.
"A lot of people don't like the dollar because of the ballooning American trade deficit, but they don't want to
buy the yen," he says. He also contends the euro is cheap, roughly 18% undervalued on Goldman's
proprietary valuation models.
In late-afternoon trading in New York, the euro was at $1.0531, up from $1.0464 late Monday. The dollar was
also trading at 106.24 yen, compared with 105.73 yen, the day before. Sterling was changing hands at
$1.6487, up from $1.6451.
The dollar moved higher against the yen as market players sided with safety, still grappling with the
uncertainties of what the Group of Seven planned to do to stop the yen's appreciation. But the dollar fell
against European and commoditylinked currencies in a move many analysts say was fueled by the unwinding
of so-called gold-carry trades as the price of the precious metal soared.
The gold trade involved borrowing in a product at a low interest rate to fund a higher yielding investment,
which analysts speculated may have been U.S. stocks. Market players scrambled to brace themselves
against what was feared would be a concurrent liquidation, but of what they were uncertain. With gold
skyrocketing to an intraday high of $329.00 a troy ounce, many pointed to the sell-off in U.S. stocks.
Meanwhile, the euro was buoyed by a weak opening on Wall Street and a survey of French business leaders
published by Insee, the French national statistics institute, which painted a much-brighter-than-expected
picture of the French economy.
Karen E. Parker, director of currency research at Chase Securities Inc. in New York, also noted that "the
selling of eurodenominated assets by Japanese institutional investors is running its course as the end of the
quarter approaches, and that the upward pressure on the yen has been curbed in the short-run by Japanese
official comments that they may be prepared to ease monetary policy further."
Maligned, weak and deeply disappointing to U.S. and Japanese investors, the euro has drastically
underperformed expectations. Euro-land government officials and economists at big international banks told
investors late last year to expect a strong currency -- one backed by vibrant euro-zone growth, massive
purchases by Asian central banks, a European Central Bank free of political pressure and strong European
stock markets.
Instead, Euroland's economy weakened; central banks in Asia didn't swap dollars for euros; the ECB became
the whipping boy of Germany's former finance minister; and, except for France, Euroland's major stock
markets haven't been anything to write home about.

--- Page 162 ---
Page 162 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Recently, the euro has been depressed by Japanese investors hedging their eurodenominated investments
to stem further losses, as the currency weakened.
"But over the last week, things have changed: We've seen yet more economic data and surveys from the
euro area indicating a robust economic pickup in the euro countries," Mr. Norfield says. He sees the euro the
rising to $1.10 by year end and $1.14 in 12 months.
Even the lackluster performances of the German, Italian, Dutch and Spanish stock markets could have their
appeal, if only because they aren't overpriced in comparison to the U.S., Mr. Norfield says. Last but not least
stands the huge -- and expanding -- U.S. trade deficit. "In an environment of stronger global growth and
improving investment opportunities outside of the U.S., it will weigh on the dollar," Ms. Parker of Chase
Securities says.

Bonds End Lower Amid Talk of Investors SellingTo Offset Losses Resulting From Gold-Price Rise
Credit Markets
Bonds End Lower Amid Talk of Investors Selling To Offset Losses Resulting From Gold-Price Rise
By Sonoko Setaishi
Dow Jones Newswires
1,047 words
29 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C25
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Treasurys ended sharply lower for a second day amid talk that some investors unloaded large
amounts of U.S. bonds to make up for losses partly resulting from soaring gold prices.
In fear- and speculation-driven trading, Treasurys also were pressured by selling related to a $5.25 billion
issue by Fannie Mae, worries that the Federal Reserve next week may adopt a bias toward tightening
interest rates and renewed fears that liquidity may dry up around the turn of the century.
Late yesterday, the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond was down 30/32 point, or $9.375 for a bond with
$1,000 face value, at 100 16/32. Its yield rose to 6.081% from 6.009% late Monday, as bond yields move
inversely to prices.
The main catalyst of the sell-off was talk that hedge funds sold bonds -- from less actively traded Treasurys to
agency securities and other debt. Traders said they did so to offset large losses suffered from short sales of
gold futures and so-called yen carry trade transactions.
Going short involves selling in anticipation that a market will decline, with the aim of making offsetting
purchases at lower price levels and realizing a profit on the differential.
Yen carry trade, meanwhile, involves borrowing in yen-denominated markets -- where interest rates are
dramatically lower than in many other markets -- and investing that money in higher-yielding assets, such as
U.S. Treasurys. Hedge funds have been active in recent years in such transactions.
Late in the session, Treasurys losses grew because of selling by Wall Street dealers who simultaneously
were buying various securities from hedge funds. Those dealers were trying to protect themselves against
further declines in the market.
A focus through the day was the continued surge in gold prices. The October gold futures contract closed at
$310 an ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, its highest since April 1998.
"Gold has been ignored for such a long time because it's been such a poor inflation indicator. But it
suddenly caught people's attention," said Ken Fan, bond strategist at Paribas Capital Markets in New York.
Analysts said the Treasurys market also was affected by concerns about rising international interest rates.
Yesterday, the three-month London Interbank Offered Rate, or Libor, rose more than expected. The upward
move in Libor -- the bank dealing rate for dollars on deposit in London -- suggests that investors want a larger
risk premium for the turn of the century, said Robin Grieves, government-bond strategist at HSBC Securities
Inc., New York.
"People had already thought the Fed's announcement about providing plenty of liquidity {to offset any
year-2000 liquidity crisis} took some of the risk premium out of the market, but now that the Libor is coming in
higher, people are concerned," he said.
The downward spiral in Treasurys prices came despite further weakness in the Dow Jones Industrial
Average, which at one point traded more than 200 points lower than Monday's close, but ended down 27.86
points at 10275.53. Falling equities prices typically boost the allure of government debt.
Bond investors are worried that upcoming economic indicators may show a resurgence of inflation,
prompting the Fed to raise rates for the third time this year. Fed policy makers are scheduled to meet again
Oct. 5, and some people in the market believe the Fed will likely take a step closer to an actual tightening of
rates by adopting a tightening bias, or policy stance, traders said.

--- Page 164 ---
Page 164 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Bearish tone is spreading fast across the market," said Yasunori Sugi, Treasurys trader at Fuji Bank Ltd. in
New York. "We can't expect too much of good news in terms of fundamentals."
New issues didn't help Treasurys either, as Fannie Mae sold $5.25 billion in a reopening of outstanding
five-year and 30-year benchmark debt issues.
"They drew enough demand, but {yield} spreads were at the higher end of expectations," Mr. Sugi said,
referring to the extra yields investors demand to buy bonds perceived to be riskier than government debt.
Corporate Bonds
Inter-American Development Bank priced a $1 billion offering of three-year notes at a yield margin of 0.58
percentage point over comparable Treasurys via Deutsche Bank and J.P. Morgan Securities Inc.
Triple-A-rated IADB provides economic and social assistance to Latin America and the Caribbean.
Also, New York-based integrated oil and gas company Amerada Hess Corp. made its debut in the public debt
market with a $1 billion two-part offering of 10- and 30-year notes via J.P. Morgan Securities Inc.
The offering was doubled in size from the $500 million originally expected. Amerada priced $300 million in
10-year notes at a yield margin of 1.58 percentage points over Treasurys and $700 million in 30-year
securities at a yield margin of 1.78 percentage points over Treasurys.
Investors said they were drawn not only to the attractive pricing levels of the offering but also to the positive
fundamentals in the oil industry.
Agency Bonds
Yield margins between agency securities and those of U.S. Treasurys grew wider by about 0.02 percentage
point in the resale market yesterday as government bonds and the stock market slid.
Fannie Mae was able to sell out $5.25 billion of add-ons to two of its issues already outstanding, although
each eased slightly in late-day trading.
The agency priced $3.5 billion of its five-year, 6.5% benchmark notes at a 0.585 percentage-point margin
over comparable Treasurys and $1.75 billion of its 30-year, 6.25% benchmark bonds at a spread of 0.81
percentage point over Treasurys.
Federal Farm Credit Banks Funding Corp. priced $1.325 billion of three-month and $358 million of six-month
consolidated systemwide bonds. Both came with coupons of 5.56%.
Traders also were speculating about the next jumbo offering from Freddie Mac, which they said could be in
the short to intermediate part of the yield curve.

Stocks Drop, Then Regain Ground
Tuesday's Markets
Stocks Drop, Then Regain Ground
By E.S. Browning
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
726 words
29 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
In one of the year's sharper round trips, stocks plummeted in the morning, then soared back in the afternoon,
leaving investors catching their breath but stock prices little changed.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average, down 222.26 points early in the afternoon, was just 81.13 points above
10000 at its low. But it finished down just 27.86, or 0.27%, at 10275.53.
Other stock indexes staged similar recoveries. The dollar continued to regain ground against the yen. Gold
soared for a second day on news European central banks will sharply limit their selling. But bonds, whose
main trading session ends an hour ahead of that for stocks, suffered a sharper loss.
"I'm feeling like I was in a spin-dry cycle or on a roller coaster or something," said Andy Brooks, head of stock
trading at Baltimore mutual-fund group T. Rowe Price. "It was a wild, wild day."
One reason for the craziness, traders said, was that there wasn't any clear news event to which to look. They
blamed the morning selling on a change in the composition of the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, shock
at the soaring price of gold, rumors that some investment partnerships were madly selling stocks to cover
losing bets against gold, end-of-month portfolio changes, anxiety over interest rates and worries about
possible market troubles as the year 2000 approaches. Also, Prudential Securities technical analyst Ralph
Acampora began warning of a possible market drop.
For most of the day, both the industrials and the S&P 500 were in what Wall Street calls a correction, or a
decline of 10% from their closing highs; they wound up above that level. Investors, in some cases using
sophisticated computer-driven programs, jumped in to buy up beaten-down stocks during the afternoon.
Although the major indexes closed down, several stock groups, including semiconductor stocks, Internet
stocks, technology stocks in general and banks, finished the day higher.
Buyers seemed to decide stocks looked cheap once the major indexes hit correction territory. Michael Clark,
head of U.S. stock trading at Credit Suisse First Boston, said a rebound in S&P futures prices at a critical
technical level helped reassure people that the worst was over.
"The fact that we held gave people renewed confidence that we might hold into the end of the month and
possibly into early October," he said, although he added that he would expect markets to remain volatile.
Some traders decided the market has bottomed for now.
But "as we get closer to the end of the year," said Tim Morris, chief investment officer at Bessemer Trust, "we
are looking at people taking money off the table. They are locking in profits. We don't hear too many people
talking about the year 2000, but their actions may show that that's what they are thinking."
The Nasdaq Composite Index, which yesterday fell 5.50 points, or 0.20%, to 2756.25, is down 4.53% from its
Sept. 10 record close of 2887.06, well above "correction" territory. It is up 25.70% for 1999. But the S&P 500,
which fell 1.11, or 0.09%, to 1282.20, is 9.63% off its July 16 record close of 1418.78, just above a correction,
and a mere 4.31% ahead for the year. The Dow industrials, down for six of the past seven days, are 9.28%
below the Aug. 25 record close of 11326.04, and up 11.92% since the beginning of the year.
World-wide, stocks advanced in dollar terms. Excluding U.S. stocks, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose
0.32 point to 161.09.
In major market action:

--- Page 166 ---
Page 166 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Stocks retreated. On the Big Board, where 876.9 million shares traded, 1,130 stocks advanced and 1,899
declined.
Bonds declined. The bellwether 30-year Treasury bond fell 30/32 point, or $9.375 for a $1,000 bond, pushing
the yield up to 6.081%.
The dollar was mixed. Late in New York, it traded at $1.0531 to the euro and 106.24 yen to the dollar,
weakening from $1.0464 and strengthening from 105.73 yen.

Options Report:Options Market Is Unaffected by Stocks' Rally;Traders Find Ways to Capitalize on Gold News
Options Report: Options Market Is Unaffected by Stocks' Rally; Traders Find Ways to Capitalize on
Gold News
By Steven M. Sears and Kopin Tan
Dow Jones Newswires
388 words
28 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The bounce-back rally that lifted the stock market all but ignored the options market.
Volatility, an important part of options prices, fell sharply, but there were few instances of traders using
options to predict the direction of underlying stocks, a strategist at an options-trading firm said.
For example, Motorola Inc. stock gained 4.4% on heavy volume after product announcements, but trading in
its call options wasn't substantial.
The often-ignored gold sector, however, was the exception on a rather listless day when news, not
momentum, got traders off the sidelines.
Gold and mining stocks rallied on news that 15 central banks would curtail their gold sales over the next five
years.
One firm played the news by constructing a "synthetic long" position in the Philadelphia Stock Exchange's
gold and silver index. It bought October 85 calls with proceeds from selling October 70 puts. With the index
up 15.06 at 86.52, the October 85 call gained 4 5/16 to 4 5/8 on volume of 2,539 contracts, compared with
open interest of 25. The October 70 put fell 2 1/16 to 7/16 on volume of 1,033 contracts. Open interest was
604 contracts.
The option market's fear gauge, the Chicago Board Options Exchange Market Volatility Index fell 2.27, or
7.7%, to 27.27. The decline in volatility occurred after comments by Japan's central bank that it might
combat the yen's sharp rise against the dollar by raising the money supply.
The CBOE's equity put/call ratio was 0.45. The ratio is viewed as an indicator of contrarian sentiment. Using
this contrarian criteria, the index has been considered a bullish-sentiment indicator if it is around 0.75 to 1,
neutral from 0.40 to 0.75 and bearish if it is below 0.40. The CBOE's index put/call ratio was 1.03. This ratio is
also interpreted as a contrarian-sentiment indicator. The index is considered bullish if the ratio is 1.5 or
higher; neutral from 0.75 to 1.5 and bearish if it is below 0.75.

Gold Soars on Decision by Central Banks
Commodities
Gold Soars on Decision by Central Banks
By Peter A. McKay and Michael M. Phillips
Staff Reporters of The Wall Street Journal
1,347 words
28 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
It has been a long time since the phrase "good as gold" has had a ring of truth. But yesterday came close.
The metal's value skyrocketed $14 an ounce to $281.80, hitting a five-month high on news that 15 European
central banks won't unload extra gold onto the market for the next five years.
Analysts said the unexpected move virtually erased the biggest specter that has been scaring investors away
from gold for more than a year -- a seemingly endless string of rumors or announcements that government
gold sales could flood the market.
But now, after hitting several new 20-year lows over the summer, gold has made a sharp turnaround. In the
past two weeks including yesterday, the metal has risen by $24.90, or almost 10%. Gold still is below the
$300 level it traded at almost a year ago, but analysts and traders said yesterday that the metal's price could
now hit that level by year end as investors move away from their bearish futures bets.
"This is a serious change in sentiment; we haven't had anything like this in a long time," said metals analyst
Matthew Ford, of the fund U.S. Global Investors Inc., San Antonio, Texas. "This has the hallmark of being a . .
. sustainable run, because you've effectively discounted much of what the negative speculation was."
The announcement by government banks came at a time when gold prices had become particularly
politicized, analysts and traders said.
Leading the charge in support of higher prices was the World Gold Council trade group, which rallied African
officials to oppose official sales, sponsored aggressive advertising campaigns and commissioned polls
showing citizens' support for their governments' holding more of the metal.
Yesterday, council officials said the European banks' announcement could fundamentally change their
strategy in the coming months.
"In light of what's occurred in the last nine months, we've been put a little on the defensive," said Michael
Barlerin, the World Gold Council's chief executive for Western markets. "I don't sense that some of the
programs that we've been running will need to have the same tone now. For example . . . I don't think it's
going to be necessary to continue the significant adversarial role we've had to the Bank of England
decisions."
In the announcement late Sunday, the European Central Bank and 14 individual nations said they wouldn't
sell or lease any more of their own gold holdings, beyond previously scheduled transactions such as the
Bank of England's plan to gradually sell 400 tons of the metal.
Yesterday's price rally on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange built upon a rally that
began last week when the second installment of the Bank of England's auction showed increasingly bullish
producer sentiment toward the metal.
However, such government gold sales have generally been almost poisonous to gold's value. Even after
yesterday's rally, the metal still hasn't returned to its price level before the May announcement of the British
sale. But analysts said the weekend's European announcement was unprecedented both as an affirmation of
official bullishness toward gold and as a sign of consensus among a usually fractious group of national
banks.
"Gold will remain an important element of global monetary reserves," the central banks said in an
announcement presented by European Central Bank President Wim Duisenberg. "The current situation is

--- Page 169 ---
Page 169 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
characterized by uncertainty and that uncertainty by itself led to a lot of volatility and a downward trend in the
gold price."
The European announcement came at a time when bank officials were gathered in Washington for the
International Monetary Fund's annual meeting. The IMF itself confirmed over the weekend that it had nixed
plans to sell 10 million ounces of its own 103 million ounce gold reserve on the open market to raise money
to provide debt relief for poor countries.
Instead, faced with industry and congressional opposition, the U.S. Treasury and the IMF engineered a
complex new plan in which the IMF will boost the book value of 14 million ounces of gold, then use those
resources to help developing countries cut their debts to the IMF.
Analysts said the series of announcements generally reflected the realization by governments and producers
that gold had bottomed out, perhaps artificially in an overzealous response to speculation about official sales.
Amid the general euphoria yesterday, however, some analysts were cautious to warn that the latest reversal,
spurred largely by a wave of new producer and fund buying, will eventually have to rely on more substantial
economic developments to sustain itself.
In particular, the yen will have to remain strong against the dollar because Japan is such a large consumer of
gold, said George Gero, first vice president at Prudential Securities in New York.
"As far as I'm concerned, the jury's still out on this rally," he said. "This sort of event focuses attention on gold
a lot more than a short-term disaster, war or assassination. Gold is, after all, an economic haven. But its
value is ultimately more tied to economic events than political ones."
The rise in gold prices buoyed several gold-mining stocks yesterday, most notably Newmont Mining Corp.,
which rose $4.875 to $27.8125, and Anglogold Ltd., which rose $5.4375 to $34.375 in New York Stock
Exchange composite trading.
But despite yesterday's price spike, Newmont, based in Denver, for now plans to hold onto a bearish options
hedge it recently opened on 2.35 million ounces of gold through 2009, said Chief Financial Officer Bruce D.
Hansen.
"Right now we're definitely trying to stand back for the market and digest what this means in the short term,"
he said. "It's still a tough market to do anything from a producer standpoint."
Analysts yesterday expected most mining companies to remain similarly cautious and said they were unlikely
to launch new mines, as the latest gains may evaporate by the time the five-year to seven-year start-up time
for new mining projects has passed.
One effect the price boost may have, however, is higher jewelry prices, said estate jeweler Tobina Kahn,
whose familyowned firm received 10 calls at its offices in Chicago and Palm Beach, Fla., yesterday from
customers wanting to sell gold jewelry.
Usually, Ms. Kahn says she warns consumers that there's less correlation than they might expect between
gold commodity prices and the value of finished gold items. Yesterday was a different story, though.
"The average gold investment is going to be worth perhaps three times more," she said, referring to the kind
of family heirlooms her firm usually buys. "This affects the price of anybody who has any type of gold jewelry
that has any significant weight of gold to it. We're talking about the kinds of everyday items that people have
sitting in a collection somewhere."
In addition to the European Central Bank, the following individual countries agreed not to sell or lease
additional gold over the next five years: Austria, Belgium, Britain, Finland, France, Germany, Italy, Ireland,
Luxembourg, Netherlands, Portugal, Spain, Sweden and Switzerland.
In other commodities trading yesterday:
PLATINUM: Futures prices gained on the New York Mercantile Exchange, bolstered by the rally in gold,
traders said. The January contract surged $12.20 to $383.10 an ounce. "Platinum continues to be a very
strong market," said Dave Meger, senior metals analyst with Alaron in Chicago. "Well before the gold move,
platinum moved up on the back of yen strength and the turnaround in the Asian economy."
HOGS: Futures prices advanced on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, reaching the maximum exchange-set
limit, buoyed by a strong hog and pig report from the Agriculture Department, traders said. The October

Germany's KirchPayTV Cancels Junk-Bond SaleAs Investors Continue to Stay Away From Market
Credit Markets
Germany's KirchPayTV Cancels Junk-Bond Sale As Investors Continue to Stay Away From Market
By Gregory Zuckerman
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,021 words
28 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C22
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
In the latest sign of the widespread pain in the junk-bond market, Germany's KirchPayTV GmBH was forced
to cancel a $1.1 billion bond offering, the biggest pulled junk deal on record, as investors continue to shun
such bonds.
Kirch, the big German pay-TV company, will instead turn to its investment banker on the deal, Morgan
Stanley Dean Witter, for a senior loan valued at $1.5 billion marks, or roughly $900 million.
"The bond was well received, but the market conditions weren't acceptable; the volumes were below what we
had expected," Kirch-Gruppe spokesman Johannes Schmitz said.
Investors showed little interest in the Kirch deal despite yields of about 15% for the senior discount notes. In
recent months, bond investors have been increasingly averse to high-yield bonds, otherwise known as junk
bonds, amid fears of rising interest rates and nervousness about whether investors will bail out of bonds
ahead of 2000.
High-yield mutual funds have experienced net selling in seven out of the past eight weeks, and AMG Data
Services last week reported as much as $259 million of cash flowed out of junk-bond mutual funds in the
most recent week. In fact, the average high-yield mutual fund has gains of less than 2% this year, and
investment banking firms say their profits in the area are falling short of expectations.
Amid the gloomy tone in the market, few bond buyers had ample cash, or a hearty enough stomach, to buy
bonds from Kirch rated triple-C, a low junk-bond rating, by Standard & Poor's.
"Cash positions in the market are very low, and more transactions are being pulled," said William Kourakos, a
managing director at Morgan Stanley Dean Witter, the lead manager of the deal.
As many as 17 junk-bond deals have been pulled from the market, amid tepid investor interest.
On top of that, the German pay TV company wouldn't be cash-flow positive for two to three years or so, said
Dean Kartsonas, high-yield telecommunications analyst at Federated Investors in Pittsburgh.
"It's a very difficult market with persistent mutual fund outflows making for a lackluster year," said Les Levi,
director of media and telecommunications research at Chase Securities.
Treasury Bonds
Treasurys sank as stocks rebounded, lifting the benchmark 30-year bond's yield back over the key 6% mark.
Late yesterday, the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond was down 20/32 point, or $6.25 for a bond with $1,000
face value, at 101 15/32. Its yield rose to 6.009% from 5.964% late Friday, as bond yields move inversely to
prices.
Last week's sharp losses in stocks had given bonds a boost, raising expectations that investors might shift
funds into the haven of U.S. government securities. As a result, attention yesterday focused "almost
exclusively on equity-driven trades," said Joe Lavorgna, government bond analyst at Deutsche Bank in New
York.
But bonds lost favor as stocks rallied, even though the Dow Jones Industrial Average ended well below
session highs, up 24.06 points at 10303.39.

--- Page 172 ---

However, in the absence of major economic news, Treasurys activity was relatively light. The "very thin
trading volume exacerbated the downward move" of Treasurys, said Gregg Cohen, a trader at CIBC World
Markets in New York.
Treasurys drew little support from a recovery in the dollar after a statement by finance officials from major
industrialized countries meeting this weekend in Washington. The statement said they "shared Japan's
concern about the potential impact of the yen's appreciation for the Japanese economy and the world
economy."
Late yesterday afternoon, the dollar was quoted at 105.7300 yen, up from 104.2350 yen late Friday.
Traders said a surge in gold prices contributed to the negative tone in Treasurys. Gold rose after 15
European central banks over the weekend pledged not to enter the market to sell gold, except for sales that

Friday.

U.K. Banks Lead a Powerful Rally in London,But South Korean, Other Asian Markets Slump
World Stock Markets
U.K. Banks Lead a Powerful Rally in London, But South Korean, Other Asian Markets Slump
By Craig Karmin
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,348 words
28 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
A powerful rally in shares of United Kingdom banks, which investors believe are on the brink of a
consolidation wave, sent London's stock market soaring by 2.4%.
The speculation, which began on Friday following Bank of Scotland's unsolicited GBP 20.85 billion ($34.24
billion) offer for National Westminster Bank, was stoked over the weekend by reports that Royal Bank of
Scotland is preparing a counterbid of GBP 25 billion. The latest talk helped push up the U.K. retail-bank
sector 5.6% yesterday.
The rally in the U.K. and a strong opening on Wall Street set a positive tone for most European stocks, which
closed with strong gains. The Dow Jones Stoxx Index of 592 European companies jumped by 1.4%. The
optimism didn't extend to Asia, however, where most markets slumped. South Korean share prices were hit
hardest, falling by 4%, while Taiwan stocks tumbled by 2.7% during the first day of trading since the
earthquake closed the market last week.
Overall, the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose by 1.28, or 0.60%, to 214.36. Excluding the U.S., the index
rose by 1.31, or 0.82%, to 160.77.
For the second consecutive session, bank stocks dominated U.K. trading. Traders said volume was relatively
thin and largely confined to banks and financial-services shares, which were gobbled up by long-term
investors and hedge funds alike. The FTSE closed up 141 points to 6078.6, its best performance since Sept.
3.
Leading the way, shares of takeover target NatWest surged by 9% to 14.76 following a 29% gain on Friday.
Bank of Scotland shares were up by 6% to 795.5 pence, while shares of Royal Bank of Scotland soared by
9% to 13.91.
The buying spilled over into other financial stocks, as investors looked to grab potential merger candidates.
Lloyds TSB, for instance, gained 8.1% to 786.5 pence. "If you look at other European markets, you'll see that
consolidation in [the banking sector] really helped stock prices," noted Robert Law, a banking analyst for
Lehman Brothers in London.
He added that the case to be made for an extended rally in U.K. bank stocks is better than for Continental
bank shares, because British banks offer a return on equity of about 20% and a price-to-earnings ratio of
about 15. That compares with the European bank average of a 20 P/E ratio with a return on equity of 15%.
But despite the seemingly inevitable merger activity, not everyone agrees that U.K. banks represent the best
value.
"Consolidation is coming in the middle of an intensely competitive environment, so we're cautious on the
sector," said Mike Young, European equity strategist for Goldman Sachs in London. Compared with German,
French and Austrian banks, he said, "U.K. valuations look high."
While some investors suggest that a Continental bank like France's Societe Generale or the Dutch ING
Group could enter the U.K. bank bidding war, Marc Halperin of Federated Investors in New York says
significant cross-border merger activity in the banking sector remains a few years away.
He suggests buying Standard Chartered Bank, which pulls in more than half its revenue from Asia. "That's a
way to capture both Asia's recovery and the bank merger activity in the U.K.," Mr. Halperin said.
Banks, however, weren't alone in U.K. merger speculation. Shares of large drinks company Allied Domecq
gained 23 pence to 348.25 pence after rumors of a bid from French rival Pernod-Ricard. U.K. utility Severn

--- Page 176 ---
Page 176 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Trent rose by 50.5 pence to 937 pence following news reports over the weekend that Germany's RWE is
preparing a takeover bid.
But the biggest gainer in the U.K. was Anglo American, a mining and financial conglomerate, that soared by
9.4% to 3,531 pence. The shares were boosted by a pledge from 15 European central bankers to limit sales
of gold from their reserves to 2,000 tons over the next five years.
In TOKYO, the Nikkei 225 Index closed down 50.67 points at 16821.06, while investors continued to hope
that the Bank of Japan will take action to weaken the yen. Steel issues rose after the Nihon Keizai Shimbun
newspaper reported Monday morning that Japan's crude-steel output in fiscal 1999 is paced to reach 95
million tons, surpassing prior industry estimates of 93 million. Industry leader Nippon Steel rose by seven yen
to 265 yen, while NKK finished four yen higher at 99 yen. Consumer-finance company Takefuji slipped by
2,030 yen to 16,470 yen on concern about new competition. Sakura Bank said Friday it will form a retail-loan
joint venture with Nippon Life Insurance and two other Japanese companies.
In SEOUL, the Kospi nose-dived by 37.78 to 903.79, with analysts citing last week's rout on Wall Street and
rising global oil prices that could lead to inflation. Foreigners sold shares valued at a net 130.3 billion won
($107.5 million), while local institutions sold a net 36.4 billion won. Local retail investors bought a net 264.1
billion won of shares. Samsung Electronics was down by 15,500 won to 220,000 won, and Pohang Iron &
Steel shares dropped by 8,000 won to 162,000 won.
In TAIPEI, the Weighted Index celebrated its first day of trading since last Tuesday's earthquake with a
widespread sell-off. The index declined 212.21, or 2.7%, to 7759.93 in the lightest trading volume since
February 1996. In an effort to stem losses, the Ministry of Finance over the weekend lowered the daily limit
on price declines to 3.5% from the usual 7%, which means the losses could continue for a few more days,
some analysts said. As technology stocks uniformly fell, some sectors that are expected to be part of the
earthquake recovery flourished. Steel and cement stocks were up by 6.1% and 6.8% respectively.
In FRANKFURT, the Xetra DAX ended up 1%, or 53.11 points, at 5239.64. Utilities Veba and Viag
announced their merger plans early yesterday and said the deal should be completed by the summer of
2000. Viag shareholders are to receive one Veba share for every 2.8 Viag shares held. Shares of Veba
ended higher by 3.2% at 55.20 euros. Viag shares ended lower by 3.8% at 19.48 euros.
In PARIS, the CAC-40 Index rose by 55.73 points, or 1.2%, to 4596.60. Shares in spirits group Pernod-Ricard
were up on the renewed speculation it is about to launch a takeover bid for Allied Domecq. PernodRicard
rose by 5.1% to 66.30 euros. Spokesmen for both companies declined to comment.
In LATIN AMERICA, Argentina's Merval Index rallied nearly 4% to close up 19.94 to 526.48, while Mexico's
IPC Index gained 1.5% to 5055.76. But Brazil's Bovespa Index managed to gain just 0.1% to 11542.

Dollar, Euro Post Gains Against Yen;G-7 Summit Fuels Talk of Intervention
Foreign Exchange
Dollar, Euro Post Gains Against Yen; G-7 Summit Fuels Talk of Intervention
By Stephanie Hoo
Dow Jones Newswires
700 words
28 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The dollar and euro climbed against the yen, as traders unraveled some of the yen's strong gains on signs
that Japanese officials emerged from Group of Seven talks ready to boost market liquidity.
But trading was erratic and tentative, and market opinion was mixed on whether the Bank of Japan is actually
changing its yen policy. Some traders were disappointed the Japanese central bank didn't move to loosen
credit on the first trading day after the meeting of the world's largest economies -- the U.S., Canada, Britain,
France, Germany, Italy and Japan.
In late-afternoon trading in New York, the dollar was at 105.73 yen, up from 104.24 yen late Friday. The euro
was at $1.0464, up from $1.0447; sterling was changing hands at $1.6451, up from $1.6422.
The market is eager to see what the central bank does to ensure the dollar continues to climb against the
yen.
"It's only sustainable if they come through with a monetary easing, which is what we're anticipating," said
Andrew Busch, director of foreign exchange at the Bank of Montreal in Chicago. "On a technical basis, this is
random noise until we get up to 108.00 yen."
The G-7 said Saturday it shares Japan's concern about yen strength and that the group will "continue to
monitor developments in exchange markets and cooperate as appropriate."
Given the dollar's rise yesterday, the market "assumes from the G-7 statement that the Bank of Japan has
acquiesced to the United States and decided to engage in some kind of stimulus to promote domestic
demand," Mr. Busch said.
Bank of Japan Governor Masuru Hayami said the bank "is prepared to use the flexibility it has in the context
of the zero-interest-rate policy where ample liquidity is provided to respond appropriately and timely to
developments in the economy as well as in the financial markets, including the foreign-exchange market."
Some analysts detected a significant change from the central bank's statement a week ago, when the bank
said policy makers wouldn't target currency rates.
"I don't think the policy makers make such a radical shift without intending to follow it up if the market needs
it," said Ram Bhagavatula, chief economist at NatWest Global Financial Markets in New York, anticipating the
dollar will rise toward 110.00 yen and 115.00 yen.
But others aren't so sure.
"I don't know what everyone is getting excited about," said Robert Sinche, currency strategist at Citibank in
New York, noting that the central bank has merely said it will use "flexibility." Mr. Sinche anticipates the
dollar/yen rate will stabilize above 105.00 yen.
Sterling rose to an eight-month high against the dollar and its highest level ever against the euro on
enthusiasm for British assets before retreating a bit.
"There are indications that the U.K. is joining the ranks of the strong-growth club," Mr. Sinche said.
Surging gold prices gave a lift to commodity-based currencies, though the Australian dollar's gains were
limited by heavy positioning. The U.S. dollar was at A$1.5328, down from A$1.5360.

--- Page 178 ---
Page 178 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The South African rand and Canadian dollar managed more sizable gains, as gold lept nearly $14 a troy
ounce on surprise news that 15 European central banks pledged Sunday to limit their gold sales to about 400

Saturday it would abandon the peso's five-year-old trading band, after a week in which the bank spent $40
million to defend the band against speculative attack.

Gold Rally Continues on Weak Stocks, Dollar
Commodities
Gold Rally Continues on Weak Stocks, Dollar
By Janet Whitman
Dow Jones Newswires
630 words
27 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gold gained Friday, marking four consecutive days of sharp gains since the United Kingdom's
second auction of 25 metric tons from its reserves.
While the auction helped spark the rally because it showed better-than-expected demand, it was primarily a
hurdle that the market needed to get over to allow other bullish factors to lead the price higher, traders and
analysts said.
The active December future on the Comex Division of the New York Mercantile Exchange rose $2.50 to
$269.80 a troy ounce. Since Tuesday's auction, the contract has gained $14.
"The auction was the opportunity, but not the cause," said Jeffrey Christian, managing director of CPM
Group, a New York-based research and consulting firm.
"A number of people turned bullish in August after Goldman [Sachs] took delivery," said Mr. Christian.
Goldman Sachs took delivery of a large amount of physical Comex gold in August. Traders had thought
Goldman was short in the market -- betting prices would fall -- but its taking delivery represented a large long
position, or a bet prices would rise, they said.
"That was taken as a buy sign by a lot of people, but they held back because they were waiting for the
auction," said Mr. Christian. "They were a little bit concerned about that coming up."
Some market participants feared the same result as the U.K.'s first auction of 25 tons in July, after which the
price of gold tumbled to fresh 20-year lows.
Tuesday's auction was taken more seriously by bidders, and drew a higher amount of bids and a relatively
high average price, analysts said.
The latest sale was "much better than people expected," said George Gero, first vice president with
Prudential Securities in New York. That allowed participants to focus on other bullish factors, such as the
steady decline in the U.S. dollar and weakness in U.S. equities, traders and analysts said.
A weaker dollar increases the risk of inflation by raising the cost of imports, which adds to gold's appeal as a
solid investment. Falling equity prices also add to the relative allure of gold in a portfolio.
Renewed expectations that the International Monetary Fund won't sell gold from its reserves on to the market
helped fuel gold's gains, market observers said.
Rather than selling 10 million ounces of its gold reserves to finance debt relief for heavily indebted poor
countries, the IMF now proposes to raise revenue by revaluing the value of its reserves.
Separately, yesterday 15 European central banks led by the European Central Bank pledged that they
wouldn't enter the market as a seller of gold, with the exception of sales that have previously been arranged.
In other commodity markets:
LUMBER: Futures fell sharply at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange due to cashmarket weakness, brokers
and analysts said. The November contract fell its $10 limit to $311.30 a thousand board feet. Industry
newsletter Random Lengths published a benchmark cash price of $325, down $10 from Wednesday, which
sparked selling, the sources said. "We haven't been able to re-establish a level where buyers are willing to

--- Page 180 ---
Page 180 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
come into the cash market," said Neal Schmaedick, lumber futures analyst at Salomon Smith Barney in
Eugene, Ore.
COPPER: Futures fell on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, as speculative
commodity funds sold into the recent rally, participants said. The December contract fell 1.35 cents to 80.15
cents a pound. Some participants' selling was spurred by disappointment that there wasn't a sustained rally
on news of a strike at Union Miniere's Olen copper smelter in Belgium.

Mexican Bank to Take PartIn IMF Gold-Sale Program
Mexican Bank to Take Part In IMF Gold-Sale Program
210 words
24 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C14
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- The Bank of Mexico has become the first central bank to agree to participate in the
International Monetary Fund's plan to sell up to 14 million ounces of its gold reserves outside the spot
market, IMF Managing Director Michel Camdessus said.
The agency's directors are still debating details of the plan, which is part of a strategy to raise funds for
helping heavily indebted nations, he said.
Under the program, the IMF plans to sell part of its 103-million-ounce gold reserve to central banks of
countries with outstanding IMF loans. The central banks would buy the gold at market prices and then use it
to make payments on loans owed to the IMF.
The IMF's gold sales would, in effect, revalue the 14 million ounces of gold upward to market value. Profits
from the sales are to be invested in market securities and the returns used to partly fund the IMF's debt-relief
program for developing countries.
It was expected that Mexico, with large loan repayments due to the IMF in the wake of an IMF-led
international bailout in 1995, would be one of the first nations to participate in the program.

Gasoline Futures Move Higher as Supplies Shrink
Commodities
Gasoline Futures Move Higher as Supplies Shrink
By Matthew F. Gallagher
Dow Jones Newswires
746 words
23 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C21
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Gasoline futures led the energy complex higher at the New York Mercantile Exchange
yesterday, as traders fretted over falling supply amid persistent strong demand.
Inventory data Tuesday from the American Petroleum Institute showed U.S. gasoline stocks had fallen 1.5
million barrels last week, leaving stocks at 201.6 million barrels, the lowest stock level since October 1998.
Analysts associate gasoline stocks below 200 million barrels with hand-to-mouth, or just-in-time, inventory
policies at U.S. refineries before the 1998 supply glut.
Analysts shrugged off data from the Department of Energy yesterday morning showing an 800,000-barrel rise
in stocks to 201.3 million barrels during the same period. They, instead, viewed the report as confirmation of
the low inventory level in the API data, said Tim Evans, an energy analyst with Pegasus Econometrics in New
York.
While stocks fell, demand for the product was a robust 8.8 million barrels a day, up from 8.6 million barrels a
day a week ago and reminiscent of peak summer demand levels.
"There's some worry out there. We have inventory running down, good lateseason demand and refineries
heading into maintenance turnaround," said Jim Fiedler, an energy analyst at ED&F Man in New York. The
early fall typically sees refineries in the U.S. and Europe shut to prepare for peak heating-oil demand in the
winter.
The nearby October gasoline contract jumped to 70 cents a barrel, matching last week's high, which was the
first time a nearby contract hit 70 cents since July 1997. The contract settled a bit lower at 69.84 cents a
gallon, representing a 1.71-cent gain.
Concern about immediate supply also could be seen in the widening of the premium October gasoline held
over November gasoline to nearly three cents a gallon. Traders said tightness in the physical markets,
underscored by the inventory reports, is showing up in the futures market.
Nearby November crude oil followed higher, but traders said crude was hampered by what analysts called
"sell-the-fact" action after the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries yesterday issued a statement
from its meeting in Vienna confirming output cuts will continue through March 2000.
November crude oil gained 18 cents to settle at $24.12. By comparison, on a barrel basis, October gasoline
gained 71 cents, while November gasoline gained 45 cents.
In agreements in 1998 and 1999, OPEC agreed to cut oil exports by more than 4.3 million barrels a day to
stop the dramatic drop in world oil prices in 1998.
Since February, the crude market has been busy factoring in that falloff in supply, propelling front-month oil to
current prices from a February low of $11.26 a barrel.
In the past few months, a greater sense of concern gripped the market as traders worried that the OPEC cuts
amid peak oil demand in the fourth quarter would lead to a sharp drop in world oil supply.
But with November crude appearing to be dragged higher by gasoline yesterday, some analysts were worried
that the crude oil run-up may be nearing a correction.
"There was a tug of war going on between crude and gasoline. And we'll have to see in the coming days if
crude vetoes a further rally in gasoline," said Pegasus's Mr. Evans.

--- Page 183 ---
Page 183 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But analysts, including Mr. Evans, are cautious not to rule out higher prices for the energy complex. There
was a slight touch of winter in the air in New York, reminding traders that an early blast of cold winter could
propel prices toward $30 a barrel.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Futures made further gains at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange following
strong demand at Tuesday's auction of 25 metric tons by the United Kingdom. The December contract rose

also supporting gold, analysts said.
COFFEE: Futures fell to contract lows on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange due to selling by producers,

Dollar Weakens Further Against Yen,While Euro Tumbles in Choppy Trading

Dollar Weakens Further Against Yen, While Euro Tumbles in Choppy Trading
By Stephanie Hoo
Dow Jones Newswires
709 words
23 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar weakened further against the yen, still smarting from the Bank of Japan's decision
Tuesday to keep monetary policy unchanged.
The dollar strengthened against the euro. While the common European currency began global trading by
rising following Tuesday's gains, it tumbled late in New York when the market showed its bias to sell the euro
when the common currency rallies.
Continued heavy selling of euros for yen didn't help the euro's cause either.
All in all, trading in the major currencies was choppy and erratic, as exemplified by the euro's sudden fall in
the New York afternoon. The euro managed to rise as high as about $1.0540 before some speculators,
encountering heavy resistance there, swiftly turned their positions to trigger a chain reaction that sent the
euro down to $1.0410 within two hours.
But the one clear fundamental trend remains yen strength on Japan's improving fortunes.
Traders reported that demand for yen remains strong, despite a 3.4% fall in Japan's Nikkei 225-stock index
early Wednesday and market caution in case officials from the Group of Seven industrialized nations speak
out against yen strength at their meetings this weekend.
"Yen strength is structural anyway," said Kathy Jones, currency analyst at Prudential Securities in New York,
citing Japan's huge savings and trade surpluses, compared with the low U.S. rate of saving and the
ballooning U.S. trade deficit.
While the G-7 meeting looms large, the threat of joint U.S.-Japan intervention to weaken the yen has
dissipated substantially following the Bank of Japan's decision not to increase the supply of yen to the market.
Several U.S. traders noted that statements from the International Monetary Fund yesterday ahead of its
meetings did little to clarify currency trends.
The IMF in its World Economic Outlook warned that the dollar remains too strong relative to medium-term
fundamentals such as the U.S. current-account deficit, but IMF Economic Counsel Michael Mussa added that
further yen strength could stall Japan's economy.
Late yesterday in New York, the dollar was trading at 104.10 yen, down from 104.76 yen late Tuesday. The
euro was trading at $1.0445, down from $1.0498. The euro was also trading at 108.72 yen, below 110.19
yen.
Sterling was quoted at $1.6290, down from $1.6327, after falling in late New York trading in line with the
euro's tumble.
The Canadian dollar strengthened on news that Canadian retail sales rose a higher-than-expected 1.3% in
July from June, putting the U.S. dollar at C$1.4704 late yesterday, down from C$1.4762 late Tuesday.
The Australian dollar extended recent gains, helped by a rally in gold and rising commodities prices. It was at
US$0.6510 late yesterday in New York, up from US$0.6483 a day earlier.
The Norwegian krone weakened after the Norwegian central bank cut interest rates a half-percentage point
yesterday, the fifth time it has done so this year. The deposit and overnight lending rates were lowered to
5.5% and 7.5% respectively.

Gold Leaps Following Sale by Bank of England
Commodities
Gold Leaps Following Sale by Bank of England
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
762 words
22 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The same event that clobbered gold prices two months ago boosted them yesterday morning as the Bank of
England sold off 25 tons of the precious metal.
Front-month October gold futures then rose $5.80 to $260.80 an ounce on the Comex division of the New
York Mercantile Exchange. That contrasted sharply with the fallout from the British central bank's first gold
auction in July, which plunged gold futures almost $7 to a new 20-year low.
Market watchers said the difference was in the bidding patterns yesterday, which included far more offers at
or above the bank's asking price. Investors took that as a bullish signal and then sent gold futures soaring.
The latest result underscored the auctions' mostly symbolic importance to the gold market, which has been
softened for months by rumors of possible sales by various central banks. Analysts agree the amount of
British gold being auctioned isn't enough to create a world-wide glut, but they say the government
bearishness represented by such a sale is particularly important.
Or at least it was -- until yesterday.
"Coming in, I don't think the expectations were too good for this auction because of what happened last time,"
said George Gero, first vice president at Prudential Securities in New York. "The audience was definitely
surprised. Now it's a question of whether this can continue."
Yesterday's auction was the second 25-ton installment of a scheduled 400-ton British sell-off. The bank,
which had set a $255.75 an ounce allotment price, received bids for eight times the tonnage of the metal it
was offering.
But perhaps more significantly, the auction had a "scaling factor" of 58.5%, according to the bank's Internet
site. The scaling factor is the rate at which the bank could fill orders for given amounts of gold at its allotment
price after selling as much metal as possible above that bid.
A higher scaling factor would have meant that there were more orders below the allotment price, a bearish
omen for the overall market. That's exactly what happened at the July auction, when the scaling factor was
91.8%.
Instead, bidders' newfound enthusiasm yesterday caught a gold market that has been poised for a major
price decline off-guard, Mr. Gero said.
Nymex reports show that the number of outstanding gold contracts on the exchange had declined over the
summer to a low of 182,262 on Aug. 20. However, in the last few weeks, Nymex gold open interest has
surged back over 210,000, a sign that Mr. Gero said most likely meant traders anticipating the next British
auction were placing bets for another price decline.
Instead, they quickly had to reverse course yesterday.
"It's amazing," said Gil Atzmon, chief investment strategist for U.S. Global Investors Inc., San Antonio, Texas.
"I've seen gold move $6 before, but I haven't seen it move to the upside in a while."
Most market observers cautioned, however, that, while constructive, the latest auction doesn't represent a
basic change in the market's demand-and-supply backdrop. To see a sustained rally in gold, a fundamental
shift is needed, such as an announcement from Switzerland's central bank saying it has abandoned any
plans to sell gold, market observers said. "With that overhang gone then there'd be justification for a rally,"
said one dealer.

--- Page 187 ---
Page 187 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.

SUGAR: World raw-sugar futures on the Coffee, Sugar & Cocoa Exchange division of the New York Board of

absence of Brazilian selling kept some support under the market. Brazil is the world's largest sugar exporter.
LUMBER: Futures fell by their limit at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange due to selling by speculative
