Encore (A Special Report)---Catching Up With...Paul Volcker
Encore (A Special Report) --- Catching Up With... Paul Volcker
By Glenn Ruffenach
935 words
13 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
5
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Sitting at Kennedy International Airport in New York City, waiting for an evening flight to Sydney, Australia,
Paul A. Volcker, the former chairman of the Federal Reserve Board, is patiently answering questions about
life at age 72.
But suddenly, a query about the media and its coverage of the central bank prompts a spirited mention of his
successor.
"Alan Greenspan has a much easier press than I ever had!" Mr. Volcker exclaims -- and immediately gives
himself over to laughter.
"I guess things have changed since I was in Washington."
Just a little.
Twenty years ago, in the summer of 1979, the rate of inflation was 13.3%, the price of gold was headed
toward $875 an ounce, and the U.S. economy was in turmoil. That August, President Carter tapped Mr.
Volcker, then 51 years old and president of the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, to succeed William Miller
as chairman of the Fed. Neither the man, nor the central bank, has been the same since.
Mr. Volcker, for his part, would become a world figure, eventually taming inflation in the U.S. and helping the
economy regain its balance. Indeed, the announcement in June 1987 of his planned departure from
Washington set off a short-lived financial panic, one that cost the Reserve $513 million -- the amount spent
intervening in foreign-exchange markets to head off a plunge in the dollar.
The Fed, meanwhile, would come to be looked upon as one of the most powerful and respected institutions in
the capital -- a reputation that Mr. Greenspan both has benefited from and continued to build upon. Which
brings us back to the subject of a, well, prickly press.
"When the economy is as strong as it has been and inflation is low, it's not surprising that [reporters']
questions [today] are easier," Mr. Volcker says, laughing again.
If the spotlight has shifted from Mr. Volcker since 1987, his life has been no less busy. Within a year of
leaving the Fed, he became chairman (and later, chief executive officer) of James D. Wolfensohn Inc., a New
York investment-banking firm, and took a part-time teaching job at Princeton University, his alma mater. In
1992, President-elect Clinton briefly considered naming Mr. Volcker Treasury secretary but finally selected
then-Texas Sen. Lloyd Bentsen.
Today, the former banker still works six and sometimes seven days a week and travels overseas "at least"
once a month. Where once he had the luxury, he says, of focusing on a single task (managing the Fed), he
now finds life broken into innumerable "bits and pieces." He lists only a handful of his current responsibilities:
serving on a commission that's examining Holocaust deposits in Swiss banks; serving as North American
chairman of the Trilateral Commission; as chairman of the Group of 30; as co-chairman of the Financial
Service Volunteer Corps. His resume lists a dozen others.
"I'm not very good at saying no," he says simply.
He clearly doesn't miss Washington, although he allows that "once in a while, the juices get flowing when a
crisis erupts." If anything, he looks upon the capital today with a certain melancholy. That's because his own
belief in government service as a noble calling has had to contend with the political scandals of recent
decades. "I increasingly share the unfortunate skepticism held by many Americans," he explains, "about
campaign finances and other abuses in Washington."

--- Page 189 ---
Page 189 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
He says he is just as surprised as anyone at the economy's continued strength, as well as its ability to resist
inflationary pressure. And, "I never imagined the stock market would be where it is," he observes. Most
Americans, he adds, are more knowledgeable about the Fed today than during his tenure as chairman --
although too many people, he says, still focus on the Fed's reactions to changes in the economy, when just
as much can be learned from the changes themselves.
Only one other question draws as passionate a response from Mr. Volcker as the mention of the media:
When asked if inflation will ever again become as serious a problem as it was during his tenure at the Fed,
he replies, "If you have been involved with inflation as long as I have, you never relax -- never. It takes
eternal vigilance. I just hope we learned our lessons."
His health "seems to be OK." He tries to walk the couple of miles each day to his office in Manhattan from his
home ("when I don't have too much to carry"). And he has been trying, of late, to force himself to take more
time off. This year, in particular, he says, he has managed to get away several times for his favorite pastime:
fly-fishing. "Salmon more than trout," he notes. Those travels have taken him to Iceland and Canada.
Meanwhile, the cigars he made famous, their tendrils encircling his head in picture after picture in
newspapers and magazines, have long since been abandoned. "March 31, 1987; that was the date I stopped
smoking," he says. "That was a traumatic experience. But I had a bit of a health scare. . . [and] it was
becoming socially unacceptable, to say the least."
Does he ever think about slowing down? About. . . retiring? "I'm not sure what that word means," he answers.
"I half envision myself retiring someday, but not going off to Florida or Arizona.
"Some days, [I] think about the house beside a fishing stream. But that's not going to happen for a while."

Small-Stock Focus: Stocks Fall on Weak Tech and Financial Sectors; IMRGlobal, Covance Plunge on

By Larry Bauman
Dow Jones Newswires

English

NEW YORK -- Small-capitalization and Nasdaq stocks fell, weighed down by weakness in their two key
sectors -- technology and financial services.
The Russell 2000 index of small-capitalization stocks fell 2.34, or 0.53%, to 435.90, and the Nasdaq
Composite Index, at 2808.74, dropped 28.52, or 1%.
Several traders said that the choppy, convictionless trading exhibited during yesterday's session was a
microcosm of the essentially directionless trend the stock market has displayed for the past few months.
They noted that the stock market on any given day can surge higher or tumble lower, but that very few of
those sharp moves see significant follow through.
"The market goes down on fears of higher rates, and goes up when those fears are quelled," said Peter
Coolidge, managing director of equity trading at Brean Murray & Co. But outside of that, he said, "it's just
drifting."
Singer (NYSE), the sewing-machine company, which is based in Hong Kong, plummeted 1, or 35%, to a
52-week low of 1 7/8. The company said that its outside auditors have suspended an audit of its financial
statements, and that it hired lawyers to investigate problems stemming from an acquisition of assets in
Russia. The company also said its loss widened in both the first and second quarters, and a subsidiary filed
for insolvency proceedings in Germany.
IMRGlobal dropped 5 3/16, or 32%, to a 52-week low of 11 1/4. The Clearwater, Fla., software-services
provider said that it expects to post third-quarter earnings of 12 to 16 cents a share, compared with analysts'
projections of a 25-cent profit, citing delays in the start of several projects and a longer sales cycle. Thomas
Weisel, Lehman Brothers, Adams Harkness and Raymond James all lowered their ratings on the stock.
Covance (NYSE) plunged 4 7/8, or 32%, to a 52-week low of 10 5/8 after the Princeton, N.J.,
biopharmaceutical development service company said that its third-quarter and full-year earnings will fall
below analysts' expectations, citing in part the cancellation of a major contract and "significant" reduction in
the scope of another project.
Philadelphia Consolidated Holding, a Wynnewood, Pa., insurance company, dived 4, or 24%, to a 52-week
low of 12 11/16. The company said that it will record a $3.3 million charge in the third quarter ending Sept. 30
to increase net unpaid loss and loss-adjustment costs for nursing-home and assisted-living multi-peril
package policies issued in prior periods. Advest and A.G. Edwards lowered their ratings.
Serologicals tumbled 1 3/16, or 19%, to 5 after the Atlanta maker of biological materials said that shipping
delays and product-related issues will hurt its third-quarter pretax earnings by as much as $9 million, and that
it expects to record a third-quarter loss on an after-tax basis.
Burlington Industries (NYSE) slid 13/16, or 14%, to 5 1/8. The Greensboro, N.C., textiles and
home-furnishings company said that its fiscal fourth-quarter and fiscal year-2000 earnings will be about 25%
to 30% below analysts' projections. Burlington attributed the weak outlook to world-wide excess denim
capacity that depressed prices. Burlington said that denim accounts for about 17% of its total sales.
C-Cor.net surged 12 1/2, or 53%, to a 52-week high of 36 1/4 after the State College, Pa., designer of
network-distribution products said that it delivered an initial supply of its mini-fiber nodes to AT&T's AT&T
Broadband & Internet Services unit.

Mr. Coolidge of Brean Murray & Co. says that investors have fallen into a summer-long malaise.
"It's tough to get enthusiastic. There's no real sense of urgency to be in the market," he said.
Moreover, he said that with the outlook for interest rates, and thus the stock market, still in doubt, investors
are having difficulty focusing on what catalyst will drive stocks higher.
As a result, investors can no longer be counted on to view every decline in the market as a buying
opportunity.
Indeed, Mr. Coolidge contends that many investors have recently shifted to a strategy of using rallies as a
selling opportunity, a major shift from the long-standing pattern of buying on signs of weakness.
"The buyers are out there, but they appear to be waiting for a more precipitous dip" before jumping back into
the market, Mr. Coolidge said.
On the Nasdaq, declining issues led advancers 2,217 to 1,579 on volume of one billion shares compared with
911 million Tuesday.

IMF AbandonsProposal to SellSome of Its Gold
IMF Abandons Proposal to Sell Some of Its Gold
By Michael M. Phillips
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
713 words
8 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- Under attack from the gold industry and U.S. lawmakers, the International Monetary Fund
has abandoned its plan to sell $2.56 billion of its gold holdings and is instead likely to opt for a complex
proposal to revalue its gold stash to finance debt relief for poor nations.
The revaluation plan, which has Clinton administration support, will probably be considered by the IMF's 182
member nations at its annual meeting later this month.
The plan "has the advantage that it seems to be politically feasible and wouldn't disturb the gold market
because no gold would be put on the market," said the Dutch Finance Ministry, which disclosed the proposal
on its Web site yesterday.
That could provide a bit of relief for the IMF, which has been facing sharp criticism not only for its handling of
the gold-sale proposal, but also over its controversial loans to Russia and Indonesia.
At the behest of the Group of Seven major industrialized nations, the IMF had originally planned to sell 10%
of its 103 million-ounce gold stock on the open market, invest the proceeds and use the profits to finance
debt relief for dozens of the world's poorest countries. President Clinton touted the plan as a centerpiece of
his international economic policy. But the IMF and the Clinton administration were forced to back down amid
loud and unexpected protests from the gold industry, gold-producing nations and lawmakers from
gold-mining states, all of whom worried that a large sale would force down prices.
Under the new plan, the IMF would essentially adjust its books to reflect the true value of 10 million ounces of
its gold, possibly by selling the metal to some national central banks at market prices and then buying it back
again. A holdover from the days when world gold prices were fixed, the IMF gold is currently on the books at
about $48 a troy ounce, about a fifth of its actual market value. The IMF would invest the difference and use
the profits for debt relief and other aid for poor nations.
News of the proposal boosted gold prices for the September contract $1.70 a troy ounce to $255.60 in
trading on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
However, the plan failed to assuage the IMF's most vehement critics, who tend to oppose any increase in its
global role. "Congress should reject this and any other proposal to provide more taxpayer resources to the
IMF, at least until all the questions are answered about the possible misuse of IMF loans in Russia and
Indonesia," said Rep. Jim Saxton (R., N.J.).
The Dutch Finance Ministry also published the IMF's global economic growth projections for 1999 and 2000,
which the fund planned to release later this month. The report shows the IMF's increasing optimism that the
world is recovering from the after-effects of the financial crisis that began in Asia in July 1997.
The IMF predicts that world-wide economic growth will reach 2.8% this year and 3.4% in 2000. In its last
World Economic Outlook in May, the fund forecast 2.3% growth this year and 3.3% in 2000.
"The growth estimates for all Asian crisis countries have been readjusted upward, and the recessions in
Russia and Brazil are less deep than was initially expected," the Dutch ministry reported the IMF as saying.
South Korea, for instance, is now expected to grow 6.5% this year, instead of the 2% the IMF projected in
May. Japan is expected to grow 0.2% this year, better than the 1.1% contraction the IMF had expected in
May.
The IMF also now predicts 3.7% growth for the U.S. this year, up from the 3% it forecast in May. But the IMF
warns that the Federal Reserve may have to boost interest rates to prevent the economy from overheating.

--- Page 193 ---
Page 193 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
An IMF spokesman said the numbers released by the Dutch are preliminary and could yet be revised before
they are published on Sept. 25.
---
James M. Dorsey in Amsterdam contributed to this article.

World Watch
International
World Watch
Compiled by David I. Oyama
1,556 words

The Wall Street Journal

English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Europe
Russia Expects IMF
To Delay Payment
Of Loan Installment
Russia expects the International Monetary Fund to delay slightly a $640 million loan payout due this month to
allow more time for investigations into the handling of previous IMF loans by the country's Central Bank.
Accounting firm PriceWaterhouseCoopers yesterday began a new examination of Russia's Central Bank
focusing on the operations of five banks it controls in Western Europe. An IMF team also is in Moscow to
study the central bank's management of gold and foreign-currency reserves. Both are separate from an
investigation into money laundering involving Bank of New York. Russia's deputy finance minister, Oleg
Vyugin, said the IMF might well delay for a few weeks the release of the second tranche of a $4.5 billion loan
package agreed in July.
Mr. Vyugin also said Russia's gross domestic product this year would grow by at least 2%, the largest growth
in the post-Soviet period that began in 1991. Russia's economy shrank in all but one of the previous seven
years; it grew 0.8% in 1997.
Chairman Launches Bid for Pentland
Pentland Group's chairman, Stephen Rubin, has launched a formal bid to take the British clothing and
footwear company private. The offer of 145 pence ($2.33) a share, which values the company at more than
GBP 500 million ($803.8 million), was unveiled Friday, when Pentland also reported a 33% rise in first-half
earnings to GBP 23.1 million, on a 0.5% sales rise to GBP 254.4 million. The bid has been recommended by
Pentland's independent directors. Mr. Rubin is best known for his $77,000 investment in 1981 in footwear
giant Reebok, a stake he sold 10 years later for $777 million.
EU Plans Labeling for Modified Feed
The European Union plans to require animal feed containing genetically modified ingredients to be labeled as
such, said David Byrne, the EU's health and consumer-protection commissioner-designate. The move would
extend restrictions that already exist on genetically modified foods for human consumption. Mr. Byrne's
statement before European Parliament confirmation hearings Friday in Brussels came amid a heated public
debate in Europe about the safety of both animal feed and genetically modified organisms. About half the
soybeans and corn planted in the U.S. today are genetically modified; a U.S. official who heard Mr. Byrne's
testimony said U.S. soybeans are the "only practical source" of protein for European livestock.
Zurich Financial's Profit Rises 17%
Zurich Financial Services said its first-half earnings rose 17% from a year earlier, boosted by postmerger
restructuring in Britain and its Farmers Group unit in the U.S. The company's chairman said it expects to
"achieve a strong operating result" for all of 1999. The company was created in September 1998 through the
merger of Switzerland's Zurich Insurance and the financial-services business of Britain's B.A.T Industries.
Zurich Financial had first-half profit of $1.69 billion, at the higher end of analysts' forecasts. Zurich Financial
isn't listed but trades through shares of its parent companies, Zurich Allied, on the Swiss exchange, and Allied
Zurich, which trades in London. Assets under management totaled $423 billion at June 30.
Official Signals a British Shift on Euro

--- Page 195 ---
Page 195 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The British government signaled a fresh enthusiasm for joining Europe's common currency. Foreign
Secretary Robin Cook, in a speech in Tokyo, said the euro "is bringing new strength to the economies that
are part of it," and added: "If the euro brings benefits to its members, we will not let Britain lose out by staying
out." His comments were widely seen as a change of tone, if not actual policy, by the ruling Labor
government.

that could be valued at more than GBP 1 billion ($1.61 billion). Scottish & Newcastle is the United Kingdom's
biggest brewer. Greenalls owns 769 pubs and pub restaurants as well as 69 budget hotels.

a year earlier, the European Union's statistical office said. The jobless rate for the 15-member EU fell to 9.3%,
from 9.4% in June.

July 26.
-- Serbia's prime minister, Mirko Marjanovic, said he has secured a deal on Russian natural-gas imports that
his cash-strapped, internationally isolated republic needs to get through winter, according to Serbian state-run

ASIA/PACIFIC
Retail Investors Get Telstra Discount
The Australian government raised the curtain on its sale of a second tranche of shares in Telstra, promising
loyal retail investors discounted shares. The government expects to raise more than 17 billion Australian
dollars ($10.97 billion) from the sale of 16.6% of Telstra, the country's dominant telecommunications
company. After the sale of the second tranche of shares, the government will hold 50.1% of Telstra. Retail
investors are guaranteed to pay 40 cents less per share than institutions. The final price for the
second-tranche shares won't be known until after an institutional bookbuilding process that will start Oct. 5
and close Oct. 15. Telstra closed down 18 cents at A$8.06 a share Monday, but much of that fall was
attributed to the shares trading ex-dividend for the first time. A dividend of 26 cents was factored into the
share price previously.
Standard Chartered to Buy Thai Bank
Thai authorities, after months of negotiations and delay, picked Britain's Standard Chartered Bank to buy
nationalized Nakornthon Bank, the first of four ailing banks the Thai government plans to auction to foreign
buyers by year end. The central bank's Financial Institutions Development Fund accepted an improved offer
from Standard Chartered to pay 12.4 billion baht ($309 million) for 75% of Nakornthon Bank, said Salinee
Wongtal, a central bank official and Nakornthon's acting president.
Seoulbank to Get $3.78 Billion Infusion
South Korea will inject an additional 4.5 trillion won ($3.78 billion) into ailing Seoulbank to make it more
attractive to foreign investors, the Financial Supervisory Commission said Friday, after declaring Seoulbank a
"weak financial institution." The funds include 1.1 trillion won for the purchase of bad loans. Morgan Stanley
Dean Witter of the U.S., Seoulbank's financial adviser, is seeking new management for the bank.

stake in the soft-drink concern. Coca-Cola Amatil's other major shareholder is Coca-Cola of the U.S., which
has a 37% stake.
-- Thai Farmers Bank said it will postpone the subscription period for its 1.18 billion new shares until talks are
concluded with the Bank of Thailand on what to do with Phatra Thanakit, the finance company Thai Farmers
wants to cease funding. Its announcement came as the central bank continued to delay releasing details on
how it will assist Thai Farmers in the liquidation of Phatra.

Mexico Raises Bank-Bailout Estimate
The cost of Mexico's bank rescue as of June is estimated at 873.1 billion pesos ($93.28 billion), representing
19.3% of gross domestic product, according to Vicente Corta, the executive secretary of Mexico's deposit
insurance agency, known as IPAB. He told reporters Friday that the net cost of the rescue, excluding
payments already disbursed, is 732.2 billion pesos, or 16.2% of GDP. IPAB's figures are far above the
government's previous estimate of 14.4% of GDP. The updated fiscal cost stems from a reduction in the
expected recovery of assets to 20% of total assets from 30% previously. The government now hopes to
receive 206 billion pesos, or 4.6% of GDP, through the sale of assets assumed from banks following the crisis
triggered by the peso's December 1994 devaluation, Mr. Corta said. He said the Mexican Congress's delay in
approving the creation of IPAB and the restructuring of the bank-rescue program caused the government to
reduce its estimate for asset recovery. Standard & Poor's Ratings Services recently said the cost of the bank
rescue would reach 21% of Mexico's GDP.
Minister Resigns in Brazilian Dispute
Brazil's embattled president, Fernando Henrique Cardoso, said he accepted the resignation of a key minister
who had criticized and endangered the government's crucial reform drive. Mr. Cardoso said Clovis Carvalho
resigned Friday as development, industry and trade minister after comments he made at a political party
convention Thursday showed a serious policy breach within the cabinet.

compared with a deficit of $524 million in the year-earlier period.

Sweet Equity: WorkIn Silicon ValleyIs Richly Rewarding---Build a Kitchen, Get PaidIn Internet Stock and BuyToys With Your $600,000
Sweet Equity: Work In Silicon Valley Is Richly Rewarding --- Build a Kitchen, Get Paid In Internet Stock
and Buy Toys With Your $600,000
By Lisa Bransten
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,310 words
2 September 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
SAN FRANCISCO -- A year ago, Paul Hansen, a Berkeley, Calif., building contractor, put up some walls,
installed windows and put in a kitchen for the offices of a friend's Internet start-up.
Critical Path Inc. didn't have the cash to pay Mr. Hansen, so it persuaded him to take his payment in stock.
Today, Mr. Hansen's stake in the San Francisco company is worth nearly $600,000. Not bad for work that
otherwise would have paid only about $30,000. "It was really something, it was beyond what I imagined,"
says the 38-year-old, who bought himself a $20,000 race car and who is on the lookout for another "sweat
equity" deal.
"I've got my ear to the ground," he says.
Sweet equity is more like it, and he isn't the only one getting in on the action.
Masseuse Brigitte Stein, 39, landed 200 shares of Bigstep.com, a company here that helps small businesses
get online, when co-founder Andrew Beebe tipped her in stock because he was on her massage table when
he got the idea for his company. Today, her shares, initially valued at less than $60, would be worth more
than 20 times that and could be still more valuable were the company to go public. The German-born Ms.
Stein doesn't even own a computer, but now she would be more than happy if more clients paid her in stock.
"You never know how people will react," she laughs, when they are relaxed.
With stories of young Internet millionaires abounding, a lot of people in Silicon Valley are hoping for such
good fortune. Lawyers, of course, have long demanded equity in exchange for services at early stages of a
new company's development, but the trend now includes landlords, publicity agents, corporate recruiters,
Web-site developers, contractors and even restaurateurs.
Of course, taking stock can be a huge gamble. Despite lofty valuations placed on companies by investors, the
shares are essentially worthless until such corporations go public or are sold, and even amid the craze for
Internet stocks, that is the fate of only a select few companies.
Early in his career, Picasso covered his restaurant tabs with sketches. Kon Leong tried something similar.
Last year, when Mr. Leong was brainstorming with his partners about a new venture, the trio met at the Roast
Baron, a Burlingame, Calif., chicken-and-ribs place near their homes. They spent many hours at the Roast
Baron honing their idea for ZipLip.com Inc., which makes software to securely transmit e-mail, and running up
big food bills. Mr. Leong says the group could have paid cash, but that was money he wanted to put directly
into the business.
Ultimately, Mr. Leong owed "hundreds of dollars," says Soi Tee Lee, the Singapore native who owned (and
has since sold) the Roast Baron. "I just kind of fed them whenever they came in," he recalls. A stock-for-food
swap ensued.
It all began as "a tongue-in-cheek offer," Mr. Lee says. "I had been reading in the paper about start-up
companies and the valuations of companies in the Valley and how people had become very rich by stock
options and things like that, so I said, `Why not?' I mean, the most I would lose is the cost of my chicken and
ribs."
The ZipLip folks took him up on it and gave him about 10,000 shares, which were worth about $800.
To date, Mr. Lee has a pretty good deal going for himself. According to Mr. Leong, ZipLip, which now has
offices in Mountain View, Calif., has raised about $1 million and has a valuation of $6 million. The shares Mr.

--- Page 198 ---
Page 198 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Lee got for those hearty meals are now worth about $12,000, and he has since invested some of his own
money in the venture.
While swapping stock for services can help scrambling entrepreneurs who are short on cash, or in Mr.
Beebe's case, reward people who helped them along the way, the practice also has become something of an
irritant for successful entrepreneurs who know how valuable their stock may well become.
To get space in the Valley's overcrowded real-estate market, for example, some entrepreneurs find
themselves giving up shares to demanding landlords who could lease it to a competitor instead. When Max
Levchin, co-founder and chief technical officer of Confinity Inc. wanted to rent space in Palo Alto, Calif., he
threw in some stock in his company to make it more attractive to Amidi Partners a Menlo Park, Calif.,
real-estate investment partnership, which had bids from four other prospective tenants. And not only did Mr.
Levchin have to give his landlord some stock to win a bidding war for the space; he also had to present his
business plan and explain why his company was a good bet.
"They brought in someone who was well-versed technically and they grilled me to make sure I knew my
stuff," says Mr. Levchin, who anticipated a quiz from real-estate types who knew nothing about technology. "It
was quite an educated conversation."
Mike Ivancich, financial director at Amidi, said his group had never before made an equity investment in a
tenant but didn't want to miss the Internet stock gold rush. After all, Amidi's previous tenant in the space,
Internet search engine Google, got $25 million in venture capital, which put the firm's value at more than $62
million just about two months before it moved to a bigger space.
"We are more open these days to investing in other companies," says Mr. Ivancich. "With Internet stocks
going through the roof, we didn't want to miss out."
Nor did the Internet company that was willing to take a gamble on Eng-Siong Tan's Third Voice Inc. in
Redwood City, Calif. Third Voice, which allows people to "paste" electronic notes on Web sites, needed
AboveNet Communications Inc. to run its Web site -- a $6,000-a-month service. That was money the
company didn't have in it early days.
"Six thousand dollars a month, that's a big burn rate," says Mr. Tan, who is chief executive and co-founder of
Third Voice. He persuaded AboveNet to host Third Voice's Web site for $2,000 a month plus $4,000 a month
in equity for a brief period until the company got enough venture capital to pay its bill in full. That was good for
a while. But now that Third Voice has more than $5 million in funding, Mr. Tan has become a jealous
guardian of his company's stock. "At this point, I don't want to give out equity because it's becoming
valuable," he says.
But that's a pipe dream these days, when stock is almost the only currency many people in the Valley will
accept. Elon Musk, founder of financial-services site X.com, was forced to pay for his domain name in stock
because techies Marcel DePaolis and Dave Weinstein, who owned the coveted one-letter name, refused to
take the nearly $1 million in cash he was offering.
Mr. Musk "wanted to pay us in cash, but we weren't really interested in a onetime cash thing," says Mr.
DePaolis, a software engineer at America Online Inc.'s Netscape Communications. "It's all about upside."
"They're Valley people," Mr. Musk says in reply, "which is why those b---s insisted on equity."

What's More Important Than the Fed?
What's More Important Than the Fed?
By Lawrence Kudlow
879 words
27 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A8
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Now that the Federal Open Markets Committee has raised interest rates for what we hope will be the last
time this year, it's time to stop obsessing about Fed policy and turn to what's really important in today's
economy. Thanks to a combination of positive developments, including the far-reaching impact of the
Internet, the long bull market cycle of prosperity is alive and well, regardless of what the Fed does.
Consider all the good news: Treasury bond rates have peaked. Consumer price inflation is running at only
1.7% annually year-to-date, excluding the temporary oil shock. This is considerably less than last year's 2.4%
pace. Technology investment is growing at a phenomenal 40% annual pace, expanding the economy's
long-run potential. Using this technology, worker productivity is increasing 5% a year in the factory sector,
4.5% in nonfinancial corporate America and 3.5% for overall nonfarm business -- faster on the whole than the
3.5% to 4% rise in wages and salaries.
Business profits through midyear have risen 16.5% from 1998, including a 44% gain for computers and
software, 41% in durable goods, 38% in telecommunications, 30% for retailers, 15% for financial services and
12% for capital goods and health care. This explains yearly stock market gains of 31% for the Dow Jones
Industrial Average, 26% for the Standard & Poor's 500 and 57% for the Nasdaq.
Industrial production has risen 3.6% in the past year, while factories' capacity to produce is growing at a 4.1%
rate. So the supply side of the economy is expanding rapidly, leaving plenty of room for future growth without
straining resources.
Why all the good news? Because the Internet is more important than the Fed. Easily accessible, low-cost
information and increased competition, the hallmarks of Internet economics, will contribute substantially more
growth with significantly lower prices. Over the past year computer production has increased 41.5%, while
computer prices have fallen 30%. Think of it as deflationary growth, a classic consequence of long-wave
technology cycles.
Record-breaking venture capital investment is nurturing Internet innovation and high technology in general.
PricewaterhouseCoopers reports that venture-backed investments in this year's second quarter hit a record
$7.7 billion, a 104% rise over last year. Internet-related company financing quadrupled, to $3.8 billion. The
number of companies receiving funds more than doubled, to 412 from 174. Investments in this year's second
quarter exceeded the total for all of 1998.
Venture capital is the lifeblood of the new economy. New ventures require seed capital in order to be
commercialized and then brought to the mass market. These record venture-capital flows, undoubtedly
bolstered by the recent decline in the federal tax rate on capital gains, point toward even faster technological
innovation in the next century.
Now back to the Fed. Regrettably, Alan Greenspan & Co. continue to stray off the price rule. Instead, they
have wandered into the historical back alley of Malthusian limits to growth and employment. This
old-economy thinking, epitomized by the discredited Phillips curve trade-off between inflation and
unemployment, reflects the tired wisdom of the economics establishment. Promoting the incongruous policy
prescription of higher interest rates and paydowns of the federal debt, the Fed has even abandoned the
original Keynesian first principle of growth.
Growth is the intellectual dividing line in American politics. Free market supply-siders embrace it; it makes
orthodox economists nervous. Slumps born of austerity policies open the door for economic planners and
bigger government influence. Prosperity from free-enterprise growth has no need for government targets and
controls. The market itself is the most effective regulator of resources.
In monetary policy, market prices are the most efficient regulators of the supply and demand for money.
With gold low and the dollar index high, it is clear that the volume of money provided by the Fed is just about

--- Page 200 ---
Page 200 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
right. There is no need to withdraw liquidity or attempt to fine-tune the economy's growth rate. Today's growth
is real, not inflationary.
If the economic establishment continues to press austerity and pessimism, it will be confronted by a tidal
wave of opposition from the shareholders, farmers, seniors, homeowners, Web site operators, venture
capitalists, small-business men and others who make up the new investor class. These asset owners know
that markets, not governments, create wealth. They will not permit new tax, monetary, trade and regulatory
obstacles to impede free enterprise. They know that entrepreneurship spells opportunity.
Think of the Internet as an economic-freedom metaphor for our time. The Internet empowers ordinary people
and disempowers government. The Internet creates wealth, expands growth, produces jobs and spreads
prosperity. Standing behind the Net is the political power of well over 100 million investors and asset owners.
Because of this, I believe the future economy will outperform all expectations. The Dow Jones Industrial
Average will reach 15000, then 30000, then 50000 and higher. Believe it or not, the Internet is more important
than the Fed.
---
Mr. Kudlow is chief economist at Schroder & Co. and at CNBC.com, and author of "American Abundance:
The New Economic and Moral Prosperity" (Forbes, 1998).

Laundering Probe Could Mean Trouble for Gore, IMF
Politics & Policy
Laundering Probe Could Mean Trouble for Gore, IMF
By Bob Davis
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,360 words
26 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A20
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
WASHINGTON -- Al Gore and the International Monetary Fund are among those who have the most to lose
as a result of an alleged scheme by Russian mobsters to sneak billions of dollars into U.S. accounts.
Vice President Gore's vulnerability comes by virtue of his co-chairmanship with Russia's prime minister of a
commission that oversees U.S.-Russian relations. The IMF is on the hot seat because of allegations that
$200 million of its funds may have been diverted into the banking accounts of Russia's organized-crime
families.
The money-laundering questions deepen an already bitter debate about whether Clinton administration and
IMF policies worsened Moscow's post-Cold War plight, and thus should be blamed for "losing" Russia.
Foreign-policy issues rarely dominate presidential campaigns. But billionaire Republican presidential
candidate Steve Forbes, who has the financial wherewithal to spend millions of dollars on television
advertising, says he intends to put Russia front and center in the coming campaign.
"Gore has been the point man with Russia," says Mr. Forbes. "His policies have been an unmitigated
disaster. They have piled billions of dollars into the hands of kleptomaniacs."
Texas Gov. George W. Bush's campaign is more circumspect -- in part because Mr. Bush's father, former
President George Bush, has been criticized for being ineffective in helping Russia reform itself after the
breakup of the Soviet Union.
"The Bush administration had one year in office after the collapse of the Soviet Union," says Condoleezza
Rice, a former Bush-administration Russia expert who is now the top foreign-policy adviser to Mr. Bush's son.
"This administration has had seven. The real question is what lessons should we learn from policy mistakes
that have been made."
The allegations that U.S. banks helped launder billions of dollars for Russian criminals or that the Russians
got their hands on IMF funds haven't been proved. Investigators say they are looking into both charges.
"The vice president's relationship with world leaders is important to the country, the national security and the
conduct of foreign policy," says Marla Romash, deputy chairman of Mr. Gore's campaign.
But Mr. Gore's defense of his actions concerning the possible money-laundering scheme and diversion of
IMF funds is quite different -- and much more open to attack. His aides say he was out of the loop.
The vice president wasn't ever briefed on the money-laundering probe, says a senior Gore aide. To the extent
that Mr. Gore discussed money laundering with the Russians, he adds, the vice president acted at the behest
of the Treasury Department. "The vice president followed the advice he receives from the Treasury when
counseling Russia on the IMF," the aide says.
Senior aides to Mr. Gore and the administration say that the vice president dealt only twice with
money-laundering issues during meetings of the binational commission on Russia-U.S. relations. In January
1997, Mr. Gore urged Mr. Chernomyrdin to improve Russia's crime-fighting capabilities so Russia could join
international organizations that try to thwart money laundering.
Last month, he urged a new prime minister, Sergei Stepashin, to cooperate with outside audits of Russian
finances that were being conducted at the behest of the IMF and to publish the results. "The vice president
wasn't sitting on foreknowledge that the Russians may have been diverting money from the IMF," says Mr.
Gore's aide.

--- Page 202 ---
Page 202 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But Mr. Forbes, the Republican candidate, dismisses the explanation, in what is likely to be a standard
refrain: "If a man in his position can't accept responsibility or ask questions, he ought to throw in thetowel."
For the IMF, the risk is that a Congress already skeptical of its competence will turn more broadly against the
institution. That would make it more difficult for the Treasury to win approval of IMF-related proposals, such
as a plan for the IMF to sell between $1.5 billion and $3 billion in gold to help reduce debt in the world's
poorest nations.
Already, an odd amalgam of lawmakers oppose the deal, including House Majority Leader Richard Armey of
Texas, a longtime IMF foe, and several dozen members of the Black Congressional Caucus, who fear that
gold sales could harm gold producers in South Africa. The campaign for gold sales appears "to be lost,"
says Barney Frank, a Massachusetts Democrat who sits on the House Banking Committee. Treasury officials
say they are working on new proposals involving the IMF gold that wouldn't affect market prices.
Last fall, after a lengthy battle, Congress approved a $17.9 billion contribution to the fund's reserves. While
the IMF isn't asking for more money now, it usually makes such requests every five years or so. As a result, it
is crucial politically for the fund to maintain good relations with Congress. Other nations won't make
contributions unless they know the U.S. will put up its share.
But the suspicion of massive Russian money laundering is causing some of the IMF's supporters to rethink
how the U.S. should deal with the fund. House Banking Chairman Jim Leach, an Iowa Republican, says it
may make sense for the U.S. to press the IMF to direct its Russian lending to Russia's provinces or to the
other states that once made up the Soviet Union. "There's an issue of how the IMF can or should give
assistance to Russia this time," he says.
Mr. Leach plans to hold hearings next month on the money-laundering allegations and call top regulators. It is
unclear yet whether the committee will ask bankers to testify, including any from Bank of New York Co.,
which is at the center of the probe. Depending on the outcome of congressional and law-enforcement
investigations, bankers may face stiffer regulations. "The principal issue is not whether Bank of New York has
broken our laws," Mr. Leach says, "but whether they facilitated in the impoverishing of Russian people."
For its part, the IMF is continuing its pressure on Russia to tighten its financial practices. A $640 million loan
to Russia in July was transferred to one IMF account from another -- and never passed through Russia. The
proceeds were used to help Moscow repay other IMF loans. Before the IMF approves a second $640 million
payment at the end of September, PricewaterhouseCoopers is supposed to finish a new audit of the central
bank's relationships with a number of offshore entities it controls. PricewaterhouseCoopers has previously
completed two audits.
In addition, IMF officials are looking into a plan that would use Russia as a model for other loan programs.
For instance the IMF might insist that borrowers keep more of their loans within the IMF rather than disburse
the money to the central banks of borrowing countries.
IMF loans are generally used to bulk up a borrowing nation's reserves and to assure investors there are
adequate funds in case they want to change local currencies into dollars. But the most successful IMF
programs accomplish this by soothing fears and forestalling capital flight; little of the IMF money actually gets
spent. So, some IMF officials argue, loans could be kept at the IMF and accomplish the same goals. That
would greatly reduce the chances that a nation's central bank or commercial borrowers could misuse IMF
funds.
However it is far from clear that the IMF borrowers, who already chafe at IMF supervision, would go along
with such a proposal -- or whether the IMF's senior management would even dare to present the proposal to
the fund's 182 members.

Energy Prices Slip as Bret Causes Little Damage
Commodities
Energy Prices Slip as Bret Causes Little Damage
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
762 words
25 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C15
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Crude-oil and natural-gas prices fell after it became clear Hurricane Bret hadn't stifled drilling in the Gulf of
Mexico past the weekend, as feared.
Reports filtering from energy producers depicted only brief interruptions on Saturday, when Bret passed
through the Gulf with force similar to Hurricane Andrew, which devastated South Florida in 1992. But when
drill crews, like most Texans, returned to the areas they had evacuated, they found surprisingly little damage,
analysts said.
Front-month crude oil fell 37 cents on the news to $21.47 a barrel on the New York Mercantile Exchange, and
natural gas declined 0.5 cent to $3.059 per billion BTU's after rising more than 30 cents in the days leading
up to Bret, the worst hurricane to hit Texas in 20 years.
Traders described the setback as a relatively minor one for crude oil, which has generally been rallying in
recent weeks. But they remain more concerned about the prospects for natural gas, which remains near
historic highs for this time of year despite strong supply and a premium based almost entirely on storm
damage that didn't materialize.
The nation's natural-gas stocks are about 5.6% below their year-ago levels, which has led traders to bid up
prices recently. However, those production numbers are skewed by last year's record yield, so there is no
true shortage, said Guy Gleichmann, senior trader for Futures Trading Group, Fort Lauderdale, Fla.
"It's like a mother constantly going to the kitchen to make sure that there's enough milk for the kids. But
there's plenty in the fridge," Mr. Gleichmann said.
For oil, the outlook should remain bullish as long as the Organization of Petroleum Exporting Countries sticks
to its planned schedule of production cuts, said John Kilduff, senior vice president for energy risk
management at Fimat USA.
"People are just taking back some of their gains today, but in the long run, this doesn't change much," he said
of yesterday's market activity.
Also weighing on energy markets was the announcement by Germany that it planned to sell 34 million barrels
of its crude-oil reserves. Combined with the lack of damage from Bret, that announcement helped continue
oil's recent history of not maintaining price levels above $22 a barrel, according to energy analyst James R.
Steel of Refco.
Energy firms Conoco Inc., Apache Corp., Costal Corp. and BP Amoco all reported they had returned staff to
their drilling rigs after brief delays Saturday. Two of the companies estimated they had written off a combined
140 million cubic feet of natural-gas production for the day, which Mr. Gleichmann characterized as a minimal
loss.
"Overall, this was a bear day in a bull market," Mr. Steel said of the energy complex. "But I don't see it as a
pullback of any kind."
In other commodities markets:
COPPER: The September copper contract rose 1.65 cents on the Comex division of Nymex to 75.55 cents a
pound as consolidation among copper producers and recovering Asian demand continue to buoy the metal's
price, analysts said. In particular, the prospect of Phelps Dodge Inc. buying smaller competitors Cyprus Amax
Minerals Co. and Asarco Inc. would be a most-bullish scenario for investors, said John E. Gross, publisher of
the weekly Copper Journal. "Copper is a cost-intensive commodity, and it is the low-cost producer who does

--- Page 205 ---
Page 205 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
best at the end of the day," he said. "That's Phelps Dodge in this situation." The two smaller firms have also
proposed merging with one another at a time when copper prices remain almost 25% of what they were just
two years ago, before the Asian financial crisis.
GOLD: August gold prices fell $1.50 to a 20-year low of $253.50 an ounce on Comex. Large gold-carry
trades, heavy producer selling, and the scheduled sale of more British central-bank gold on Sept. 21
continue to weigh on the market, said George Gero, first vice president at Prudential Securities in New York.
"Everybody knows another 8,000 contracts will be sold the day of the auction, so there are some people
taking advantage of that public information by selling now," he said. He said the number of gold contracts
outstanding on Comex has declined almost 5,000 recently, a signal that many investors are liquidating their
positions, effectively getting out of the market.

Gold Drops Amid Commodities-Fund Selling
Commodities
Gold Drops Amid Commodities-Fund Selling
By Nicholas Elliott
Dow Jones Newswires
652 words
24 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Gold futures fell at the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange as a burst of selling by
speculative commodities funds wiped out gains from a recent interlude of bullishness.
Although traders said the funds were the immediate cause of price weakness, they said the market remains
hobbled by longer-term bearish factors, including large gold-carry trades, producer selling and the next Bank
of England auction in September.
The December Comex contract declined $3.40 to $256.20 a troy ounce.
"It was pretty much all fund-related selling," said Tim Gardiner, head of precious metals at Mitsui USA in New
York. "There was also the tail end of some people bailing out of long positions built over the last couple of
weeks."
Comex gold rallied in the first part of last week, reaching $264.70 an ounce on Wednesday, which traders
said resulted in more long positions, or bets that prices would rise.
Six-month lease rates for goldcharged by bullion banks for loans of the metal for that period -- were being
quoted yesterday at around 4%, up from a typical level under 1%. Traders saw that as evidence of more
gold-carry trades, which involve borrowing gold to sell it, then investing the cash in other assets.
"Not only do we have producer selling, central-bank selling and leaseholder selling, we don't have
investment buying," noted George Gero, first vice president at Prudential Securities in New York.
Australian producers were selling last week, as a weakening of the Australian dollar enabled them to lock in
prices at a higher level in U.S. dollar terms.
A trader on the Comex floor said the market faces more such selling by producers to protect themselves
against price weakness, because of the higher earnings reported by companies engaging in such "hedging."
His concern, endorsed by Mr. Gero, was that producer selling would accompany new production coming on
line from Latin America.
"The biggest worry is who's going to [buy] the excess," Mr. Gero said.
Traders in Japan said earlier that demand for physical gold was supporting prices around $256, but U.S.
traders said the market seemed set to test lower levels.
Another source of bearishness was the Sept. 21 Bank of England auction of 25 metric tons of gold, given that
prices fell $6 in wake of the first such auction in July. The United Kingdom plans to sell 415 metric tons of the
815 tons it holds in reserves.
Mr. Gardiner of Mitsui said trading at the Tokyo Commodity Exchange today could be important because of a
large number of long positions that might be sold, as participants try to minimize losses.
In other commodities markets:
HOGS: Futures on hogs rose on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange in response to strong buying by packers
in the cash market, analysts said. The October contract added 1.77 cents to 46.42 cents a pound. Dan
Vaught, analyst with A.G. Edwards & Sons in St. Louis, said, "The cash market is what's driving this" rally in
futures. He added that "there's also some spillover from the pork market," referring to gains in CME pork-belly
futures.

--- Page 207 ---
Page 207 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
COTTON: Futures slipped on the New York Cotton Exchange division of the New York Board of Trade
because of rains in cotton-growing areas in the Southeast, traders said. The October contract dropped 1.1
cent to 51.13 cents a pound. "There were some beneficial rains in the Southeast, relieving some of the recent
heat stress in those areas," said Ray Streker, a trader at LFG Inc., a commission house in New York. "And
even with the hurricane [Bret] hitting some of the crop areas, a good chunk of cotton has already been
harvested, so nobody got scared."

EUROPE/AFRICA
WIND Group
To Retry Ousting
Telekom Directors
WIND Telecommunicazioni will try again next month to oust two board members representing partner
Deutsche Telekom, as the fallout from the German group's failed attempt to merge with Telecom Italia
continues to spread. WIND, the fixed and mobile-telecommunications group controlled by Italian electric utility
ENEL, Deutsche Telekom and France Telecom, has been under serious strain since Deutsche Telekom tried
last spring to merge with the group's main competitor. Because of that move, WIND and France Telecom
have said they consider their partnership with the Germans effectively finished, and have been looking to oust
Deutsche Telekom's two representatives from WIND's board.
WIND said Friday that it has called a shareholders meeting for Sept. 14 or 15 to expel the German group's
two board members. WIND made an attempt in July to dismiss the directors, but was blocked from doing so
because of technical reasons stemming from a decision in a German court regarding a complaint Deutsche
Telekom had filed to block such a move. Now WIND will base its ouster attempt on an Italian law allowing for
the disenfranchisement of board members who have allegedly failed to fulfill their responsibilities to a
company. WIND claims that a negative vote by ENEL, which holds 51% of WIND, is sufficient to expel the
two board members.
StoraEnso to Shed Power Assets
Nordic paper group StoraEnso said it will sell power assets with an estimated market value of two billion
euros ($2.13 billion) and vowed to explore further expansion in Europe and Asia. The Finnish-Swedish paper
group said it wants to focus on producing publication papers, fine papers and packaging boards and shed
power assets not located at its Nordic paper mills, about half of all its electrical power operations. The
announcement sent the company's share price climbing 4.95% Friday in Stockholm, to 116.5 kronor ($14.18).
StoraEnso's move is part of a painstaking consolidation in the paper industry, where, in the wake of financial
turmoil in Asia, several big competitors are trying to ease their perennial struggles with overcapacity and
reduce wide swings in paper and pulp prices.
No Need for Rate Move, Official Says
Edgar Meister, directorate member of Deutsche Bundesbank, doesn't see the need for an interest rate move
by the European Central Bank in the near future. "In comparison to the U.S., where signs of a slight upward
interest rate correction are increasing amid continuing robust growth and the danger of inflationary tensions,
the situation in Europe is still relatively relaxed," he said. Mr. Meister doesn't hold any policy-making post on
the ECB's governing council, but acts as an adviser to Bundesbank's president, Hans Tietmeyer, who sits on
the ECB's governing council. Separately, Mr. Tietmeyer said the euro is on the right path, and that he is
"altogether very satisfied" with the development of Europe's common currency. He also said the internal
stability of the euro is as solid as was the mark in its best times.

ASIA/PACIFIC
Trade Authorities Halt Hitachi Exports
Hitachi Electronics said the Ministry of International Trade and Industry was punishing it for a violation of
Japan's foreign-exchange and foreign-trade laws by ordering a halt in all of the company's exports for 15
days. Hitachi Electronics said it saw the matter as extremely serious, and already had taken exhaustive
measures aimed at preventing a recurrence. In February, police arrested Isao Sasaki, a former managing
director of Ryokosha KK, on suspicion that Ryokosha exported Hitachi Electronics' precision-measuring
devices to China without permission from the trade ministry. Japanese trade law requires that exporters
receive official approval before selling such sensitive equipment overseas. Ryokosha, a wholesaler of
precision instruments, receives orders for Hitachi's products, which it then sells both domestically and abroad.
Seoul Court All But Acquits Ex-Officials
A South Korean court all but acquitted former Finance Minister Kang Kyong Shik and another ex-official, who
were charged with negligence of duty and abuse of power over the country's worst economic crisis in nearly
half a century. The Seoul District Court's decision on the negligence charges clears the men of responsibility
in the economic collapse that led once-highflying South Korea to ask for a $58.35 billion bailout from the
International Monetary Fund in December 1997. Mr. Kang was found guilty of charges that he abused power
to pressure banks to give loans to troubled companies, but on those charges the court decided to suspend
sentencing for two years, which means Mr. Kang will be pardoned if he commits no other crime in that time.
Kim In Ho, chief economic secretary to the president during the financial crisis, who also was on trial for the
same charges, received the same verdict.
Taiwan's Recovery Is Firmly on Track
Taiwan said its stronger-than-expected economic recovery is firmly on track, as foreign demand for
electronics continues to drive exports. A rebounding stock market and recent compromises by the
government to rescue two large construction projects also will boost the economy, which has been lagging
recently but is seen growing at a faster pace next year, economists said. Taiwan raised its 1999 forecast for
gross domestic product growth to 5.74%, from 5.07%.

THE AMERICAS
Premier of British Columbia Resigns
British Columbia Premier Glen Clark resigned Saturday following the release of a court document alleging he
used his influence to help a friend obtain a casino license. The premier's resignation, which culminates a
series of missteps for his provincial New Democrat party, casts further doubt on the future of one of the last
remaining socialist governments in North America. Mr. Clark's political popularity has nose-dived in the past
two years as mounting government deficits and huge cost overruns on a government-funded ferry project
have compounded the province's economic problems. Mr. Clark strenuously denied the allegations against
him and predicted he will be exonerated. No criminal charges have been brought against him. Dan Miller, the
British Columbia deputy premier, was named acting premier.
Venezuelan Devaluation Worries Ease
The rebound in oil prices reduced expectations of a forced devaluation of Venezuela's bolivar, the Congress's
Economic and Financial Assessment Office said in a report for the first half of the year. The rebound has
meant not only an increase in foreign reserves -- along with interest rates, a principal instrument in the

--- Page 210 ---
Page 210 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
defense of a currency -- but reduced inflation expectations, given that devaluations often fuel inflation. The
office figured that $5.5 billion left the country during the past year and a half, with about $400 million of that
occurring in the first semester of this year. Venezuelans are taking money offshore not just because of
devaluation worries, but also because of an uncertain political and economic outlook. The rebound also
sustains the overvaluation of the bolivar, estimated at 30% to 50%. The currency closed at 616 pesos per

Peru's military took control Friday of the highland Andean city of Huaraz to halt a two-day protest against two
nearby Canadian mining companies. Schools, public buildings and most businesses stayed shut, and public
transportation remained at a standstill that morning, despite the removal of stone barricades and burnt tires
from the roads. Thousands took to the streets Thursday at the behest of Mayor Waldo Rios to demand a
greater share of jobs and profits from the Antamina copper-zinc mine and Barrick Gold's Pierina gold mine. A
Barrick official said the mayor's complaint is with the central government, not the mine. Antamina officials
declined to comment.

The Fed's Annual Itch
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
The Fed's Annual Itch
847 words
23 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Federal Reserve gurus convene tomorrow to decide monetary policy, just before packing their bags for their
annual retreat in the Tetons. The scene seems weirdly familiar. As summer draws to a close, the economy is
growing smartly, thank you. Inflation has made itself so scarce most analysts can barely find it. But what's
emanating from the Fed is an itch to tighten, sending the bond markets into a shimmy. Wall Street is holding
its collective breath for fear the Fed's Open Market Committee might raise interest rates because everything
is going too well.
Haven't we been here before? Yes, just a year ago. In his July 1998 testimony to Congress, Chairman
Greenspan warned: "Should pressures on labor resources begin to show through more impressively in cost
increases, policy action may need to counter any associated tendency for prices to accelerate, before it
undermines this extraordinary expansion." This July's testimony brought similar talk, with Mr. Greenspan's
warning that the Fed would have to act "promptly and forcefully" if the lively job market led to any signs of
rising prices.
Last time around, with the Jackson Hole meeting in progress, we wrote "Time to Relax, Alan," pointing to
falling sensitive prices and even an inverted yield curve -- the classic interest-rate sign of recession, with
short rates climbing above long rates. Returning from Wyoming, the Fed could see the Asian crisis about to
penetrate the U.S. markets. That fall Mr. Greenspan went on not to tighten but to ease interest rates thrice --
in our estimation averting a nasty world recession.
The most overt reason for the current tightening talk, of course, was the Fed's small tightening in June. It
went ahead to boost its target rate on Federal Funds by one-quarter, despite the surprise of a totally flat
consumer price report. It did add some verbiage suggesting it didn't really mean it: "Owing to the uncertain
resolution of the balance of conflicting forces in the economy going forward, the FOMC has chosen to adopt a
directive that includes no predilection about near-term policy action." Not surprisingly, markets are confused.
As the gurus weigh whether to tighten further, we only hope they look over their shoulders at their own
experience. In particular, last fall they also itched to tighten, but suddenly found themselves instead easing
aggressively. Where is all the resulting inflation?
If the Fed's main job is to keep the price level steady, the result has been one of the most stunningly
successful years in generations. Inflation year-on-year, since that fretful July of 1998, has been an official
2.1%. Given the upward bias of Consumer Price Index measurements, the real number may be practically
zero.
The good news about low inflation stretches right up to last week's CPI numbers for July. Were price level a
piece of furniture at the Fed, it would still look steady enough for board members to safely rest their coffee
cups upon it at tomorrow's meeting. Insofar as gold remains an indicator, it signals -- if anything -- deflation.
Commodities generally, after a dip this past year, are about where they were this time last year. Another
interesting indicator, the spread between inflation-indexed Treasury bonds and non-indexed T-bonds, has
been holding steady -- again, suggesting no inflation. Bank lending -- which enlarges the money supply and
is therefore the kind of thing that unlike jobs, or growth, really can lead to inflation -- has been steady since
last October. There just aren't a lot of credible warning flags out there.
Vigilance against inflation is a useful thing. But it works best when grounded in good sense about how the
world works; instead, the air is now filled with theories that have no business on the road in today's
fast-moving global markets. Will Mr. Greenspan's team ever get over the much-discredited Phillips Curve
notion that the way to prevent inflation is to take away jobs and trash growth? Over at Larry Summers's
Treasury, the latest brainstorm is that paying off government debt is an elixir for low interest rates, though of
course rates plummeted as debt accumulated in the 1980s. At the end of a hot, sticky summer in our nation's
capital, patience and cool reasoning can be hard to come by.

--- Page 212 ---
Page 212 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Happily, the air is clearer in the Tetons. We hope Mr. Greenspan and friends delay anything dramatic until
they have had a chance to take a few deep breaths. So our advice is to enjoy the mountains. Maybe by the
time next month's inflation numbers start rolling in, there will be something to worry about. Or it may just turn
out that this year's fret over inflation on the horizon has been about as pointless as last year's -- that things
are fine. In which case the Fed will be in a good spot to congratulate itself that instead of choking growth, it
has begun to learn from its own success.

Nymex Aluminum Contract Buffeted by Mergers
Commodities
Nymex Aluminum Contract Buffeted by Mergers
By Peter A. McKay
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
994 words
23 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
The New York Mercantile Exchange has struggled in its attempt to get back into the aluminum-futures
business.
Now comes news that five major aluminum producers may soon merge into two, which on its face should hurt
Nymex's effort even more by reducing the number of potential customers for the futures product. But market
participants say there are already so few investors in the new aluminum contract on Nymex's Comex division
that removing a few more potential ones will make a negligible difference.
In fact, the exchange is hoping just the opposite -- that a more-focused competition between two major
players will highlight the need for an aluminum market outside Europe, home of the dominant London Metal
Exchange.
Either way, Comex traders expressed frustration last week over their market's latest attempt to drum up
aluminum business, saying the new contract's survival may ultimately rely on a broader range of metal users,
such as car makers, airlines and soft-drink bottlers, in addition to producers.
"It certainly hasn't been as popular as we would have liked," Nymex Chairman Daniel Rappaport said of the
new aluminum contract, which began trading May 14. "But we actually think there'll be more of an opportunity
for business now. There'll be more of a need for an aluminum market in this time zone."
Open interest, or the number of outstanding contract commitments to buy or sell aluminum, has leveled off
slightly this month at Comex, slipping below 2,000 for much of August after what had been a steady period of
growth since the contract's launch. The volume of contracts changing hands has been in the single- or
double-digits almost half of the trading days this month.
The slippage at Nymex has coincided with the proposed combination of American aluminum giants Reynolds
Metals Co. and Alcoa Inc. In addition, Alcan Aluminium Ltd. of Canada, Pechiney SA of France and Alusuisse
Lonza Group AG of Switzerland are proposing a union that would create the world's largest aluminum
producer.
Such consolidation in other industries has stifled new commodities contracts in the past or led to less liquidity
in existing markets by reducing the number of buyers and sellers. Typically, both users and producers of a
commodity will use futures markets to protect themselves against price fluctuations.
But at Comex, the new aluminum merger mania may not have that effect, since many market participants
doubt the producers involved in the deals use the New York contract anyway.
Also, the mergers wouldn't eliminate the companies' need to hedge, just the way they do it, said aluminum
analyst Stewart R. Spector, who publishes a newsletter, Spector Report.
"There could be a modest decline in dealing with the LME or Comex, but what the mergers would really be
more likely to affect is the amount of business the producers do with traders," he said. "They would just swap
materials internally instead."
Right now, the LME is by far the more dominant outlet for aluminum hedgers. Comex investors are currently
committed to delivering 8,804 metric tons; the LME contracts cover 790,625 metric tons.
This isn't the first time that the New York exchange has tried to get a chunk of LME's aluminum business.
Comex offered an aluminum contract from December 1983 to July 1993, although it ultimately failed to draw
business because its delivery and supply terms were too similar to the LME futures, said Nymex
spokeswoman Nachamah Jacobovits.

--- Page 214 ---
Page 214 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"The only similarity between the two is the word aluminum," agreed one veteran base-metals trader, who
blamed the latest contract's slow start on poor marketing instead.
Another trader said the Comex contract's "only hope" lies in either soaring aluminum prices or more
enthusiasm from big producers to use it as a hedging tool.
"Right now, they're obviously either taking a wait-and-see approach, or they don't care at all," he said.
"Otherwise, it would have more volume."
In other commodities markets:
HOGS: Good demand for pork and positioning ahead of Friday's Agriculture Department cold-storage report
sent hog futures soaring at the Chicago Mercantile Exchange, market watchers said. The nearby October hog
contract closed 1.45 cents higher Friday at 44.65 cents a pound. The December contract closed 0.72 cent
higher at 42 cents a pound. The Agriculture Department said the stocks of frozen bellies on hand was up
122% from the same time last year and represented a record amount of bellies in cold storage for July.
COPPER: Copper futures closed lower in a choppy, up-and-down session on the Comex, after news of a
merger proposal among major copper producers. The nearby September contract fell 0.7 cent to close at
74.05 cents a pound. Traders said copper futures first rose as news spread that Phelps Dodge Corp. offered
to acquire both Asarco Inc. and Cyprus Amax Minerals Co., then fell when Asarco and Cyprus rejected
Phelps Dodge's offer. "The market came off just as fast as it went up," said a New York copper trader. After
the market closed, Phelps Dodge had sweetened its offer to a share swap valued at about $2.64 billion.
GOLD: Gold sputtered into the weekend, as late dealer selling and liquidation dragged Nymex gold futures
lower, traders said. The benchmark December contract fell $1.60 to close at $259.60 a troy ounce. Traders
said there were indications of hedge-selling by producers, as well as selling by commodity funds as they
adjusted their ratio of gold to silver. Comex September silver rose 3.5 cents to $5.255 an ounce. Traders said
the gold market looks as if it is on hold, waiting to see if U.S. interest rates rise this week.

Dollar Declines Against Yen and EuroOn Wider-Than-Expected Trade Gap
Foreign Exchange
Dollar Declines Against Yen and Euro On Wider-Than-Expected Trade Gap
By Michael Casey
Dow Jones Newswires
693 words
20 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C17
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- The dollar was dealt another heavy blow when an unexpected widening in the U.S. trade
deficit for June sent the dollar tumbling against most major currencies.
With consensus forecasts earlier predicting a narrower deficit of $20.8 billion, the announcement at 8:30 a.m.
EDT that the monthly trade gap had ballooned to a record $24.6 billion from a revised $21.17 billion in May hit
the U.S. currency hard in the New York session.
"People were positioned for a little bit better number, so when they didn't get it, boom," said Ralph DelZenero,
vice president, foreign exchange at First Chicago.
European currencies were the biggest beneficiaries, with the euro jumping two cents on the news before
steadying comfortably above the $1.06 level for the remainder of the trading day. The Swiss franc and British
pound charted similar surges.
The dollar initially nosedived against the yen also, briefly hitting a low of $110.68, its lowest level since Jan.
12, before swiftly reversing course to move back above 111 yen.
On Jan. 12 the Bank of Japan intervened to weaken the Japanese currency. Fears of the same happening
again -- perhaps this time with U.S. assistance -- were cited as a reason for traders' reluctance to take the
dollar even lower yesterday.
Late yesterday in New York, the dollar was trading at 111.63 yen, down from 111.91 yen late Wednesday.
The euro was trading at $1.0638, up from $1.0522. The euro was also at 118.75 yen, up from 117.75 yen.
Sterling was at $1.6175, up from $1.6027.
The only major currencies that actually weakened against the dollar yesterday were the Australian and
Canadian dollars, which were hurt by a mixture of a technical factors, a declining gold price and concerns
about widening interest-rate differentials with the U.S.
A wider U.S. trade deficit is a problem for the dollar because it also implies a widening in the current-account
deficit, which must be financed with inflows of capital from overseas. Either U.S. interest rates need to go
higher to attract those funds or the dollar must weaken to boost export competitiveness and improve the trade
balance.
The euro also was helped by German economic data in the European session. The Ifo West German
Business sentiment index rose for the third consecutive month, to 93.6, in July. Because these data helped
reinforce beliefs that Europe's largest economy is in recovery, they gave the euro a quick burst of buying.
Even so, the European Central Bank isn't expected to raise European interest rates before the Federal
Reserve tightens U.S. monetary policy, which is widely expected at a meeting of the Federal Open Market
Committee next week. The robust import segment of yesterday's trade report further underscores the strength
of the U.S. economy and the risk of overheating that the Fed is keen to contain.
Michael Rosenberg, head of currency research at Deutsche Bank in New York, said an official rate increase
should lead to a widening in rate differentials between the U.S. and Europe that would "in the long run," boost
the dollar.
Recently, however, "the failure of [market] rates to move meaningfully higher in the U.S. has been a major
drag on the dollar since July," he argued.

--- Page 216 ---
Page 216 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
This sort of fundamental reasoning is less applicable to the yen's recent performance, Mr. Rosenberg and
others say. The Japanese currency's movements are being driven by capital flows, with a surge in Japanese
stocks over recent days the defining factor.
In this environment, market participants seemed to have reached their yen limits yesterday, having built up
unsustainable short positions in the dollar and the euro. The yen's brief rally against the dollar early yesterday
thus offered some traders a chance to buy cheap dollars. As yen were then sold in the brief dollar
short-squeeze that followed, the Japanese currency fell even further against its European counterparts.
