`Carry Trades' Could Be Problem for Gold Market
Commodities
`Carry Trades' Could Be Problem for Gold Market
Michael Casey and Janet Whitman
Dow Jones Newswires
917 words
19 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C19
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
Gold's relentless price slide in the past three years has spurred more investors to undertake what are known
as gold "carry trades" -- essentially, low-interest loans using borrowed gold.
But the trades could be a problem for the gold market if investors suddenly have to unwind them, as
investors did with the popular carry trades involving the Japanese yen in the fall.
The gold carry trades involve borrowing gold from banks at prevailing low interest rates, selling or using it,
then using the proceeds to buy higher-yielding assets such as Treasurys. The low borrowing rates, combined
with weak gold prices, make repaying the loans inexpensive, even for manufacturers who must replace
borrowed metal they have turned into finished goods.
But if the gold price spikes and borrowers are forced to cover their short positions, some say the effect could
resemble the turmoil caused by the reversal in the yen carry trade, when the dollar nose dived and investors
were forced to get out of high-risk assets. Treasurys, already under pressure from concerns over U.S.
inflation risks, could be among those markets to suffer in the fallout.
Limited disclosure means no one can ever really know the full extent of the gold carry trade. But more people
in the market are talking about it, and London-based Gold Fields Mineral Services Ltd. this year made its first
reference to the carry trade in its closely watched annual survey of the gold market.
"For many years a significant quantity of gold has been borrowed as a source of cheap finance," Gold Fields
said in its report. "The arbitrage has in fact been remarkably similar to (though far smaller than) the famous
'yen carry trade.' "
Gold Fields managing director Philip Klapwijk later told analysts he estimated the size of those positions to
be 400 metric tons -- worth about $3 billion at current rates.
At this stage, there isn't a lot of knowledge about the gold carry trade outside the gold market. That could
suggest Wall Street's risk-hungry clients aren't as enamored with the idea as they once were with the
equivalent yen strategy.
In that sense, it presents less of a systemic risk. Conservative policies adopted by hedge funds and other
institutions after the fall crisis may have had a constraining effect.
There is no question, however, that the gold carry trade has been an attractive deal for some market
participants.
From the time gold peaked at around $417.50 a troy ounce in February 1996 until just recently, gold leasing
rates rarely rose above 1%, while long-term Treasury yields have been either side of 6%. That is a healthy
spread. However, lease rates, which reflect the cost of borrowing gold, shot above 4% last week, with some
market observers speculating that year-2000 fears and liquidity concerns were behind the jump in rates.
Traders said those factors also helped drive the price of front-month gold futures down $1.80 to $260.30 per
ounce on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange yesterday.
"The locals and the speculators are selling, essentially saying, 'Gee, let's try this market again another time,' "
said George Gero, first vice president of Prudential Securities in New York. "The market is essentially down to
the funds and trade houses now."
For those investors, who reportedly are also among the heaviest gold borrowers, a continued increase in
lease rates could portend a sudden and damaging short-covering rally in the metal. Such a scramble would

--- Page 218 ---
Page 218 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
be of huge benefit to the price of gold, although it would be unlikely to move other markets significantly, said
Frank Veneroso, a gold expert at Veneroso Associates and author of the Gold Book Annual.
"I don't think [the gold carry trade] is a big deal," he said. "I think that sometime down the road, it will get
unwound. But I've never believed it's been large enough to create any systemic risk."
In other commodity trading:
COPPER: Prices fell on the Comex on the prospect of an end to strikes at a Canadian mine and at Chilean
ports. The September Comex copper contract declined 2.5 cents to 74.55 cents a pound. Highland Valley
Copper was due to present new proposals to workers at its mine in British Columbia today, union officials
said. The mine, which produces about 170,000 metric tons of copper a year, has been closed since May 15
due to the failure of management and workers to forge a cost-cutting agreement. Also, a Chilean radio station
reported yesterday that striking dockworkers were set to sign an accord with the government to end a walkout
that has crippled the nation's three largest ports. Exports of copper from Chile -- the world's largest producer
-- have been held up by those strikes and by another unrelated strike at the port of Iquique.
LUMBER: Futures fell on the Chicago Mercantile Exchange due to a weak cash market and technical, or
chart-related, factors, analysts and traders said. The September contract slid its $10 limit to $354.30 a
thousand board feet. "The cash market has just died," said Roger Ovens, account executive at Refco Futures
Ltd. in Vancouver. He added that selling was accelerated by a fall through $357, where there were sell-stops,
or preplaced sell orders.

Peter A. McKay contributed to this article.

Powerful Earthquake Jolts Northwest Turkey,Killing Thousands and Devastating Urban Areas
International
Powerful Earthquake Jolts Northwest Turkey, Killing Thousands and Devastating Urban Areas
By Hugh Pope
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
839 words
18 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
ISTANBUL, Turkey -- A powerful 45-second earthquake cut a swath through Turkey's industrial heartland
yesterday, killing thousands of people, shutting businesses, wrecking major power lines and jolting national
confidence just as the economy showed signs of leaving this year's recession behind.
The temblor surged from deep under the Marmara Sea at 3:02 a.m. local time. Turkish seismologists said the
earthquake measured 6.7 on the Richter scale, and U.S. scientists in Golden, Colo., say it may have peaked
as high as 7.8. In Istanbul, Turkey's biggest city and financial center 50 miles west of the epicenter, the quake
brought down buildings, triggered a cacophonic downtown chorus of car and shop alarms and sent many in
the population of 10 million out onto the streets.
Among those shaken awake was U.S. Energy Secretary Bill Richardson, in Turkey for talks on Caspian oil
and natural-gas pipeline projects.
"We were shaking," he said later in his 12th-floor hotel suite. "I could see lights going out, people running in
the streets. The light was going out, the power was going out," he said. "I can tell you having experienced an
earthquake before, in Mexico: This was a very strong earthquake."
Because telephone lines and power went down over much of northwestern Turkey, the rest of the nation only
gradually learned about the nightmare that unfolded along the seismic fault line from Istanbul to Ankara 250
miles to the east. The semiofficial Anatolian news agency late yesterday put the death toll at 2,011, and said
10,764 were injured. But those figures could climb.
No Americans or other foreigners in Turkey were immediately reported hurt. Istanbul's Ottoman and
Byzantine monuments appeared undamaged. The Turkish straits remained open throughout.
Closer to the epicenter, people were thrown out of their beds by the force of the quake. Panic seized the
worst-hit urban areas of Izmit, Golcuk and Yalova. People broke ankles and legs leaping from windows and
balconies. Hundreds, perhaps thousands, of poorly constructed concrete apartment buildings crumpled into
pancake piles of rubble. Survivors huddled in the blacked-out streets, huddling fearfully during strong hourly
aftershocks.
Medical help was minimal. One regional hospital had collapsed. Another had just one working operating
theater, and the chief doctor said most surgical implements had been shaken out of their antiseptic
containers. As night turned into a hot, sunny day in the devastated region, patients filled up parking lots,
sidewalks and roads outside hospitals. Angry relatives ransacked pharmacies for desperately needed
supplies.
"My house collapsed on me," said 40-year-old salesman Ozhan Zeyrek, his skin coated with gritty concrete
dust. "They dug me out after eight hours. I thought I had died and come back to life." He was limping down a
street, naked except for a curtain hitched round his waist like a skirt. He held out a swollen right forearm with
a grisly open wound. "Take me to a hospital. But not the one back there . . . All they could do was give me a
couple of injections."
In Istanbul, gold, bond, and stock markets closed when the Central Bank fund transfer computer went down.
The markets are expected to reopen for business today.
Rescue efforts were hampered by roads blocked in one direction by fleeing survivors, and in the other by
relatives searching for family members. And a damaged bridge cut the main Trans-Europe Motorway.

--- Page 220 ---
Page 220 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Turkey also lacks sophisticated rescue services. The U.S., Germany, Britain, Israel, Switzerland, Japan and
even Turkey's rival Greece prepared and sent trained teams to look for survivors. The Turks had little but
pickaxes, heavy construction equipment and their bare hands.
Lack of electric power, due to heavy damage to transmission lines and transformer stations, was a major
problem. Factories all shut, as did many other businesses in northwestern Turkey. The mayor of Izmit begged
on live television for foreign help to extinguish fires in the Tupras refinery, which he said was abandoned at
nightfall as the fires spread through naphtha tanks. The complex accounts for a third of Turkey's refining
capacity.
The shock to the area, mostly Kocaeli province around Izmit, will jolt Turkey at a delicate time for its
economy. The province generates 10% of Turkey's industrial production. Many foreign joint-venture factories
and car plants have chosen sites there. Its automotive industry helped production figures improve after the
economy shrank 8.4% in the first quarter of 1999.
Still, bankers believe the personal tragedies of the earthquake won't be matched by an economic meltdown.
"Our preliminary assessment is that the economic impact would be mildly negative on industrial production
and on the fiscal situation, but should not affect the chances of Turkey getting International Monetary Fund
funding in the fourth quarter of this year, or Turkey's progress toward stabilization," said a report from Laura
Papi of Deutsche Bank.

Cyclical and Growth Stocks Rally in Europe;Asian Markets, Led by Malaysia, Bounce Back
World Stock Markets
Cyclical and Growth Stocks Rally in Europe; Asian Markets, Led by Malaysia, Bounce Back
By Craig Karmin
Staff Reporter of The Wall Street Journal
1,167 words
13 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
European shares enjoyed a broad rally that encompassed both cyclical and growth stocks, an odd
phenomenon that investors quipped was nearly as rare as the solar eclipse spotted this week across the
Continent.
Markets in Germany, France and the U.K. posted gains of more than 2%, as a few surprisingly strong
earnings reports and falling European bond yields lifted stocks in the financial and technology sectors. Oil,
metals and mining shares also rose.
Asian markets, meanwhile, bounced back strongly on the heels of Wall Street's rally Wednesday. Malaysian
stocks, up 5.9%, were the biggest winners after the country was reinstated in a popular global index. Overall,
the Dow Jones World Stock Index rose 1.57, or 0.74%, to 213.73. Excluding the U.S., the index rose 2.81, or
1.81%, to 157.75.
European investors said they were pleasantly surprised to see both cyclical stocks and growth stocks, whose
prices tend to move in the opposite direction, chalk up strong gains. The Dow Jones Stoxx Index of 592
European companies gained 2%.
"The market seems to be of two minds," said Bill Smyth, European equity strategist for Henderson Investors
in London. "Cyclicals on the whole are performing well, but some of the growth-stock prices are at least
showing signs of flattening out, if not rising."
The dual nature, he added, reflects the presumed shift in world economic growth as Europe appears to be
gradually gaining strength and the U.S. shows early signs of cooling off. The question is: Will the U.S.
economy still be up sharply by the time Europe has clearly rebounded?
Those who foresee strong global economic expansion as the U.S. and Europe both advance are loading up
on cyclical stocks such as metals and commodities, which was apparent yesterday. The basic resources
sector of the Dow Jones Stoxx Index was the best performer, up 4.2%.
"People are still not overweight on cyclicals," said David Bowers, European equity strategist for Merrill Lynch
in London. "They've only come up to a normal weighting. I see more buying to come as bond yields go
higher."
Most notably, British mining group Billiton soared 13% to 302.75 pence on speculation that Anglo American
Gold may be preparing a takeover bid. Anglo American climbed 173 pence to 37.71.
France's Pechiney also rose 8.6% to 58.50 euros, one day after the announcement of its three-way merger
plan with Canada's Alcan and Switzerland's Algroup.
At the same time, fund managers who believe the U.S. economy is slowing and that interest rates won't
have to climb much higher are picking up banks, financials and technology stocks. The
second-best-performing sector of the Stoxx Index yesterday was technology, which rose 2.9%. Shares of
Getronics, a Dutch technology services company, were buoyed 8.5% to 41.50 euros after reporting a 57%
rise in first-half profit.
Insurance companies also were standouts, aided by falling bond yields, especially the return of Germany's
10-year government bond yield to below 5%, a psychologically important level. These companies are heavily
invested in bonds, so their net assets rise and cost of capital falls when bond yields decline.

--- Page 222 ---
Page 222 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Shares of Dutch insurance group Aegon jumped 3% to 79.3 euros. The company reported
better-than-expected first-half profit of 679 million euros and announced it will buy the U.K. life-insurance
operation of Guardian Royal Exchange from Sun Life & Provincial Holdings.
Still, some investors don't expect such a broadly based rally in European stocks to reappear too frequently. "I
don't think it's sustainable for both growth and cyclical shares to continue upward," said James Clunie,
portfolio manager for Murray Johnstone International in Glasgow.
In other markets:
In TOKYO, the Nikkei 225 Index rose 211.81 points, or 1.2%, to 17422.97, gaining for the fourth consecutive
day. Honda Motor gained 170 yen to 4,980 yen after the auto maker reported better-than-expected fiscal
first-quarter results. A rally in U.S. technology shares Wednesday also boosted Japanese electronics
companies. Sony jumped 260 yen to 14,390 yen, while Fujitsu shares gained 80 yen to 3,230 yen.
In HONG KONG, the Hang Seng Index rebounded to close up 341.95, or 2.7%, at 12779.75, shaking off five
straight days of losses. Swire Pacific leapt 5% to 35.60 Hong Kong dollars after reporting its mid-year results.
The group posted a 9.4% decline in net profit to HK$1.82 billion, but analysts say investors are now betting
on a strong second half for the conglomerate. Cathay Pacific rose 4.1% to HK$11.55 after it reported
better-than-expected earnings late Wednesday.
China-related shares also rose sharply as positive China export figures for July, released Wednesday, eased
concerns about a possible currency devaluation. Citic Pacific, which has been under strong pressure in the
past few weeks, shot up 8% to HK$20.15.
In KUALA LUMPUR, the Composite Index soared 40.46, or 5.9%, to 728.96. Stocks gained on word that
Morgan Stanley Capital International was about to reinstate the country in its Emerging Markets Free Index.
After the market's close, the firm confirmed Malaysia is slated to rejoin the index starting in February.
Separately, senior financial executives close to the government said Kuala Lumpur is expected to reduce a
10% exit tax on profit from portfolio investments that were made after Feb. 15. A 10% levy on principal
investments is already scheduled to end Sept. 1. The market's biggest winner yesterday was casino operator
Resorts World, which gained 23% to 1.60 ringgit.
In SAO PAULO, the Bovespa Index fell 91, or 0.9%, to 10,045. Preferred shares of local aircraft manufacturer
Embraer jumped 9.9% to 5.43 real on talk the company could soon sell a minority stake and carry out an
American depositary receipt issue by October. Officials have denied that either move is imminent.

Zonagen, Gerber Childrenswear, Razorfish Fall;Russell 2000, Nasdaq Stocks Hurt by Tech Sector
Small Stock Focus
Zonagen, Gerber Childrenswear, Razorfish Fall; Russell 2000, Nasdaq Stocks Hurt by Tech Sector
By Larry Bauman
Dow Jones Newswires
1,070 words
11 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C7
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
NEW YORK -- Small-capitalization stocks tumbled in active trading, although the group's losses, as
measured by the Russell 2000 index, were slimmer than those of the broader stock market.
The overall Nasdaq market was solidly lower, but both the small-cap and Nasdaq markets managed to pare
their intraday losses with the help of a late-day burst of buying activity.
As has been the case for the past few weeks, the small-cap and Nasdaq markets were weighed down by the
weakness of technology stocks, although Internet stocks rebounded.
The Russell 2000 index of small-capitalization stocks fell 3.07, or 0.72%, to 422.82, and the Nasdaq
Composite Index, at 2490.11, tumbled 28.87, or 1.15%.
Zonagen, a Woodlands, Texas, biopharmaceutical concern, plummeted 2 3/8, or 32.5%, to a 52-week low of
4 15/16. The Food and Drug Administration put on hold all clinical trials of the active ingredient in Zonagen's
erectile-dysfunction therapy, Vasomax. The trials were put on hold until the company can evaluate whether
abnormalities found in a two-year study of rats could occur in humans. If approved, Vasomax would compete
with Pfizer's blockbuster impotence drug, Viagra. Zonagen said it can provide sufficient information to the
FDA to remove the clinical hold.
Gerber Childrenswear (NYSE) dove 2 7/16, or 32.5%, to a 52-week low of 5 1/16. The Greenville, S.C.,
manufacturer of infant and toddler clothing and related products late Monday posted second-quarter earnings
that fell significantly shy of analysts' projections. Yesterday, Merrill Lynch lowered its near-term rating on the
stock to neutral from accumulate and cut its longterm rating to accumulate from buy.
Paging Network tumbled 13/16, or 19.1%, to 3 7/16. The Dallas wireless-messaging and information-services
provider reported a second-quarter loss of 92 cents a share, compared with analysts' projections of a 38-cent
loss. The company also said third-quarter results will be hurt by conversions and systems reconciliation, as
well as by about 50,000 cancellations from a national account relationship and about 70,000 cancellations
from the bankruptcy of a reseller.
Razorfish slid 3 1/4, or 11.5%, to a 52-week low of 25 following news the New York provider of digital
communications services agreed to acquire i-Cube for $677 million in stock. Razorfish said it expects the
acquisition will add to its earnings in fiscal 1999 and fiscal 2000.
Union Bankshares surged 4 5/8, or 37%, to a 52-week high of 17 1/8 after the Denver bank-holding company
agreed to be acquired by Gold Banc for $23.05 a share in stock.
Biocryst Pharmaceuticals leapt 3 15/16, or 26.4%, to a 52-week high of 18 7/8. Salomon Smith Barney raised
its price target on the Birmingham, Ala., developer of small-molecule pharmaceuticals to $25 from $12.50.
The firm based its move on the favorable outlook for Biocryst's flu treatment, which is being developed by
Johnson & Johnson.
Copley Pharmaceutical jumped 1 15/16, or 21.8%, to 10 13/16 after the Canton, Mass., maker of prescription
and over-the -- counter drugs agreed to be acquired by Israeli drug maker Teva Pharmaceutical Industries for
$11 a share.
New York Stock Exchange-traded Medicis Pharmaceutical climbed 3 1/8, or 13.6%, to 26 1/16. Hambrecht &
Quist added the Phoenix marketer of skin products and prescription skin lotions to its focus list.

--- Page 224 ---
Page 224 of 225 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
NYSE-traded Lodgian gained 7/16, or 9.6%, to 5 after the West Palm Beach, Fla., hotel operator reported
second-quarter earnings of 32 cents a share, compared with its 29-cent year-ago profit. The results were in
line with analysts' projections.
Remedy added 1 13/16, or 9.2%, to 21 7/16. Banc of America Securities raised its rating on the
Mountainview, Calif., software concern to strong buy from buy.
NYSE-traded Comstock Resources was up 3/8, or 7.1%, to 5 5/8. The Dallas energy company posted a
second-quarter loss that was narrower than analysts had been expecting.
Asahi/America, a Malden, Mass., maker of thermoplastic valves, pipe, piping systems and components,
rallied for a gain of 1/2, or 6.3%, to 8 3/8 after agreeing to be acquired by Midnight Acquisition Holdings, a
unit of Asahi Organic Chemicals Industry, for about $9.50 a share.
On the Nasdaq, declining issues led advancers, 2,611 to 1,317, on national market volume of 946 million
shares and overall volume of 978 million, compared with 717 million and 748 million, respectively, Monday.
Several times during the selloff of the past few weeks, there were signs that the retreat had run its course,
such as yesterday's late-day rebound.
But so far, those signals have been misleading.
"At best, we're only two-thirds of the way through this drop," said Greg Kuhn of Kuhn Asset Management. Mr.
Kuhn expects the Nasdaq composite will fall more than 20% from its July 16 record close before it hits
bottom.
A key reason Mr. Kuhn sees further declines is his perception that the market is unusually illiquid. He said
short interest is very low compared with the levels of the past two years, and the level of cash in mutual funds
is at one of the lowest points since the 1970s.
"There are no bids," he said, largely because "the money's been spent."
Mr. Kuhn said the money that came out of the stock market during last summer's selloff has already been
re-invested, and that with the Federal Reserve having reversed course by raising interest rates, with another
rate increase widely anticipated, that money "is leaving again."
While Mr. Kuhn expects the stock market will continue to move lower, he said the relative performance of
small-cap stocks compared with large caps "will be the flip of last summer," when small-cap stocks posted
much sharper declines than the overall Nasdaq market and the other major indexes.
As the overall stock market moves lower, "we'll see large stocks fall more than small caps," he said. "Small
caps won't do as poorly on the down side."

IMF Rethinks PlansTo Sell Gold ReservesTo Help Relieve Debt
IMF Rethinks Plans To Sell Gold Reserves To Help Relieve Debt
289 words
10 August 1999
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
(Copyright (c) 1999, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)
PRETORIA, South Africa (AP) -- The International Monetary Fund is reconsidering plans to sell some of its
gold reserves to help finance a Western debt-relief plan for some of the world's most debt-burdened
countries, a top IMF official said yesterday.
Gold prices have plunged to 20-year lows on the prospect of a global gold sell-off, which was hastened by
the Bank of England's auction of 3.5% of its reserves last month.
Gold's loss of luster has triggered bankruptcies, layoffs and street protests in South Africa, the world's
biggest producer of gold. And tens of thousands of miners there stand to lose their jobs if gold prices
continue sliding.
"We are trying very hard to see if there is an alternative to selling gold," said Stanley Fischer, the IMF's first
deputy managing director, who was visiting South Africa for the inauguration of Tito Mboweni, the country's
first black central-bank governor.
Mr. Fischer's remarks, reported by Dow Jones Newswires, were the first official comment from the IMF since
criticism of the proposed sale began mounting three months ago.
Opposition by South Africa and by other gold-producing countries to the IMF plan to sell as many as 10
million ounces of its 100 million-ounce reserves has "caused us to look for another solution," Mr. Fischer said.
South Africa has one of the highest average costs of production, at $246 an ounce. Yesterday, gold for
current delivery closed at $257.50 a troy ounce on the New York Mercantile Exchange, a gain of 40 cents.
Gold peaked at $875 a troy ounce in New York in January 1980.
